# Buried City, Unearthing Teufelsberg

## S. 2

Buried City, Unearthing Teufelsberg
Cities are built over the remnants of their past buried beneath their present. We build
on what has been built before, whether over foundations formalising previous
permanency or over the temporal occupations of ground. But what happens when
you shift a city – when you dislodge its occupation of ground towards a new ground,
bury it and forget it?
Focusing on Berlin’s destruction during World War II and its reconstruction after
the end of the war, this book offers a rethinking of how the practices of destruction
and burial combine to reform the city through geography and how burying a city is
intricately tied to forgetting destruction, ruination and trauma. Created from 25
million cubic metres of rubble produced during World War II, Teufelsberg (Devil’s
Mountain) is the exemplar of the destroyed city. Its critical journey is chronicled in
combination with Berlin’s seven other rubble hills, and their connections to
constructing forgetting through burial. Furthermore, the book investigates Berlin’s
sublime relation to Albert Speer’s urban vision to rival the ancient cities of Rome
and Athens through their now shared geographies of seven hills. Finally, there is a
central focus on the role of the citizens who cleared Berlin’s streets of rubble, and
the subsequent human relationships between people and ruins.
This book is valuable reading for those interested in Architectural Theory, Urban
Geography, Modern History and Urban Design.
Benedict Anderson practices in design, architectural theory and public art. He has
worked in many different universities, lectured extensively as an invited speaker and
exhibited in major exhibitions. He previously held the position as Professor of Spatial
Design, University of Technology Sydney.

## S. 4

Teufelsberg
Berlin and its Geography of
Forgetting
Benedict Anderson

## S. 12

Preface
The view
I ﬁrst became interested in the idea of the buried city through walking a hill. I had
come to Teufelsberg (Devil’s Mountain) to ﬁlm what I could not see. Holding my
heavy 16 mm Bolex camera, I clambered up one of the hills while ﬁlming, tripped
and fell. What I had tripped over – protruding bricks and fragments of concrete and
steel – was what I was attempting to ﬁlm: the visible remnants of buildings buried
more than 70 years ago. I imagined my ﬁlm being a type of X-ray, sending electro -
magnetic waves through the hill’s surface to reveal what lied buried beneath.
Teufelsberg consists of approximately 16,000 buildings destroyed in the Allied Air
War on Berlin (1942–1945) during World War II. Built over the humic foundations
of a buried city, it is a landscape designed to be forgotten. On the one hand, Teufelsberg
marks the ground for a collective consciousness and, on the other, a ground for
forgetting. Even though what lies under the surface is invisible to the naked eye,
Teufelsberg combines city, ruination and geography in a way that connects to human
practices of burial.
Built between 1946 and 1971 and rising to 115 metres, Teufelsberg transformed
Berlin’s destruction into a swollen landscape, ﬁlled with the physical remnants of its
past urbanity. To appreciate this vast hill of rubble is to view it in terms of
archaeology, geography and cartography. The Greek roots of the word geography
tell us that geo is earth and graphien is to write. Reading geography requires us to
comprehend the writing of the ground, whereas archaeology unearths the ground in
order to recompose the histories of the land. Topography, by contrast, records and
measures the ground, allowing cartography to write the terrain. Buried City is a
working through of the connections between city and human burial through ground,
landscape and geography. Questions arise as to what these connections between
human and city burial say about our relationship to the humus we share with ground.
As my interest in Teufelsberg grew, I started collecting photographs of Berlin’s
destruction and Teufelsberg’s construction from the Landesarchiv (City Archive). They
show women and men working in the ruins of their city, clearing and carting rubble
from bombed-out buildings and burying it at Teufelsberg. The portrayal of post-war
Berlin in each photograph can be viewed as a type of phantasmagoria of destruction;
an inverse spectacle of the city in ruins. Viewing the photographs, I was aware of
what Roland Barthes describes as their punctum – the personalised pricking of the
viewer in their viewing. The graphic images of the Vietnam War by the photographer
Donald McCullin, discussed by John Berger in Ways of Seeing, depict the horrors of
that war, the moment of agony, pain and terror of its victims. By contrast, the archive
photographs of Berlin are images after war, pain and terror. They show a people
working through ruins, tasked with the reconstruction of their city.

## S. 13

Buried City can be read as a seismic measuring of how a people came to bury
their city and asks what can be exhumed (materially, structurally, philosophically)
by its unearthing. The aim is to dig into its past to redeﬁne its symbolic, cultural and
political presence. Cities are still being destroyed today; the destruction of Aleppo
and Gaza City is a continuum of war’s focus on the destruction of cities and civilians.
When the war ends, what will these people do with their destroyed city?
Note
Throughout the text, Teufelsberg and buried city are used interchangeably. Teufelsberg is used
as the entity of the buried city and the geography of the site. Buried city is used as its material
contents, the remnants of a former Berlin.
xii
Preface
Figure 1 Landscape – View of ground, Teufelsberg, Winter 2014
Photo: Author

## S. 14

Acknowledgements
My interest in the buried city is through archaeology, architecture, geography and
landscape as a way to formulate a material existence between city and burial. Like
millions of other people I have walked through cities that were buried but are now
unearthed, such as Herculaneum and Pompeii, and I have walked over burial sites
in the cities of Rome, Naples, Athens, Istanbul and Amman, to name a few. For this
book I walked on top of a hill under which lies a buried city. In writing, I have tried
to avoid any delusional designs of impressing the reader. As such, the book is designed
as a geographical walk over the buried city in order to understand it through cultural,
physical and natural forgetting that can lead to its unearthing and remembering.
Most of the book was written while on sabbatical from the University of
Technology Sydney in 2015. This time away and, more importantly, returning to my
home in Berlin, situated the writing of the book ‘in location’. The book has a history
that began with several journal articles and book chapters such as Trümmer
Geographies: Teufelsberg as a site for forgetting in On Ruins and Ruination
Performance Research (2015) edited by Richard Gough, Carl Lavery and proofed by
Sandra Laureri; Self-ruining and situated vagrancy: the geography of performance in
Occupation: Ruin, Repudiation and Revolution (2015) edited by Lynne Churchill,
Diane Smith and proofed by Christine Houen; The architectural ﬂaw in The Practice
of Spatial Thinking (2014) edited by Leon van Schaik; and, way before that, a few
pages in my PhD. Early conversations with Charles Anderson and Paul Carter sharing
their interest in the rubble hill of Teufelsberg were important in the thinking and
framing of the book.
There are people who I would like to thank in helping me get to the ﬁnal
completion, who put up with my complex ‘all in one sentence ideas’, problematic
structuring, my twists and turns in making sense of what I was writing, the arguments
I was making and making clear my ideas. Editor Nick Grindell was, I suspect, very
patient, more than he cared to show, and declared to me how he understood his role
that was as much as unpacking my thoughts as dealing with my sentence structure
and grammar. Researcher Tim Tetzner, whose research and guidance unearthed a
myriad of references and connections for me to draw upon. Sharing ideas with
Russell Walsh and consolidating earlier thoughts with Olivia Barr helped in framing
my writing. My sister Laura Anderson, who read through earlier manuscripts and
supported me with enthusiasm, and Tran Tuan Anh Dang, whose aesthetic pictorial
image making helped me to visualise my ideas in unearthing the buried city. Finally,
I am indebted to Bronwyn Clark-Coolee, who saw the progression and value of my
ideas and who was able to ground my thoughts within a wider context.

## S. 15

Libraries also played a big part in securing information: the Danish Archeological
Institute in Athens, the Staatsbibliothek in Berlin, the library at the Palazzo Farnese
of the French Embassy in Rome, to name but a few. A big thank you to the
Fotosammlung (photograph collections) of the Landesarchiv Berlin for their generous
support in allowing me to publish a substantial quantity of their photographic archive
without which the book would be a lot poorer, and especially to Monika Bartzsch,
who tabled and veriﬁed the crediting of the photographers. I would like to thank the
support that I have received from Ashgate and Routledge publishers in seeing the
value of what the book was aiming to achieve, in ﬁnalising the manuscript and
especially Richard Skipper in helping in the tedious task of acquiring permissions
from various rights holders and to Richard Sanders and Aoife McGrath for their
editorial assistance, prooﬁng and layout of the book.
The slow manifestation that led to the conception of the book, and the framing
of my ideas in combining the destruction and burial of cities with human burial, will
I suspect not end at its conclusion. The destruction of cities continues unabated today.
Questions as to why this archaic form of warfare on cities from despotic leaders,
right-wing political zealots and the delusional and single-minded beliefs of cultural
and religious supremacy, and to the women and children who become victims, remain
real and emblematic points of destruction even in the 21st century. The cities of Gaza,
Aleppo and Mosul and so many others share the same fate as Carthage, Thebes and
Babylon millennia before that were destroyed and erased from the face of the earth.
Gaza, Aleppo and Mosul will no doubt be reconstructed, but to what end? To again
be destroyed by the same manifestations that pursue their ruin? The book does not
seek answers to this dilemma but asks questions regarding the mass destruction of
cities and what to do with their remnants.
Benedict Anderson, 
28 November 2016
xiv
Acknowledgements

## S. 16

Introduction
Reﬂecting: above and below
In the early 1990s, my adoptive city of Berlin was engulfed by national, institutional
and commercial building programmes. Many urban sites in the former East Berlin
remained scarred by World War II and the subsequent partition of Germany. The
programme of urban renewal referred to as critical reconstruction, an approach
intended to achieve uniﬁed national identity with commercial interests through largescale architectural and urban planning schemes, created a city constantly shifting
between past, present and future. In my view, such reconstruction of destroyed sites
ensured the erasure of Berlin’s near total destruction during World War II, amounting
to ‘making history go’.1 What I was witnessing prompted me to investigate sites and
buildings where reconstruction and history appeared to be on a collision course,
aiming to develop alternative reconstruction practices in line with my focus on the
destruction of East German architecture, speciﬁcally the Palace of the Republic (Palast
der Republik) that was dismantled to make way for a reconstruction of Berlin’s Royal
Palace (Berliner Stadtschloss) that had previously stood on the same site before its
bombed ruins were blown up by the GDR in the early 1950s.2 At this, one of the
most politically contested sites in Berlin, the pull and push of history (war, partition,
reuniﬁcation) has placed ideology and architecture at the centre of Berlin’s late 20th
and early 21st-century identity. Architecture has always proﬁted from alliances with
political ideologies and is thus always ﬁrst to be targeted and destroyed in times of
both war and peace.
Other sites of interest included the tiled façades of the grand Soviet-inspired
buildings lining Karl-Marx-Allee (originally Stalin-Allee). This 2.5 km propaganda
symbol of post-war East German urban planning created the opportunity to build a
new political and cultural identity in opposition to the national socialism of the Nazi
regime and Western capitalism in West Germany. The façades featured embossed
tiles with scenes displaying socialist principles appropriate to their ideology. In an
interview, the architect Irene Henselmann, colleague and wife of the chief planner of
Stalin-Allee Herman Henselmann, likened the resurfacing of the façades to a pixelated
television screen, breaking the uniformity and, in her mind, erasing the socialist
aesthetic. In Dresden, my focus turned to the Church of our Lady (Frauenkirche).
Destroyed except for a section of the apse in the closing stages of WWII, the church
lay in ruins throughout the GDR years and became a place of political protest against
the regime. Uniﬁcation brought the opportunity to reconstruct this centrepiece of
Dresden’s Baroque architectural history. I was interested less in this reconstruction
than in the remnant building stones collected from the ruins, numbered and interned
in massive storage shelves adjoining the site. In my mind, the shelves and stones
symbolised a new type of religious architecture; one transformed by destruction.

## S. 17

My interest in destroyed urban environments later widened in scope to incorporate
not just architecture and urban design but the disciplines of geography, landscape
architecture and archaeology. The concept of the buried city began to form as
something real and tangible in Berlin’s vast rubble hill where approximately 16,000
buildings destroyed during the Air War on the city during WWII are buried.
Constructed over 25 years and located 17 kilometres from the city centre in the district
of Wilmersdorf, the rubble hill known as Teufelsberg (Devil’s Mountain) came to
symbolise city burial and, as I developed my thinking, human burial. On one level,
the task of removing the rubble of bombed-out buildings from the streets of Berlin
can be understood as a functional part of the reconstruction process. A more complex
understanding involves investigating how a people decided to bury their city. In the
aftermath of a war that had resulted in the murder and death of tens of millions of
people and the destruction of cities, an environment was created where confronting
the horrors of the Nazi Regime was in fact replaced with the tasks of reconstruction.
The destruction of cities continues to this day, and it seems likely to continue, as
it remains the most highly visible result of one army’s superiority over another. The
current civil war in Syria has left many cities reduced to ruins and tens of thousands
of civilians killed. When the conﬂict is over, what strategies will be planned to make
them liveable again? The scale of destruction of the Syrian cities of Aleppo and Homs,
and of Gaza City, presents a dilemma: What to reconstruct? What to bury? The
concept of the buried city may allow the destruction of cities to be conceived of in
new ways. It may lead to a shift in the cycles of destruction and reconstruction, with
the waging of war on cities becoming a less prominent part of military strategy. The
main result from the bombing cities is the destruction of homes and the killing of
civilians. In response, Buried City attempts to formulate a resilience to the devastation
of cities by revealing a city at its very end, its burial to its unearthing by conceiving
of an archaeology as a way to rethink destruction and the city.
When I went in search of previous works, examples and guides for Buried City,
I realised I was quite alone with my subject. Although there is a vast amount of
literature dedicated to the destruction of cities by war from ancient to modern times,
work on city burial is in its infancy. Earlier works include Thomas Gray’s The Buried
City of Kenﬁg (1909), about a Bronze Age city built on the sand dunes of Glamorgan,
Wales, that later covered it.3 John Fletcher Horne’s The Buried Cities of Vesuvius:
Herculaneum and Pompeii (1895) gives a romantic account of their burial and
discovery.4 Austen H. Layard’s The Ruins of Nineveh and Babylon (1856) is a
similarly romantic account of these two ancient Assyrian cities in Mesopotamia,
destroyed in war but not buried.
A vast array of books, some of which I reference, cover various funeral architectures
(tombs, pyramids, crypts, mausoleums, sepulchres, catacombs, gravesites) that position
human burial within the context of architecture, including Jan Assmann’s Death and
Salvation in Ancient Egypt (2001), Mike Pearson’s The Archaeology of Death and
Burial (1999), Francois Pieter Retief and Louise Cillier’s Burial Customs, The Afterlife
and The Pollution Of Death in Ancient Greece (2006), Robert Pogue Harrison’s The
Dominion of the Dead (2003), and Performing Death, a collection of essays edited
by Nicola Laneri. A book that takes a similar title to my own is Jennie Hall’s ﬁctional
novel for children, Buried Cities – Pompeii, Olympia, Mycenae, originally published
in 1922 with a foreword to boys and girls who ‘like to dig for hidden treasure’.5
Works on landscape and burial (burial mounds, piled rocks, interment) include David
2
Reﬂecting: above and below

## S. 18

Bourdon’s Designing the Earth, Human Impulse to Shape Nature (1995), Simon
Schama’s Landscape and Memory (1995), Robert Smithson’s Frederick Law-Olmsted
and the Dialectical Landscape (1979) and Landschaftsgestaltung (Landscape Design,
1952), by German post-war landscape architect Reinhold and Alice Lingner.
Works dealing with political, social and cultural relationships among architecture,
war and destruction include: Robert Bevan’s The Destruction of Memory, Architecture
at War (2007), Julia Hell and Andreas Schönle’s Ruins of Modernity (2010), Gary
A. Boyd and Denis Linehan’s Ordnance: War + Architecture & Space (2013), Paul
Virilio’s Bunker Archaeology (1976), Gaston R. Gordillo’s Rubble, The Afterlife of
Destruction (2014), John Pendlebury, Erdem Erten and Peter J. Larkham’s Alterative
Visions of Post-War Reconstruction – Creating The Modern Townscape (2014) and
J. M. Mancini and Keith Bresnahan’s Architecture and Armed Conﬂict – The Politics
of Destruction (2015). These books offer a substantial analysis of city, architecture,
destruction, ruins and memory, themes that beguiled 16th-century Italian artist
Piranesi but which remained absent in the architecture of modernity, as Andreas
Huyssen points out:
It would have never occurred to Corbusier or Mies van der Rohe to develop a
ruin theory as Speer did, even though by the early twenty-ﬁrst century much
modernist architecture has reached its own ruinous stage. However, the
catastrophes of the twentieth century have mainly left rubble behind rather than
ruins in Piranesi’s sense, even if some of that rubble has lent itself quite well to
beautifying representations. The age of the authentic ruin is over. We can write
its genealogy, but we can’t resurrect it.6
When I look at newsreel images of the destruction of Aleppo or Gaza City, I see
the overwhelming annihilation of people and city as the realisation of modernity’s
increasingly absolute technological capacity for mass destruction. It would be unwise
to suggest that Buried City can address issues of conﬂict and war, but it may address
destruction, city and burial and, human and burial; each is a burying of the other.
City and destruction
Razed, erased and abandoned
Throughout the history of human settlement there have been cities razed to the ground
and erased from the face of the earth by war, natural catastrophes (ﬂooding, volcanic
eruptions, earthquakes) or, if we are to believe, divine intervention. The biblical cities
of Sodom and Gomorrah, whose societies were judged to be sexually debauched and
destroyed by a revengeful God as described in the Book of Genesis, is an early example
of absolute destruction. ‘Then the LORD rained upon Sodom and upon Gomorrah
brimstone and ﬁre from the LORD out of heaven’.7 The crushing of Troy at the end
of the Trojan War in 1229 BC by the Achaean forces led by the Mycenae King
Agamemnon, and the ﬂattening of Babylon by the Assyrian King Sennacherib in 
689 BC, are examples of cities destroyed and razed to the ground. In I Burnt, Razed
(And) Destroyed Those Cities, Heather D. Baker tells of the ‘accounts of deliberate
archi tectural destruction’.8 Baker’s interest in destruction is formulated by the
descriptions credited to the Assyrian King Tiglath-Pileser I:
Reﬂecting: above and below
3

## S. 19

4
Reﬂecting: above and below
Their cities I burnt, razed (and) destroyed.
I burnt all their cities.
I conquered their great towns, burnt, razed, (and) destroyed (them) and turned
(them) into ruin hills.
Baker 2015, 49
Cities such as Thebes and Carthage were not only razed to the ground but also
erased from the face of the earth. After the Great Siege on Thebes, which ended in
his victory in 335 BC, the Macedonian King Alexander not only razed the city to the
ground but also erased it from the face of the earth. In the Third Punic War, which
ended with Roman victory in 149 BC, Carthage, too, was razed to the ground and
erased from the face of the earth. Not content with erasing Carthage, the Romans
also barred any future resettlement, ploughing the surrounding land with salt and
thus making it unsuitable for the cultivation of crops. Alexander the Great and the
Romans obliterated Thebes and Carthage, but they did not bury them.
Cities destroyed by natural phenomena such as the Roman cities of Pompeii 
and Herculaneum are cities neither razed or erased. The cataclysmic eruption of 
Mt Vesuvius in 79 AD that buried these cities under hundreds of tons of volcanic
ash also led to their preservation. Their discovery and unearthing in the 18th and
19th centuries, which still continues to this day, has created a breathtaking archaeo -
logical experience. Other natural devastations of cities, such as the 1906 San Francisco
earthquake and the tsunamis in the Indian Ocean (2004) and Japan (2011) that swept
away people, towns and villages, are a continual reminder of the overwhelming and
unpredictable destructive forces of nature.
Cities buried by human intervention are far rarer. One partial example is Roman
Emperor Vespasian’s burial of Nero’s Domus Aurea (Golden House) in AD 68. This
vast palace complex, covering an estimated 200 acres (approximately one third of
Rome), contained 300 rooms surrounding an internal man-made lake. ‘The Domus
Aurea was not just the imperial palace on the Palatine, but also a huge artiﬁcial
parkland covering the Celian and Esquiline hills and the valley between them, the
area now occupied by the Colosseum’.9 (Ball 2003, 4) After Nero’s suicide in AD 68,
Vespasian set about erasing any memory of Nero from the city of Rome and gave
orders to bury the Domus Aurea under hundreds of tons of soil carted from outside
of Rome. In AD 70 Vespasian then set about constructing on top of the now in-ﬁlled
lake the Roman Coliseum that was later joined with other buildings, such as the
Baths of Titus in AD 79 and the Baths of Trajan in AD 104. While Nero had set
about building Rome in his vision, Vespasian set about erasing that vision through
burial. Vespasian’s burial of Nero’s Domus Aurea connects city and human burial;
it is as much the burying of Nero as the burying of the Golden House.
In 1809, the ﬁrst publication of the Monuments of Egypt (Descriptions de l’Ègypt)
created a sensation with the French public. Napoleon’s 1798–1801 military expedition
to conquer Egypt unsuspectingly led to the documentation of its ancient monuments
from the reign of the Pharaohs. The engravings and sketches produced by Napoleon’s
engineers and draftsmen are testament to their artistry and skill:
‘. . . his staff recruited them with a view to their building roads, bridges, canals,
and other public works, and to their collaborating on topography and cartography

## S. 20

. . . They did do those things. What enthralled them, however, was Egypt, its
sand-choked monuments, its stilled enigma, its petriﬁed humanity’.10
Gillispie 1987, 2
The stunning engravings of Egypt’s monuments, reproduced by Princeton University
Press in 2002, showing colossal statues, tombs, pyramids, the Sphinx and palaces
partially buried under sand dunes, display an entirely different image, an authenticity
of ruins and decay in contrast to how they appear today.
The Great Fire of 1889 that burnt Seattle’s wooden buildings to a cinder also
presented the authorities with the opportunity to drastically alter the city’s dramatic
undulating topography as part of its reconstruction. Covering a number of stages
over a 30-year period, the regrading of Seattle not only ﬂattened the city but also
created an unusual urban condition in the city’s rebuilding. With both rebuilding and
regrading taking place simultaneously, many of Seattle’s new brick buildings were
designed to accommodate two sets of entrances, one at ground level and the other
on the ﬁrst ﬂoor. As the regrading swept through the city, many of the new buildings’
ground levels were buried, opening up the ﬁrst ﬂoor as the new street-level entrance.
Many of Seattle’s subterranean spaces can be visited, offering a 19th-century
archaeological experience of former street fronts, stores and sidewalks.
Reﬂecting: above and below
5
Figure 2 Bunker Humboldthain (Wedding) Zuschuttung des zerstorten Hochbunkers, 
F Rep.290-(02)-0264729, Landesarchiv Berlin
Photo: E. Schwab

## S. 21

The 20th century is arguably a historical period of unprecedented destruction,
hand-in-hand with the progressive technologies of war. Cities bombed, damaged and
destroyed during WWI (Ypres, Albert and Verdun), the Spanish Civil War (Guernica),
WWII (Tokyo, Osaka, Hiroshima, Nagasaki Berlin, Dresden, Cologne, Hamburg,
Coventry, London, Warsaw, Stalingrad) and the Vietnam War (Hanoi, Haiphong
and Huê) bear witness to mass technological devastation. All of these cities have been
rebuilt to one degree or another and many have had their urban environments
drastically redrawn and altered to incorporate urban density and infrastructure
modernisation programmes. Destruction from war was not limited to cities but also
affected the landscapes in between. According to Nicholas J. Saunders, the destruction
and scarring of the countryside during WWI permanently altered its topography.
The Great War brought cataclysmic disorder to large areas of northern France
and Belgium, banishing the images of a prosperous mainly pastoral past, and
replacing them with those of total war. The destruction of land and life created
new landscapes infused with new meanings – a reordering of existence whose
memories and associations came into conﬂict with other realities after 1918, and
continue to do so today.11
Saunders 2001, 38
During the Vietnam War, the spraying of the chemical defoliant Agent Orange,
designed to denude forest vegetation along the Vietnam/Cambodian border, also
caused horrendous effects on unborn babies. As if this was not enough, carpet
bombing of the same area saw the obliteration of the topography with massive craters.
In Theory of the Earth (1719), Thomas Burnet refers to a world of ‘Fractions, Ruins
and Dissolutions that have been on the Earth, and which we have shewn to be
inexplicable, unless we admit that the Earth was once in another Form’.12 Burnet’s
‘Fractions, Ruins and Dissolutions’ are earthbound but can also be related to what
we build on the earth. In Post Mortem: Building Destruction, Kazi K. Ashraf
formulates a non-discretionary world kaleidoscope operating in architecture and
destruction from the ancient world to the architectural failures of modernity.
Presented at the outset, a provisional typology of building destructions suggests
the following: nihilistic (most famously, the apocryphal scene of Nero playing
the lyre while the city burnt, or Pompeii or Fukushima ravaged by a natural
disaster); tactical (triumphal destruction of cities from Alexander to Genghis
Khan, the Blitzkrieg of the Second World War, or the communally-fuelled
demolition of the mosque, the Babri Masjid, in Ayodhya, (Uttar Pradesh) India
in 1992; and transitional, one that leads to the release of a new epoch (Pruitt-
Igoe or Philadelphia’s Veterans Stadium).13
Ashraf 2013, 81
Pruitt-Igoe, the 1950s housing complex in St. Louis, Missouri, mentioned by
Ashraf, can be seen as the fall from grace of 20th-century modernist idealism. Mostly
abandoned and decaying only 20 years after their completion, the 33 buildings that
made up Pruitt-Igoe were successively dynamited and by 1976 all had been demolished
and erased. Philadelphia’s Veterans Stadium had a similar fate. Completed in 1971,
it soon became apparent that it was unsuitable for playing and viewing of either
6
Reﬂecting: above and below

## S. 22

baseball or American football. Calls for its demolition gathered strength and in 2004,
after only 32 years in existence, the stadium was imploded and completely erased.
Both Philadelphia’s Veterans Stadium and Pruitt-Igoe illustrate the fate of architecture
as temporary and dispensable.
In The City in History, Lewis Mumford points out that the development of
civilization and cities is built on a history of annihilation by societies over one
another. Mumford states that, ‘cities have repeatedly outlived the military empires
that seemingly destroyed them forever . . . that (they) still stand on the sites they
originally occupied’.14 Mumford believes that these cities are reborn through ‘the
constant recruitment of new life, fresh and un-sophisticated, from the rural regions,
full of crude muscular strength, sexual vitality, procreative zeal, animal faith’. The
destructive-creative-destruction cycle Mumford points to has relentlessly propelled
the evolutionary progress of civilization, city and history: ‘Thus the most precious
invention of civilization, the city, second only to language itself in the transformation
of culture, became from the outset the container of disruptive internal forces, directed
toward ceaseless destruction and extermination’ (1961, 53). If not erased, the city
can be rebuilt, continuing the human occupation over the same ground.
The survival of the city aided by such ‘muscular strength, sexual vitality, pro creative
zeal, animal faith’ also reckons with the human ability to erase violence through
Reﬂecting: above and below
7
Figure 3 Zuschuttung des zerstorten Hochbunkers in Humdoldthain Bezirk Wedding, 
F Rep.290–264730
Photo: Landesarchiv Berlin

## S. 23

rebuilding. In Place Annihilation: Area Bombing and the Fate of Urban Places,
Kenneth Hewitt explains: ‘The destruction of places has been a recurrent consequence
of natural calamity and human violence’.15 In The Time of Violence, Deconstruction
and Value, Elizabeth Grosz asks ‘What is the counter to violence? What is the other
of violence?’16 The answer may well be that the human capacity for destruction is
equalled by its instinct for survival. In The Anatomy of Human Destructiveness, Erich
Fromm references the English psychologist William McDougall’s analysis of human
destruction ‘under two categories – the sexual instincts and the instinct for selfpreservation’.17 If the city satisﬁes the human desire/need for collective living and
war, the capacity for destruction, then instinct provides the desire to retrieve our
material culture. To reconstruct is to erase the memory of destruction. In Oblivion,
Marc Augé argues that it is necessary to concede memory to oblivion so that forgetting
may be enacted.18 Augé cites three ﬁgures of oblivion in regard to memory:
The ﬁrst is that of the return, whose ﬁrst ambition is to ﬁnd a lost past again
by forgetting the present – as well as the immediate past with which it tends
to be confused . . .
The second ﬁgure is that of suspense, whose ﬁrst ambition is to ﬁnd the
present by provisionally cutting it off form the past and the future and, more
precisely, by forgetting the future inasmuch as the later is identiﬁed with the
return of the past.
The third is that of the beginning or, shall we say, the rebeginning . . . 
It aspires to ﬁnd the future again by forgetting the past, to create the
conditions for a new birth that, by deﬁnition, opens up into every possible
future without favouring a single one.
Augé 2004, 56–7
In The Destruction of Memory, Architecture at War, Robert Bevan charts the
repression and material destruction of sites (political, cultural, religious) as ‘the fate
of buildings as part of genocide and ethnic cleansing’.19 Bevan’s work is wide-ranging
and takes in signiﬁcant conﬂicts of the 20th century, from the destruction of
synagogues and Jewish-owned shops by the Nazis in 1938 (Kristallnacht) to the
destruction of mosques, churches, government buildings and historical infrastructure
by Bosnians, Croats and Serbs in the Bosnian War (1992–1995), to the Taliban’s
destruction of the Bamiyan Buddhas in Afghanistan in 2001. As cultural, religious
and political symbols, buildings are the material presence of individual societies,
expressing their culture as much as literature, music and art.
A library or art gallery is a cache of historical memory, evidence that a given
community’s presence extends into the past and legitimising it in the present and
on into the future. In these circumstances structures and places with certain
meanings are selected for oblivion with deliberate intent. This is not ‘collateral
damage’. This is the active and often systematic destruction of particular building
types or architectural traditions that happens in conﬂicts where the erasure of
the memories, history and identity attached to architecture and place – enforced
forgetting – is the goal itself.
Bevan 2007, 8
8
Reﬂecting: above and below

## S. 24

The destruction of societies’ iconic landmarks is ‘the targeting of buildings on
campaigns of terror and conquest, in the structures erected and demolished to keep
peoples apart or force them together, and those levelled at the hands of revolutionary
new orders that want to build Utopia on the ruins of the past’ (Bevan 2007, 10).
Referring to On War, a treatise by the 19th-century German military tactician Claus
von Clausewitz, Gary A. Boyd and Denis Linehan note that the city, by the very
nature of its infrastructure and the production of armaments, is, even in peacetime,
connected to war.
Thus, the creativity emphasised by Claus von Clausewitz, which we draw upon
here, as being so necessary to the pursuit of war can be seen as a darker and
more explicit iteration of the creative destruction in space necessary for the
accumulation of capital or, the production of architecture.20
Boyd and Linehan 2013, 1–2
In Sketches of War: The Graveyards of Historical Memory, Jonathan Charley’s
chapter in the same book echoes Bevan’s material destruction coming from the other
side, i.e. the memorialisation of destruction and death through building rather than
erasing.
The need to objectify the memory of war and tragedy is a survival mechanism
by which we distance the overwhelming reality that the history of civilisation is
overshadowed by barbarism. We feel obliged to build memorials, erect plagues,
and preserve the remains of brutal episodes in which the act of remembrance is
also a desire to forget.21
Boyd and Linehan 2013, 263
Charley’s point is twofold: the destruction of buildings and artefacts in war allows
forgetting through reconstruction, while erecting monuments allows what is
supposedly being remembered to be forgotten. In On the Natural History of
Destruction, W.G. Sebald ﬁnds such forgetting in post-war German literature’s silence
concerning the destruction of German towns and cities during WWII. Sebald charts
the collective forgetfulness permeating every level of German civilian life in the
aftermath of the war. Sebald attributed this forgetting to a type of amnesia, a selfanaesthesia ‘shown by a community that seemed to have emerged from a war of
annihilation without any signs of psychological impairment’22 (2003, 11). This,
Sebald informs us, accounts for the lack of any substantial historical testimony from
a German perspective of their horror and loss in the war. The ‘images’ Sebald has
in mind are the annihilation of German towns and cities:
The question of whether and how it [the ﬁre-bombing of cities] could be justiﬁed
was never the subject of open debate in Germany after 1945, no doubt mainly
because a nation which had murdered and worked to death millions of people
in its camps could hardly call on the victorious powers to explain the military
and political logic that dictated the destruction of German cities.
Sebald 2003, 13
The cultural and socio-political absences expressed in Sebald’s theory of selfanaesthesia reﬂect a society consumed by guilt at the very moment when it was faced
Reﬂecting: above and below
9

## S. 25

10
Reﬂecting: above and below
with the immense task of reconstructing its devastated cities. Sebald implies that this
forced forgetting created a fragmented memory of the war and, importantly, suppressed
any voice that would recount the suffering of the civilian population resulting from
the destruction of its cities. Bevan, on the other hand, unravels the thought processes
that order the destruction of cultural representations of societies to erase their memory,
while Charley suggests that building memorials to commemor ate war and destruction
facilitates visual memory but in doing so enacts forgetting. Each author points to 
a relationship between destruction and memory through either material or psycho -
logical erasure. Destruction, Paul Virilio informs us, has always been an integral part
of the city’s evolution. ‘In truth the principle of arms has always been aimed at this
deconstruction, ﬁrst of man’s body, of armour, then of the rampart built for his
protection. Afterwards, the very conditions of the human habitat became the primary
objectives of this destruction/destructuration’.23
In War and the City, Gregory Ashworth argues: ‘Urban destruction as part of a
campaign strategy has a long lineage – from the Israelites’ massacres of the inhabitants
of the Philistine and Canaanite cities, to Hiroshima’24 (1991, 114). In their
introduction to Walter Benjamin’s Philosophy Destruction and Experience, Andrew
Benjamin and Peter Osborne write:
The present is constructed in the destruction and reconstitution of tradition. 
As the temporality of history is rethought under the pressure of such a conception
of the present, so this rethinking comes to deﬁne the present (this present, now),
philosophically.25
Benjamin and Osborne 1994, 12
In his chapter from the same book, Benjamin, Heidegger and the Destruction of
Tradition, Howard Caygill writes: ‘In Benjamin tradition is ruination – barbarism –
it destroys what it hands over; yet without this destruction nothing would be handed
over. The work of art is a ruin, a site of mourning where the destruction of tradition
can be acknowledged’26 (1994, 21). In Illuminations, Benjamin himself portrays the
ascendancy of warfare over the capacity for production:
The shorter the training period of an industrial worker is, the longer that of 
a military man becomes. It may be part of society’s preparation for total war
that training is shifting from the practice of production to the practice of
destruction.27
Benjamin 1968, 193
Benjamin’s observations are no doubt a reference to the Nazi militarisation of
society that later would lead to Propaganda Minister Joseph Goebbels’ infamous 1943
speech declaring Total War in Berlin’s Sports Palace (Sportpalast). Total War as
espoused by Goebbels meant absolute commitment from the German people to the
total destruction of their enemies and themselves. ‘Its self-alienation has reached such
a degree that it can experience its own destruction as an aesthetic pleasure of the
ﬁrst order. This is the situation of politics which Fascism is rendering aesthetic’
(Benjamin 1968, 242). Goebbels’ Total War was in fact a product of industrialisation.
According to Stephen Graham: ‘By the 19th and 20th centuries, industrial cities in

## S. 26

the global north had grown in synchrony with the killing powers of technology. They
provided the men and material to sustain the massive, industrial wars of the 20th
century’28 (2004, 166). According to Graham’s Postmortem City, Towards an Urban
Geopolitics, cities, as spaces for living, are also spaces synonymous with terror, war
and destruction.
Cities, then, provide much more than just the backdrop or environment for war
and terror. Rather, their buildings, assets, institutions, industries, infrastructures,
cultural diversities, and symbolic meanings have long actually themselves been
the explicit target for a wide range of deliberate, orchestrated, attacks.
Graham 2004, 166
The destruction of cities, it can be assumed, is negated by the capacity to remove
the visual remainders of destruction. In Rubble, The Afterlife of Destruction, Gaston
R. Gordillo asks for a revaluation of what is left of a city when reduce to ruins.
In questioning my own abstracted veneration of the ruins’ material form, I
gradually learned to see such objects through the lens of the most concrete,
unglamorous term we have to name what is created by the destruction of space:
rubble. But this shift in perspective also forced me to do away with the mainstream
downgrading of rubble as shapeless, worthless debris, and instead to explore
rubble as textured, affectively charged matter that is intrinsic to all living places.29
Gordillo 2014, 5
Destruction becomes reconstruction as much as reconstruction becomes destruc -
tion. As Bevan pointed to the ethnic destruction of the cultural and architectural
artefacts of individual societies, destruction can also be used to erase societies from
their spaces of occupation. In her book Dwelling in Conﬂict, Emily McKee describes
the orchestrated destruction of a Bedouin village in the Negev region of southern
Israel.
Before dawn one July morning in 2010, hundreds of police ofﬁcers and heavy
machinery rolled into the Bedouin village of al-’Araqib. Police ofﬁcers, helmeted
and holding large shields, evicted families and moved all the residents away from
the houses. As some residents shufﬂed away, others attempted to hold their ground.
Police formed a line between residents and their homes, and destruction teams
with bulldozers moved in and crushed the village’s eleven cinderblock buildings
and thirty-four homes made of corrugated metal, while approximately three
hundred residents and several dozen allied Bedouin rights activists looked on.30
McKee 2016, 51
These modern tools of the sanctioned destruction of people’s dwellings (bulldozer,
army, police) continue the war on architecture, people and societies, culture and
beliefs. Destruction begets destruction. Razing and erasing cities and societies begs
the question of what to do with the remnants of destruction and of how these
remnants can be reshaped to reﬂect human experience on the war on people and
their cities.
Reﬂecting: above and below
11

## S. 27

Mound and earth
In Designing the Earth, The Human impulse to Shape Nature, David Bourdon
discusses human relationships with geography, landscape, building and ground.
Exploring the human impact on ground (shelter, burial mounds, war defences, vast
mining works, land art), Bourdon’s book pictorially illustrates the immense cultural
diversity in how societies have shaped landscapes. Bourdon cites monumental earth
constructions in Mexico such as the Olmec Pyramid of La Venta (900 BC) built by
the Olmec people and, approximately 1500 years later, the Pyramid of the Sun (AD
500) constructed by the Mochia people of the Moche Valley in Peru. These massive
earth constructions, designed for ceremonial communications with their Gods, are
distinctly different yet ultimately connected to the earth mounds of the dead. The
Urümqi Muslim necropolis of Xinjiang Uygar in north-western China contains
hundreds of burial mounds marked with tombstones of the dead, resembling the earth
mounds ringed by stones in the shape of a boat of the 11th-century Viking necropolis
at Lindholm Høje in Northern Jutland, Denmark, or the great earth construction
undertaken by the Chillicothe Indians of Ohio that contains more than twenty mass
burial mounds. The Aboriginal mounds in the Murray region of Western Victoria,
Australia, were not only burial sites but also used as ovens for cooking and, in time
12
Reﬂecting: above and below
Figure 4 Trummerberge an Knie (spater Ernst-Reuter-Platz) Bezirk Charlottenburg,
25.07.1949, F Rep.290–2127, Landesarchiv Berlin
Photo: Willy Kiel

## S. 28

of ﬂooding, protection.31 All of these movements in shaping the ground for burial
reﬂect the intrinsic desire of cultures to reshape their surroundings.
Moving from the shaping of earth to the shaping of stone, the elevated tomb of
Darius II in Naqsh-e Rustam Iran, carved into the rock face in the 5th century BC,
or the Nabataean Treasury of Pharaoh in Petra in Jordan, likewise carved from the
rock face in the 1st and 2nd centuries AD, combine burial tomb and geological
architecture. The Gila cliff dwellings of New Mexico, the carved dwellings of the
Puebloans in the Mesa Verde National Park in Colorado or the the Lycian tombs
near Dalyan in Turkey are examples of geological architectures. The San Gennaro
and San Gaudiosus catacombs in Naples further develop the relationship of earth
and strata, city and burial. These underground urban environments of passageways,
crypts, altars and niches for the dead carved into the tufa limestone are also mirrored
spaces, with life-sized portraits painted onto the walls to reﬂect their position in the
above-ground city of the living.
The underground burial places of the dead can be also be spaces for the living.
The early Irish and Viking dugout dwellings that bore only one entrance are examples
of inhabiting within ground. Multiple dwellings in ground can be found in the
underground city at Derinkuyu in Turkey’s Nevşehir Province. Carved from soft
volcanic rock in the 7th and 8th centuries BC, ﬁrst by the Phrygian people and later
by Christians, this underground city complex is unique given its size. Reaching a
depth of 60 metres, this multi-level city and home to 20,000 people reached its peak
during the Byzantine era when it was used as a place of refuge during the wars between
Arab tribes and Byzantine Christians from the 7th to 11th centuries AD. The complex
arterial passageways, airshafts, living, sleeping, cooking, water and sewerage quarters
constitute an intrinsic connection between architecture and earth.
In Making, Anthropology, Archaeology, Art and Architecture, Tim Ingold states
that the nature of earth, if carted and piled, will always ﬁnd its natural form.
If you heap stuff up over a period of time, always adding to the top of the pile
and allowing it to settle of its own accord, it will generally form a mound, roughly
circular in plan and conical or bell-shaped in elevation.32
Ingold 2013, 75
Dwelling in ground as much as dwelling above ground has entwined societies with
earth as the inseparable unison for survival. The earthed dwellings of the living and
the departed continue the in-ground relationships between humans in shaping the
earth. In The New Science, 16th-century Italian theorist Giambattista Vico identiﬁed
that the earth relationships of the dead in ground were also the ancestral markers
for claiming ground. Vico refers to the placement of the funerary urn as one such
marker. In Robert Pogue Harrison’s The Dominion of the Dead, human and earth
form the humic foundation within ground.
The work of history fell to the ground it had tried to surmount under the auspices
of god. This is the ground, or humus, of the ancestors. As the subterranean
commandments of the dead cease to persuade the ironic generations, the forests
gradually overtake the clearings and close the lids of the lucus.33
Harrison 2003, 13
Reﬂecting: above and below
13

## S. 29

Integral to life and death, the living and the dead were joined by their in- and onground relationships. As the dead mark ground, the living claimed the ground the
dead occupied. In ancient Greek and Roman societies the dead were viewed as 
a form of pollution, and anyone who came in contact with the dead became pol -
luted. The ground that covered the dead allowed the pollution to seep from the body
and, over time, purify it and the family of the living. As cities grew and outbreaks
of plagues ravished societies, the dead were expelled from the living and buried in
designated mass burial sites outside the city. As the laying of artiﬁcial surfaces
increased over ground and the earth mounds that interred the dead were levelled,
the separation between human and ground increased, forever shifting the power of
the dead and living hold on ground.
In Flesh and Stone, Richard Sennett explores the relationships between the body,
the city and capital. Sennett cites the 11th-century English author and clergyman
John of Salisbury, who likened the city to the body and no doubt to the architecture
of the church; the extruded plan of the cruciﬁed Christ.
John of Salisbury in turn connected the shape of the human body and the form
of a city: the city’s palace or cathedral he thought of as its head, the central
market as its stomach, the city’s hands and feet as its houses.34
Sennett 1996, 23
Connecting Salisbury’s body and city symbolism and the dead and living
associations with ground and burial to the subject of the buried city is to form the
view that city and burial are equally intertwined.
The subject of this book, the buried city at Teufelsberg, metaphorically marks a
return of the burial mound; the material body (rubble) of the destroyed (dead) city.
Teufelsberg’s vast rubble hill marks the ground and history of Berlin’s destruction.
Its connection to the city of Berlin can be viewed in the same way the dead can be
traced to the living. It is burial – city and human – that is at the heart of Buried
City. Yet Teufelsberg remains invisible, an unmarked grave to the present city of
Berlin. This book proposes a connection between city, people and burial from the
point of burial. Connecting city and human burial would, I suspect, amount to deﬁning
their association and then representation in terms of their mirroring. Jean Baudrillard
informs us that ‘representation tries to absorb simulation by interpreting it as false
representation, simulation envelops the whole ediﬁce of representation as itself a
simulacrum’35 (1983, 11). One way to connect Teufelsberg’s simulacrum to Berlin is
to respond to the burial mound designed for the disappearance of the destroyed city.
‘We live in a world of simulation’ Baudrillard tells us, ‘in a world where the highest
function of the sign is to make reality disappear, and at the same time to mask this
disappearance’36 (1997, 12). To unmask the buried city is to portray it as an extension
of the present city of Berlin; an unearthing of its ground and forgetting.
Conclusion
The chapters of Buried City address various concepts and ways of thinking human
and city burial: ruins, forgetting, burial, disappearance, new ground and remembering.
Chapter 1, Ruins – self-portraiture, capturing forgetting, surveys a series of archive
images, seeking a way to interpret their depictions of city, people, destruction and
14
Reﬂecting: above and below

## S. 30

ruins. It references works on image and photography by writers including Susan
Sontag, John Berger and Roland Barthes. The chapter details the images relating to
the women and men (Trümmerfrauen and Trümmermänner) who worked in clearing
the rubble from their city to burying it at Teufelsberg. It also examines the aesthetics
of ruins including Albert Speer’s ‘Ruin Value Theory’ (Ruinwerttheorie), Alexander
Kluge’s 1961 ﬁlm Brutality in Stone (Brutalität in Stein) and the geographies
connecting Berlin’s seven rubble hills (Trümmerberge) to the seven hills of Athens
and Rome. Chapter 2, Forgetting – self-anaesthesia, cultural forgetting, offers a
literary review of the silence in recording the civilian population’s experiences of the
war and the destruction of their towns and cities during WWII, from a German
perspective. Referencing W.G. Sebald’s On the Natural History of Destruction and
a number of German writers, including Heinrich Böll’s The Silent Angle, Hans Erich
Nossack’s The End and An Offering for the Dead and Alexander Kluge’s Air Raid,
the chapter lays the foundation for understanding the self-imposed cultural amnesia
permeating post-war German society.
Chapter 3, Burial – abandoning the city, physical forgetting, examines how
practices of human burial and city burial can be interrelated, exploring ancient
Egyptian and European burial as well as the work of Giambattista Vico and Robert
Pogue Harrison on burial and ancestry. It also explores the materiality of trauma,
referencing mourning and cryptography within burial. Crucially, the chapter
introduces the landscape theories of Reinhold Lingner, one of the main proponents
of transforming Berlin’s vast rubble mounds into landscapes of recreation and wellbeing through his Biomorphic Vision. Chapter 4, Disappearance – planting the forest,
natural forgetting, takes a look at how the planting of a forest hides the buried city,
leading to its forgetting. It refers to Psychotope of Destruction, Hanns Hörbiger’s
pseudo-science of World Ice Theory and the connections of society and leisure
discussed by Henri Lefebvre in The Critique of Everyday Life.
Chapter 5, New Ground – unearthing Teufelsberg, against forgetting, is dedicated
to exploring conditions for remembering the buried city. It begins with an exploration
of archaeology (the unearthing of historical artefacts) and how we might value the
unearthing of Teufelsberg in the same way. It also introduces the narrative of a future
archaeologist, an explorer who, 1000 years from now, unearths Teufelsberg to
discover the remnants of a vast buried city. The Conclusion, Remembering – undoing
forgetting, introduces two proposed archaeological digs on Teufelsberg. Collectively
titled The Reveal, the archaeological projects are aimed at unearthing the buried city
in two distinct scales. Project 1, Graphiens, proposes a series of extracted core rubble
samples from Teufelsberg’s Drachenberg (Kite Hill) to create above-ground standing
memorials to the Trümmerfrauen and Trümmermännen of Berlin. Dissection proposes
an excavation into Drachenberg to create an in-ground intersection and experience
between the public and the buried city.
Walking the rubble hill of Teufelsberg is another way of walking the city of Berlin.
In my mind, each is an extension of the other. It is also another way of experiencing
Berlin. Teufelsberg and Berlin are inextricably tied to histories of destruction and
reconstruction. The destruction of one created the making of the other. Both are
cities. One a city of people living and working (of houses, streets, trains, hospitals,
shops, cinemas, bars and clubs), the other a burial mound to be walked over (concrete,
bricks, stone, mortar, crushed interiors and lost homes of the former city transformed
into a park). To understand the buried city is to acknowledge its inverse mirror reality
Reﬂecting: above and below
15

## S. 31

to present-day Berlin. It carries the continuity of the city as ruins as much as human
burial transmits the body into decay.

## S. 34

Ruins
Self-portraiture, capturing,
forgetting
Introduction
As Berlin lay in ruins at the end of WWII, the immense task of clearing the rubble
from the streets fell to the Trümmerfrauen (rubble women) and the accompanying
girls, boys and men. The carting and off-loading of millions of cubic metres of rubble
undertaken by them is a story of their labour in both the reconstruction and the
burying of their city. Referencing an archive of photographs I have collected over
many years, this chapter addresses a population working within the ruins to salvage
both their city and their humanity devastated by war.
Rubblescape – working in ruins
To understand how a large part of Berlin was buried, one must begin with the city’s
destruction by the Allied bombing campaign (1942–1945) during World War II.
Walking through Berlin after the end of the war in Europe (7 May 1945) and
surveying the scale of the damage, Winston Churchill pondered the onslaught
unleashed on the city. For a moment, such empathy with the tragedy that had befallen
Berlin and its people was possible (though this soon changed as more of the atrocities
committed under the Nazi regime emerged). The graphic images of Berlin’s destruction
are overwhelming. Carpet bombing resulted in a city ﬁlled with ghostly shells spewing
debris, blurring street and building, merely hinting at what happened to the
inhabitants. Within this absence of what could be called a city (materially crushed
and void of utility) was perhaps a moment where Churchill, self-assured in victory
over Nazi Germany, mindful of the millions of Jews murdered and the 60 million
lives lost, could question, momentarily, the Allies’ own policy of air war obliteration
of German cities.
Watching documentary ﬁlms of Berlin recorded immediately after World War II
is a numbing experience, an experience of disbelief in which the spectacle of material
destruction overshadows the horrors of the human cost in terms of lives lost. Blackand-white footage shows the devastation via street views, ﬂyovers and contrasting
camera angles of a city fallen out of gravity create an historical distance, keeping the
images ﬁrmly in the past. Films in colour, on the other hand, show the immediacy
of the time, bringing the destruction into the present and heightening its realness:
views of buildings spilling out onto the street, piles of red bricks, ravaged trees and
wild vegetation claiming the ruins. People walk on pathways carved through the
rubble and disappear behind its mounds, while others emerge from this landscape.
There are also a number of feature ﬁlms, such as Roberto Rossellini’s Deutschland

## S. 35

im Jahre Null (Germany in Year Zero, 1948) that centres on a family’s ability to
survive in this environment, and especially the struggle of a young boy, Edmund
Köhler, to come to terms with growing up in a city consumed by destruction,
exploitation and the reality of an uncertain future.1 Through the boy’s eyes, Rossellini
portrays the city as a series of obstacles to survival among the ruins. The boy runs
errands for shadowy ﬁgures, buying items on the black market, and is exposed 
to (implied) paedophilia as other children enjoy a paradise playground of rubble and
debris. The boy’s streetwise survival is interwoven with his silent suffering, innocence
and attempts to understand the recent past as his search for answers is thwarted.
The ﬁlm ends on the day of Edmund’s grandfather’s funeral, where he climbs to 
the top ﬂoor of a bombed-out building and falls to his death. We are left unsure
whether the boy’s suicide is prompted by his grandfather’s death or whether Rossellini
sees the boy as symbolising the guilt and dissolution of society as a whole. The ﬁlm
is a stark reﬂection of Berlin’s (and Germany’s) post-war years. In The Sound of
Ruins, Lutz Koepnick describes Renzo Rossellini’s sound in the ﬁlm: ‘In allowing
nondiegetic music and voices to guide our paths to the diegetic space of Berlin ruins,
Rossellini at once stirs and restrains the viewer’s affect’2 (2008, 199). Rossellini’s
ﬁlm describes a family’s struggle to survive in a city immersed in rubble. In my mind,
the boy embodies the resilience that was to characterise German post-war society
caught in a vacuum of depression and melancholy. The boy’s suicidal fall links to a
nation fallen to incomprehensible depths. Any re-emergence from the horrors and
tragedy of the war are bound up with answering the questions of the young.
A series of archive photographs depicting life among the ruins in post-war Berlin
portray, various forms of tragedy. These black-and-white pictures show men and
women working in the ruins of their city, removing the rubble from streets and
destroyed buildings. This immense task fell mainly to the women and men who had
survived the war and, later, to soldiers returning from Russian and Allied prison
camps. The photographs show workers pushing rubble-ﬁlled trucks along temporary
tracks laid over vast mounds of debris (Trümmerbahnen, rubble railways), their backs
bent by the exertion. Elsewhere, women form chains from the top of the mounds
down to street level, passing buckets laden with rubble between them. Evident in
every image is the acute reality of their labour and the combinations of people
working together to form an interconnected entity.
Other photographs show women chiselling mortar from bricks that are stacked
in huge rows for the reconstruction of buildings. Combustible materials such as
wooden beams, ﬂoorboards, doors and frames do not appear in the images, burnt
to ashes by the intense heat generated during the ﬁrestorms that engulfed the city
after the bombing raids. If not melted into puddles, malleable metals such as lead
and copper (rooﬁng, guttering, electrical wiring) were pulled from the rubble for
recycling, along with reinforcing rods and steel beams. What is compelling about
these photographs is the way they capture the immense task and the manual labour
undertaken by the women and men working to rescue their city from ruination. Fragile
bodies of ﬂesh and bone are counterpointed with the material mass of stone, brick
and steel. Within each movement the women and men undertake, destruction passes
between their bodies. Each photograph takes on a fascination with regard to the
overall spectacle of ruination, yet each image captures a traumatic scene. As materials
traverse the process of physical labour, a working through of inconsolable destruction
emerges. Another compelling aspect is the difference in the women’s work clothes.
20
Ruins: capturing, forgetting

## S. 36

Some wear dresses, shoes and sandals, while others have protective overalls, boots
and gloves. The acute shortage of clothes and footwear meant that many women and
men worked in the rubble with whatever they had.
The photographs I have sourced overwhelmingly depict more women working in
the rubble than men. Whether this reﬂects the choice of the mainly male photog raphers,
who thought it novel to photograph women working among the rubble, or whether
it reﬂects the reality of the labour available, is debatable. With more than 3.5 million
German soldiers killed and many imprisoned after the war, the photo graphs could be
seen as evidence of who was left to carry out the work. Collectively the photographs
show the destruction of a city and the role women played in clearing rubble from the
streets. According to Susan Sonntag in On Photography: ‘Photo graphic images are
pieces of evidence in an ongoing biography or history. And one photograph, unlike
one painting, implies that there will be others’3 (173, 130). Sonntag’s view of photo -
graphs as ‘pieces of evidence’ can be contested with regard to the reality of the time
in which they were taken. In her book Mythos Trümmer frauen (The Rubble Women
Myth), Leonie Treber explores the idea that the rubble women were a mythical social
construct needed at the time to show to the outside world the German people’s
resilience in the face of adversity and their ability to rebuild from the ashes of
destruction.4 This view is supported, Treber argues, by the relatively small portion 
of women engaged in clearing the rubble. She estimates that a mere 5% of the total
workforce clearing the streets of rubble were women, and, further more, that they
worked only for a brief period immediately after the war.
Ruins: capturing, forgetting
21
Figure 5 Feldbahnloren Konigsplatz 1948, F Rep.290–0172325, Landesarchiv Berlin
Photo: Ewald Gnilka

## S. 37

Although this contestation of the numbers of Trümmerfrauen may be read as an
attempt to downgrade their actual value in Berlin’s early reconstruction,5 they can
be explored in less critical terms as positive post-war propaganda images in a country
on the brink of moral and physical collapse. In this view, what is important is the
active presence of these women in the ruins of their city, taking back some form of
control from a war created and dominated by men (and supported by women?) with
their capacity for terror, killing and destruction. Each photograph illustrates a system
for reordering the immense disorder of their destroyed city. The complexities we
normally associate with the city (its infrastructure, workplaces, cultural institutions
and homes that support the population) have all but vanished. Each image is a
reminder of the fragility of the city and our existence within it.
The occupation of the Trümmerfrauen within the ruins reinforces the absence 
of a city; their physical connection to Berlin’s ruination forms their continuity with
the city; its survival as a place for living is linked to their own. Differing although
not challenging Treber’s Trümmerfrauen myth, I am concerned not with using
statistics to dispute their contribution, but with asserting their role as much as the
men’s. A series of photographs from 1965 shows the then Berlin Mayor Willy Brandt
(who became Chancellor in 1969) addressing a gathering of women in the Schultheiss-
Restaurant at Fehrbelliner Platz in Wilmersdorf. These are the Trümmerfrauen who
formed the Club der Trümmerfrauen as a name for their social gatherings. On a
separate occasion, twenty years later in 1985, the then Mayor Eberhard Diepgen is
Figure 6 Trümmerfrauen Hagelberger Strasse Kreuzberg 1949, F Rep.290–0184802,
Landesarchiv Berlin
Photo: Durniok
22
Ruins: capturing, forgetting

## S. 38

seen hosting a reception in their honour at Schöneberg Town Hall. The Club der
Trümmerfrauen as a social entity can also be seen as marking their history and role
in Berlin’s reconstruction. The presence of Brandt and Diepgen may be taken as
evidence of the debt society owes to these women in the early reconstruction of Berlin,
and as politicians it may simply be lip service. In my mind, what is important and
clear is that by setting up their association, these women were afﬁrming their role –
it was both social and political.
The political dimension can be highlighted with reference to the physical absence
of memorials to the role of the Trümmerfrauen in Berlin’s reconstruction. While
actively looking for physical remembrance, I have so far encountered two statues
dedicated to their work. One is to be found in the Marx-Engels-Forum in Berlin’s
Mitte district; cast in bronze, this small statue of a standing ﬁgure on a plinth is hard
to ﬁnd due to its obscure location in the park. A second statue stands in the
Hasenheide Park. Much larger than the Trümmerfrauen statue in the Marx-Engels-
Forum, this statue carved from stone and unveiled in 1956 is likewise obscurely sited
within the park and partially hidden by overgrown bushes. The statue consists of a
seated ﬁgure dressed in a headscarf, a cloak and smock and clogs holding a hammer
Figure 7 Club der Trümmerfrauen Schultheiss-Restaurant Fehrbelliner Platz Bezirk
Wilmersdorf, 07.12.1965, F Rep.290–109578 Landesarchiv Berlin
Photo: Bert Saß
Ruins: capturing, forgetting
23

## S. 39

in her lap. The naïvely rendered aesthetic of the Trümmerfrauen statue contrasts with
the consciously ascribed aesthetics of the many statues of men that dot Berlin.
Aesthetic arguments aside, there remain vast representational differences between the
remembrance of the Trümmerfrauen and the many heroic memorials to men and
various wars. At some stage a project is called for to adequately depict the role of
the Trümmerfrauen; the labour of their hands and the hardships they endured in
Berlin’s reconstruction. The archive photographs of women labouring in landscapes
of ruination bring to the viewer both fascination and terror. My interest in these
photographs is the relationship of the ruin with the body and manual labour. I ﬁnd
myself peering into the images, looking into the faces of the women and appraising
their tasks as they cooperate in a self-organising system to clear, clean and stack
reusable bricks, push rail trucks or salvage steel rods.
Ruinenlust – attraction and spectacle: looking on
The presence of passers-by in the photographs, who stop to gaze at the women and
men working to clear the rubble, speaks of an attraction to the spectacle of their
destroyed city. These spectators, some dressed in coats with briefcases in hand, others
cycling to or from work, engage in this fascination – a theatre of ruins. Three years
of continual bombing had made Berlin’s inhabitant’s witnesses and victims of its
destruction. Given the absence of death in the photographs, the frame is ﬁlled only
Figure 8 Trümmerfrauen 1948, FRep.290–371191, Landesarchiv Berlin
Photo: Seigfried Blohm
24
Ruins: capturing, forgetting

## S. 40

with the violence of destruction. They are a striking compilation of destruction where
dwelling and shelter, the hallmarks of functional architecture, have fallen to the
massacre of the city.
In About Looking, John Berger begins his investigation into how we look at
something by arguing that what we look at is not necessarily what we see. He uses
the example of our fascination with looking at animals, through which our human
distinction and separation from the animal world surfaces. For Berger, this curiosity
with animals is also tied to avoiding our individual human selves and others. In
animals, he suggests, we try to understand humanity through restrictions employed
in looking. Looking at animals in zoos, Berger claims, is to witness their indifference:
‘Their dependence and isolation have so conditioned their responses that they treat
any event which takes place around them – usually it is in front of them, where the
public is – as marginal. (Hence their assumption of an otherwise exclusively human
attitude – indifference.)’6 (1980, 24). As humans in search of ourselves, we ﬁnd the
same restrictions experienced by animals in zoos. To look at animals is to be free of
ourselves, and of others. It is the same, I would argue, when the passers-by look at
the women and men working in the ruins. This is of course not to associate these
women and men with animals in cages, but to illustrate a threshold of indifference
and affectation existing between them, as when we look at animals. The connection
to be drawn is that both (animal and ruin worker) are on the margins (put there by
capture and destruction respectively). To engage in the passive spectatorship of the
zoo visitor and the onlooker in the photographs is to be at the margins of the spectacle.
You can only look, which is very different form knowing and experiencing.
Something different from the viewing of animals in zoos and of people in ruins
occurs where the immediacy of an event of war is captured by the equal immediacy
of photography. Berger cites Donald McCullin’s explicit and often disturbing photojournalistic coverage of the Vietnam War as an example of complex spectatorship
involving fascination, shock and terror.
As we look at them, the moment of the other’s suffering engulfs us. We are ﬁlled
with either despair or indignation. Despair takes on some of the other’s suffering
to no purpose. Indignation demands action. We try to emerge from the moment
of the photograph back into our lives. As we do so, the contrast is such that the
resumption of our lives appears to be a hopelessly inadequate response to what
we have just seen 
(1980, 38).
Looking at McCullin’s war photographs, Berger suggests that ‘the most literal
adjective that could be applied to them is arresting. We are seized by them’ (1980, 38).
Their overwhelmingly stark portrayal of the perpetrators and innocent victims of war
poses the question as to how to view and comprehend what is being shown. Berger
tells us that McCullin’s images show the fear in the eyes of the subjects and in our
own viewing of them: ‘They bring us up short.’ ‘In both cases, the issue of the war
which has caused that moment is effectively depoliticised. The picture becomes evidence
of the general human condition. It accuses nobody and everybody’ (1980, 40). Rather
than drawing a direct comparison between the pictures of people working in Berlin’s
ruins and McCullin’s stark depiction of the human toll of war (perhaps the best known
example is the Vietnamese girl running with no clothes on and covered in burns after
Ruins: capturing, forgetting
25

## S. 41

a napalm attack), the link I am making here is in the overall scene, where spectator
and ruin worker are indifferent to each other, each negating the other, each behaving
differently. The camera is passive: as much as the onlooker, it is how the user of the
camera looks and reacts that determines what image emerges. The onlooker peers at
the other from the vantage point of the street, the other group looks back from within
their ravaged environment. There are no bars separating these groups, yet both are
caged within the destruction of their environments and locked within the overall scene.
German playwright Berthold Brecht identiﬁed such separation in his dramaturgy
as the distancing effect (Verfremdungseffekt) deployed to make the audience identify
the meaning of the work rather than identifying with the actor. This gap (or alienation)
between message and actor would be ﬁlled by the informed spectator with an effective,
objective judgement of the play’s politico-socio-economic signiﬁcance. Brecht’s
message was simple: spectatorship of his plays necessarily involves interpretation
rather than entertainment. The message was to be activated by the experience of the
play and, on exiting the theatre, taken into the audience’s living and thinking. Brecht’s
work is intended not as a moral coding of how to construct and justify one’s sociopolitical beliefs, but to prompt a questioning of beliefs currently held and authorised.
Brecht’s alienation theory is part of how I look at the onlookers in the images of
ruins as they stop and gaze before moving on. In the photographs, fascination and
pleasure combine with the images of destruction and ruination.
Figure 9 Einsatz der Sogennten Ttummerhexe zur Bergung von Eisentragen Friedrichstrasse
Bezirk Mitte 1947, FRep.290–329189 Landesarchiv Berlin
Photo: Johann Willa
26
Ruins: capturing, forgetting

## S. 42

In the onlooker, these photographs elicit not empathy but a startled affectation
via the fascination of each image. In The Dominion of the Dead, Robert Pogue
Harrison argues: ‘The spectacle of ruins reveals the fact of destruction, yet at the
same time it also reveals the fact of survival – the survival not so much of the ruins
themselves as of the earth on which they stand or fall’ (2003, 15). The photographs
are evidence that Berlin was able to do both: to ‘stand’ again through its reconstruction
and to ‘fall’ through its burial at Teufelsberg. The onlookers display a separation
from the spectacle in what Rose Macaulay refers to as the Ruinenlust of Europe’s
cultural obsession with ruins during the 18th and 19th centuries. In The Pleasure of
Ruins, Macaulay asks what the pleasure of ruins tells us ‘of this strange human
reaction to decay’ (1953, 15). In her view, this pleasure is formulated through a
romantic attachment to the material decay of structures where nature takes over. She
explores the fascination with ruins including Egyptian, Roman and Greek historical
sites, the medieval period, and Enlightenment art by the likes of Poussin, Guardi and
Piranesi, as well as poetry, ruination landscapes and ruin reconstruction.7 Macaulay
points to the Roman world where ruins were derided as an outcome of war:
But the Roman world was to become a world of ruins. The barbarian tide swept
in like a ravening sea, breaking down the walls, leaving wreckage in its wake;
the cities, plundered of their stones and marble by citizens and foes, crumbled
away like wave-stormed cliffs. In the minds of Romans and barbarians the
gigantic ruins brooded, formidable unlaid ghosts; whether they liked it or not,
men became ruin-haunted, ruin-minded.
Macauley 1953, 9
Macaulay’s ‘ruin-haunted, ruin-minded’ men included the emperors, kings and
armies involved in the destruction of Troy and Babylon, the sackings of Rome, and
the ‘razing to the ground’ of Mycenae and Carthage. The destruction of these cities
in antiquity bears witness to the human capacity for destruction down the millennia,
right through to the world wars of the 20th century. Creating ruins was viewed as
evidence of destruction and the city, the ultimate sign of the grand human endeavour,
is also the deﬁnitive sign of war and destruction, as Macaulay explains: ‘But
Ruinenlust has come full circle: we have had our ﬁll. . . . Ruin must be fantasy, veiled
by the mind’s dark imaginings: in the objects that we see before us’ (1953, 455).
In Architecture and Disjunction, Bernard Tschumi suggests that the architecture
of pleasure is pleasing to the eye but that it also disturbs the senses.
The architecture of pleasure lies where the concept and experience of space
abruptly coincide, where architectural fragments collide and merge in delight,
where the culture of architecture is endlessly deconstructed and all rules are
transgressed. No metaphorical paradise here, but discomfort and the unbalancing
of expectations. Such architecture questions academic (and popular) assumptions, disturbs acquired tastes and fond architectural memories. Typologies,
morphologies, spatial compressions, logical constructions – all dissolve. Such
architecture is perverse because its real signiﬁcance lies outside utility of purpose
and ultimately is not even necessarily aimed at giving pleasure.8
Tschumi 1996, 92
Ruins: capturing, forgetting
27

## S. 43

## S. 44

Figure 11 Ennttrummerungsarbeiten an der Dreifaltigkeits-Kirche-Warnsignal für eine
