## S. 45

Tschumi is not talking about ruins per se, but the intention of ‘typologies,
morphologies, spatial compressions, logical constructions’ are part of dissolving archi -
tecture towards a de-structuralised ‘unbalancing of expectations’. One site where
architecture dissolves and morphs into a stage is Rome’s Trevi Fountain. As a piece
of architectural theatre, Nicola Salvi’s sculpture of rock and human form cascades
from the backdrop building, falling into ruination and ﬁnally ﬂowing as water. The
many millions of tourists who visit the site every year form part of the theatre of ruins.
As onlookers, they engage in the spectral dimensions of Salvi’s artiﬁce. Another ruin
artiﬁce can be seen in the surviving remains of Berlin’s Anhalter Bahnhof, where the
centre section of the entrance was left after the station’s demolition in 1960. The reverse,
the removal of the ruin artiﬁce to recreate a building out of the rubble, can be
experienced in Dresden’s Frauenkirche. Destroyed during the bombing of 13 and 14
February 1945, its ruin later became a stage for protest in East Germany; its
reconstruction, completed in 2005, sits at a half-way point between ruin and building,
failing at both. It is no longer a building but the staging of a ruin, and it is no longer
a ruin as it must resemble a building. As such, the Frauenkirche functions in a
plasticised relationship to time, building and ruin, sitting in a type of no-time where
past and present and future run simultaneously.
It is impossible to know how onlookers were affected by the ruination of Berlin
that forms part of the overall scene in each of the photographs. It can be surmised
Figure 12 Ennttrummerungsarbeiten an der Dreifaltigkeits-Kirche Mauer-Ecke
Mohrenstrasse Bezirk Mitte 1945 FRep.290–286654 Landesarchiv Berlin
Photo: Johann Willa
30
Ruins: capturing, forgetting

## S. 46

that their response was different to that of the men and women at work. The cathartic
clearing of the ruins is as much a clearing of the results of war from the sight of 
the people, as the rubble passes through hands and into the awaiting carriages of the
Trümmerbahn. In spite of the separation between onlookers and labourers, there is
the tie to the fascination or Ruinenlust in each of the photographs. Ruinenlust is
inseparable from the women and men working within the ruins of their city. One 
is the other – each is a ruining of the other. We see the content of the photographs
from the same position as the spectators in the photographs. Here we can begin to
see an emerging self-portrait of ruination, human and the city combined.
Self-ruining – portraits in the rubble
The beginning of this chapter outlined the relationship between hand and labour in
clearing rubble from the streets of Berlin. Women, men and at times even children
worked in the ruinscapes of their city that was in a constant state of disappearing.
Their physical and material connection to Berlin’s ruination formed their continuity
with the city through the removal of rubble. Evident in each photograph is the
inseparability of their labour within the ruins of their city, eliciting an attraction to
the overwhelming magnitude of destruction. In viewing the photographs, fascination
Figure 13 Ennttrummerungsarbeiten an der Dreifaltigkeits-Kirche Sprengung von Trummern
des in die Kirchenruine eingebauten Bunkers-Mauer-Ecke Mohrenstrasse Bezirk
Mitte 1945 FRep.290–286657 Landesarchiv Berlin
Photo: Johann Willa
Ruins: capturing, forgetting
31

## S. 47

and terror saturate each image from the margins of each group’s (labourer and
onlooker) participation in ruination. In Camera Lucida, Roland Barthes describes his
personal connection to photographs as a study of their meaning:
It is by studium that I am interested in so many photographs, whether I receive
them as political testimony or enjoy them as good historical scenes: for it is
culturally (this connotation is present in studium) that I participate in the ﬁgures,
the faces, the gestures, the settings, the actions.9
Barthes 1981, 26
Barthes’ use of the word studium (to study) conveys more than just looking; it is
the image in its entirety, its making, how it came into being. ‘To recognise the studium
is inevitably to encounter the photographer’s intentions’ (1981, 27). By contrast, in
reading the image in a photograph Barthes refers to the notion of punctuation: that
‘which will disturb the studium’ he deﬁnes as the punctum: ‘A photograph’s punctum
is that accident which pricks (but also bruises me, is poignant to me)’ (1981, 27).
Throughout Camera Lucida, Barthes reveals his attraction to a series of photographs,
allowing the reader to engage with his search for their meaning. Deﬁned via his
personal search in recovering from the death of his mother, Barthes speaks of the
prick or sting that occurs in some photographs. It is by the connection of studium
and punctum when viewing photographs that their reality is revealed to our inner
thoughts as viewers. To look at the archive photographs of the destruction of Berlin
(or any other city) is to be bruised by the image and what it presents and, at the
same time, fascinated by the scene; a self-reﬂection of its bruising.
Barthes’ reading of the images he engages with establishes his personal relationship
with them in terms of what they do to him. In Ways of Seeing, John Berger separates
looking from seeing by asserting: ‘The way we see things is affected by what we
know or what we believe’10 (1972, 8). Looking at something is not necessarily seeing
what it is, just as seeing something is not necessary looking at what one is seeing:
We never look at just one thing; we are always looking at the relationship
between things and ourselves. Our vision is continually active, continually moving,
continually holding things in a circle around itself, constituting what is present
to us as we are.
Berger 1972, 9
Seeing is to understand what lies before one’s immediate visual domain. Looking
is to ﬁnd, to scan the visual domain. When I look into the photographs of the women
and men working within the ruins of their city I see people engaged with both
destruction and reconstruction simultaneously – one is the end of the city and the
other its beginning. To return to Susan Sonntag: ‘Photographs alter and enlarge our
notions of what is worth looking at and what we have a right to observe’ (1973, 1).
In the immediate aftermath of World War II, chaos and atrocities raged throughout
Berlin where the destruction of the city also resulted in the destruction of the laws
governing society. The occupying forces on both sides of Berlin were, to varying
degrees, engaged in the victimisation of the city’s remaining population. As survivors
of mass destruction, some of the women in the photographs may also be victims of
violent assaults on their bodies. It is widely accepted that approximately 100,000
32
Ruins: capturing, forgetting

## S. 48

Figure 14 Trümmerfrauen; Hagelberger Strasse Kreuzberg, 19 April 1949, FRep.290(02)-
0184801, Landesarchiv Berlin
Photo: Durniok

## S. 49

Berlin women and girls between the ages of 12 and 50 were victims of rape (many
multiple) immediately after the fall of Berlin.11 Carried out predominately by Russian
soldiers and servicemen (although French, American and British servicemen were also
involved), the raping of Berlin’s women and girls remained largely unspoken between
women and within German society and the occupying forces. Although the Russian
authorities denied their servicemen’s involvement, they did argue that German
servicemen were involved in mass rape of Russian women and girls during the
German army’s invasion of Russia from 1941 until their retreat in 1944. The raping
of women on both sides was viewed as the spoils of war. Given the social stigma
attached to rape and given the fact that there was no one other than servicemen to
report it to, almost all went unreported. The numbers of women raped are partially
reﬂected in the surge of abortions (only ofﬁcial abortions were counted) and the related
suicides of approximately 10,000 women and girls12. Kept invisible on both sides,
the impact on Berlin’s female population in terms of physical and psychological
trauma, one can only begin to fathom the desperate and helpless situation the women
found themselves in. In the knowledge of these atrocities, the photographs of the
women working in the ruins of their city take on a double ruination – their violation
and the violence of destruction. In viewing the photographs, a more complex
relationship between ruins and personal trauma emerges.
In viewing the photographs, a self-portraiture of ruin and ruination emerges from
their silent visual compilations. My engagement with the photographs is through
viewing them as self-portraitures of destruction and as ruinscapes or tableaux vivant.
In Memoirs of the Blind – The Self-Portrait and Other Ruins, Jacques Derrida
conceives self-portraiture as self-ruining: ‘In the beginning there is ruin. Ruin is that
which happens to the image from the moment of the ﬁrst gaze. Ruin is the self-portrait,
this face looked at in the face of memory of itself, what remains or returns as a
spectre from the moment one ﬁrst looks at oneself and ﬁguration is eclipsed’13 (1993,
68). While Derrida detects self-ruining in the sublime judgement inherent in the act
of painting oneself, his concept is suggestive of a way to view the Trümmerfrauen
in situ: ‘for one can just as well read the pictures of ruins as the ﬁgures of a portrait,
indeed, of a self-portrait’ (1993, 68). Similar to Derrida’s reading of self-portraiture
as an act of self-ruining, the photographs are an inseparable portrait of the city, its
people and ruins; each a simultaneous capturing and reﬂecting of the other. The
photographs lay bare the ruination of the outer and inner body. Healing the body
and reconstructing the city perform the working through of ruination, city and self.
‘There is nothing of the totality that is not immediately opened, pierced, or bored
through: the mask of this impossible self-portrait whose signatory sees himself
disappearing before his own eyes the more he tries desperately to recapture himself
in it’ (1993, 69).
While Derrida detects self-ruining via judgement in the act of painting oneself, his
concept is suggestive of a way to view the Trümmerfrauen in the sites where they
appear. To read the photographs as self-portraits is not to read them as Derrida
conceived in self-portrait painting, but as a way to ﬁgure their portrayal in the
ruinscapes. ‘All symmetry is interrupted between him and himself, between him, the
spectacle, and the spectator who he also is’ (1993, 68). Derrida’s linking of selfportraiture to self-ruination at the time of painting oneself formulates his evaluation
in separating idealism of self and the subject of self as critique. In terms of looking
at the photographs, this raises the direct link between the self-ruining of the women
34
Ruins: capturing, forgetting

## S. 50

and men and the ruination of their city. Clearing and, discussed in Chapter 3, burying
the rubble from the streets of Berlin informs the connections between ruination and
human burial. Derrida offers a way of reading the photographs that goes some way
towards drawing out the connection between the self-portrait and ruination, human
and material:
It is like a ruin that does not come after the work but remains produced, already
from the origin, by the advent and structure of the work. In the beginning, at
the origin, there was ruin. At the origin comes ruin; ruin comes to the origin; it
is what ﬁrst comes and happens to the origin, in the beginning. With no promise
of restoration.
Derrida 1993, 65
To view the archive photographs is to understand them as the inseparable portrait
of the city, people and ruins, each a simultaneous reﬂection of the other. In Lessons
on the Analytical of the Sublime, Jean-François Lyotard posits a visual context for
the nature of reﬂection by declaring: ‘The “weakness” of reﬂection is what also
constitutes its “strength”‘14 (1994, 2). In my mind, the photographs also form a
tableaux vivant where each image of destruction, ‘fascinated by its own excessiveness’
builds towards a climatic image of ruination15 (1991, 55). In her essay The Great
Figure 15 Enttrummerung mit Unterstutzung der Alliierten Bezirk Charlottenburg
Wallstrasse (spater Zillerstrasse), 1947, FRep.290–09–03–88206
Photo: Landesarchiv Berlin
Ruins: capturing, forgetting
35

## S. 51

Frame-Up: Fantastic Appearances in Contemporary Spatial Politics, M. Christine
Boyer suggests: ‘In most cases invented traditions, like the tableaux vivant, arise
because the present is turbulent or in ﬂux, and the invented tradition or tableau seeks
to establish stability and order through continuity with the past’16 (1995, 95).
Associating the still-life pictures of the tableaux vivant with Berlin’s ruination is
relevant, not through its usual association with the spectacle of the theatrical event
but with what emerges over time from the photographs in the passages from destruc -
tion to recovery. Each photograph, I suggest, reveals Berlin’s move from destruction
to recovery, building towards the ﬁnal disappearance of the destroyed city and its
burial at Teufelsberg. Elsewhere, in her book The City of Collective Memory: Its
Historical Imagery and Architectural Entertainments, Boyer posits that ‘the
photograph participates in the spectral with its disturbing images from the past and
its ability to stop time’17 (1994, 297).
The inseparability of the women and men from the ruins of their city elicits, I
have suggested, both attraction and fascination within the ruinscape and the
magnitude of the task evident in each photograph. My attraction to the archive
photographs, their ‘pricking’ and ‘spectral’ capacity, is neither subjective, emotive or
reﬂectively held inside in each of the images. Writing on nature and physics, the Greek
philosopher Aristotle proposes that space is buried inside bodies. Ruination, I will
propose, also lies buried inside bodies; an interiorised space that swells and shrinks
according to events and experiences. In this context, space, it could be assumed, hides
the tragedy of the body by covering it. Yet such tragedy never leaves the body, it
remains written into the body and outside onto its surface:
If what is outside is inﬁnite it seems that body also is inﬁnite, and that there is
an inﬁnite number of worlds. Why should there be body in one part of the void
rather than in another? Grant only that mass is anywhere and it follows that it
must be everywhere. Also, if void and place are inﬁnite, there must be inﬁnite
body too; for in the case of eternal things what may be is.18
Aristotle Book III 1991, 41
Thinking through Aristotle’s conjecture of the inner and outer body-space is to
understand the salvaging of the city from ruination by the Trümmerfrauen as a double
portrait – as victims of war and again in the aftermath of war. Aristotle offers another
way in which this double portrait of ruination of the body can be thought, contained
and perhaps even disqualiﬁed:
. . . the inﬁnite body will obviously prevail over and annihilate the ﬁnite body.
On the other hand, it is impossible that each should be inﬁnite. Body is what
has extension in all directions and the inﬁnite is what is boundlessly extended,
so that the inﬁnite body would be extended in all directions ad inﬁnitum.
Aristotle Book III 1991, 42
The complexity of Derrida’s concept of self-ruining in self-portraiture and
Aristotle’s space buried inside bodies is experiential with time and self through
present and passing time. Considered in terms of self-ruining and Berlin’s ruination,
the Trümmerfrauen and men constitute a third space of ruination in Aristotle’s inside
body space. In the images of Berlin’s ruination, the women, men, children and
36
Ruins: capturing, forgetting

## S. 52

onlookers equate the later burial of their city with the burial of personal tragedy.
For Derrida, self-portraiture in painting is a self-induced traumatic act in the capturing
of oneself. Self-conscious as the self-portrait is, such trauma can be further highlighted
by Rembrandt’s self-portraits. In his earlier portraits, he shows himself as a gregarious
and virulent young male, successful and carefree in the company of his lovers. Positive
in their depiction of a life ahead, the paintings are ﬁlled with light and colour.
Rembrandt’s middle period portraits show a middle-aged and more sober man and
his wife. These paintings are composed and stolid, their gestural content subdued.
The later portraits show him reﬂective, a man who has aged and is aware of his
approaching death. They are dark paintings; his face lined and his eyes glazed. In
each picture Rembrandt looks out of the scene he depicts, a returning stare aimed
at the viewer.
Tying Rembrandt’s self-portraiture to the portraiture of the Trümmerfrauen and
men establishes the fragility I see in Derrida’s self-ruining concept. Working in the
rubble of the bombed-out city, the Trümmerfrauen and men portray self-ruining.
Their stages of working in the ruins may not bare the same resembling stages
Rembrandt depicts, but their ruination is nonetheless the ruination of the environment
(and image) in which they appear. Looking at the photographs, the self-portraiture
of the women and men renders city and self inseparable; each portrays the other.
This inseparableness can be taken further when the clearing of the rubble from Berlin’s
streets moves beyond the city to be buried at Teufelsberg. Realised as an inverse
rendering of the city, Teufelsberg becomes the self-portrait of Berlin where destruction
and burial are joined in a united self-portrait of the city and its people.
Ruins’ value – Speer’s romanticism
In the aftermath of the war, the scale of Berlin’s destruction was so incomprehensible
that time was metaphorically said to have ceased, leading to calls for a new beginning,
a restarting of the clock in what was referred to as Stunde Null (Zero Hour).19 Even
before the Allied bombing campaign, however, Berlin’s ruination had been envisaged
through an urban plan that would symbolise the architectural aesthetics of the Third
Reich. The collaboration between Adolf Hitler and the regime’s architect-in-chief
Albert Speer imagined a new vision for the city composed of vast boulevards and
colossal buildings constructed in stone. Informed by his Ruin Value Theory
(Ruinenwerttheorie), Speer’s concept was to build into the planning and design of
buildings an orchestrated time of material decay that would see Berlin pass into ruins
during the thousand years of the Reich.
The idea was that buildings of modern construction were poorly suited to form
that ‘bridge of tradition’ to future generations which Hitler was calling for. It
was hard to imagine that rusting heaps of rubble could communicate the heroic
inspirations which Hitler admired in the monuments of the past. My ‘theory’
was intended to deal with this dilemma. By using special materials and by
applying certain principles of statics, we should be able to build structures, which
even in a state of decay, after hundreds or (such were our reckonings) thousands
of years would more or less resemble Roman models.20
Speer 1969, 56
Ruins: capturing, forgetting
37

## S. 53

Speer’s grandiose concept for Berlin as a future ruin, which aimed to rival the
urban plan and buildings of, for example, the Roman and Athenian Forums, emerged
at the epicentre of National Socialism’s built ideology at the Reichsparteitagsgelände
(Nazi Party Rally Grounds) in Nuremberg:21
To illustrate my ideas, I had a romantic drawing prepared. It showed what the
reviewing stand on the Zeppelin ﬁeld would look like after generations of neglect,
over-grown with ivy, its columns fallen, the walls crumbling here and there, but
the outlines still clearly recognizable.
Speer 1969, 56
In architectural terms, Speer’s aesthetic theory of ruins is not solely dependent on
the romantic ideal per se but on practical design solutions to manage, over time, the
slow decay of his buildings. Speer’s ruination dream for Berlin may have been inspired
by experiencing ruins ﬁrst-hand or by viewing artworks like Giovanni Battista
Piranesi’s evocative sketches, including his aestheticised and phantasmagorical
portrayal of Roman ruins, such as his Appian Way etching (1756) of decaying
monuments, temples and arches overgrown with vegetation, an image that reimagines
Rome’s glorious past. Speer’s vision for a supreme alter ego accumulating in archi -
tectures of mortality forms the legacy Hitler and Speer desired for Berlin as a future
ruin of a glorious past. To realise Berlin’s future ruination, carved from the archi -
tectural legacies of Athens and Rome, also meant that buildings would need to be
‘pure’ in their construction to achieve the desired material decay. Buildings would
be designed without reinforced concrete or steel supports, enabling them to aesthet -
ically crumble over time into formations symbolising not the fall of the Nazi regime,
but its ongoing presence, as Speer writes:
What had remained of the emperors of Rome? What would still bear witness to
them today, if their buildings had not survived? Periods of weakness are bound
to occur in the history of nations, he argued; but at their lowest ebb, their
architecture will speak to them of former power. Naturally, a new national
consciousness could not be awakened by architecture alone. But when after a
long spell of inertia a sense of national grandeur was born anew, the monuments
of men’s ancestors were the most impressive exhortations.
Speer 1969, 55
Speer embarked on his grand project for Berlin early in 1939, immediately before
the start of World War II. A number of streets were cleared of buildings in central
Berlin so that construction could commence. Speer’s vision necessitated a change in
the city’s layout from its existing east–west axis to a north–south axis, making way
for grand monuments of the Third Reich to rival those of ancient Rome and Athens
and in the modern world, cities such as Paris with its Avenue des Champs-Élysées
and Haussmann Boulevard.
Sighting through the 260-foot opening of the great arch, the arriving traveller
would see at the end of a 3-mile vista the street’s second great triumphal structure
rearing out of the haze of the metropolis: the great hall with its enormous dome.
Speer 1969, 135
38
Ruins: capturing, forgetting

## S. 54

In The Anatomy of Human Destructiveness, Eric Fromm describes the difference
between Hitler the ‘necrophilous destroyer’ and Speer the ‘biophilous builder’.22 This
distinction tells of a relationship fraught with difference yet compatible in the way
each man obtained and retained his role in the vision of Germania:
Hitler’s passion to destroy buildings and cities deserves particular attention
because of its connection with his passion for building. One might even go so
far as to say that his plans to rebuild cities were an excuse for ﬁrst destroying
them.
Fromm 1973, 397
According to Speer, Hitler’s order to destroy Paris as the German army was pulling
out of the city as the Allies approached and his order in September of 1944 to turn
the German landscape into a ‘scorched earth’,23 turning the country into a barren
landscape void of any provision of sustenance for the approaching Allies and Russians,
‘opened up an abyss’ between himself and Hitler (Fromm 1973, 397). Neither of
these orders was carried out as, Fromm asserts, Speer actively usurped Hitler’s orders
behind the scenes.
Even as the devastating Allied bombing raids increased in their intensity towards
the war’s end, reducing much of Berlin to rubble, Hitler preferred to see such
destruction as aiding his and Speer’s new vision for Berlin. Within just three years
(1943–1945), Speer’s ruin theory would reach its zenith, albeit in reverse, as a result
of the near total destruction of Berlin. In his essay An Architecture of Desire,
architectural theorist and critic Léon Krier defends Speer’s ruin theory by arguing
for a rethinking not of National Socialism’s built ideology but of Speer’s use of
classical forms:
Speer’s theory of the value of ruins has been derided as still more proof of his
backward-looking mindset. Yet beyond its anecdotal interest, it reveals an acute
consciousness of the material and tectonic integrity intrinsic to traditional building
in general and monumental architecture in particular.24
Krier 2013, 18
For many of Speer’s detractors, who saw his ideas as blithely egocentric, the
classical principles that he employed are evidence of his reliance on the ideologies of
National Socialism, ‘yet another proof of his pathological megalomania’ (2013, 223).
Given Krier’s justiﬁcation of Speer’s formalist architecture, he makes a caustic
summary as to the remains of this architecture that ﬁnd their way to Teufelsberg:
. . . what could have well been the reason which compelled the authorities of 
a devastated capital to pile up the rubble of its ruins 10 kms away from the
center, and to bury under this Hill of the devil (Teufelsberg) [may well be] one
of the rare monumental building complexes actually realized during the Third
Reich?
Krier 2013, 218
As Krier points out, Teufelsberg marks the sublime antithesis of Speer’s urban
scheme, but one where monumentality and complexity still reign. Under the millions
Ruins: capturing, forgetting
39

## S. 55

of tons of rubble lies Speer’s uncompleted Wehrtechnische Fakultät (Military Technical
Faculty) built in stone. A number of attempts by the Allies to blow up the building
failed due to the exceptional technologies employed in its construction and, as a result,
the vast building lies intact, buried with the remnant city of Berlin.
Berlin’s post-war reconstruction saw Speer’s plan implemented, not through 
grand architectural schemes to rival Athens and Rome, but through their now 
shared geography of seven hills. Smaller in scale than Teufelsberg, Berlin’s seven
Trümmerberge (rubble hills) located within the city’s inner urban environments
perform ruination of the buried city similarly to Teufelsberg and as walk-up public
parks. Berlin thus ceased to be ﬂat, becoming a city with an undulating geography
of hills and elevated views. But although Teufelsberg and the seven Trümmerberge
are presences within and outside of Berlin, they remain mostly absent, unknown and
forgotten constructions of the buried city. Teufelsberg and the Trümmerberge hide
the city’s burial, yet create its appearance as geography.
Speer’s designed ruination in the revered material of stone had been transformed
into the cracked edges of concrete and brick that ﬁll Teufelsberg and the Trümmer -
berge. Speer’s measured disintegration for a long-term architectural legacy of the Third
Reich prompts the thinking of architecture’s continuity and preservation. In The
Aesthetics of Decay: Nothingness, Nostalgia, and the Absence of Reason, Dylan Trigg
suggests:
Figure 16 Teufelsberg Schuttkippe an der Teufelsseechaissee im hintergrund Ruine der
Wehrtechnischen Fakultät 11.12.1951, F Rep.290–16083
Photo: Landesarchiv Berlin
40
Ruins: capturing, forgetting

## S. 56

Aestheticizing ruins without being heedful of their eventual dissolution means
aspiring to own them either through preservation or by dint of abstracting them
from their context. Such a beautiﬁcation of the ruin succumbs to a wholly
romantic perspective. Instead of opening the space in which rational progress is
contested, the static identiﬁcation of ruins, through rendering them novelties,
implicates a detachment whereby the ruin’s powers are diluted. Mere delight
means truncating the ruin’s dissolution.25
Trigg 2006, xxvi
Trigg’s ‘truncating the ruin’s dissolution’ is what Speer intended for his architecture
in the beginning of the design process – designing backwards from grave to standing
building. Although hidden in Teufelsberg within a buried Berlin, Speer’s Military
Faculty forms such as grave and, in the sublime association, the legacy of Speer’s
ruination theory. It is worth contemplating whether Speer ever visited Teufelsberg,
walked its paths in search of a protrusion of his building breaking through the surface
from the millions of tons of rubble. In Don Delillo’s novel White Noise (1986),26 the
main character is Jack Gladney, professor of Hitler Studies, a department he founded
at an imaginary university called the College on the Hill. In the book, Delillo creates
a conversation with a colleague, a Professor Murray Jay Siskind of the American
Environments Department, who tells Jack of Speer’s ruin theory, becoming more
historically twisted as the conversation ensues:
I told Murray that Albert Speer wanted to build structures that would decay
gloriously, impressively, like Roman ruins. No rusty hulks or gnarled steel slums.
He knew that Hitler would be in favor of anything that might astonish posterity.
He did a drawing of a Reich structure that was to be built of special materials,
allowing it to crumble romantically – a drawing of fallen walls, half columns
furled in wisteria. The ruin is built into the creation, I said, which shows a certain
nostalgia behind the power principle, or a tendency to organise the longings of
future generations. Murray said, ‘I don’t trust anybody’s nostalgia but my own.
Nostalgia is a product of dissatisfaction and rage. It’s a settling of grievances
between the present and the past. The more powerful the nostalgia, the closer
you come to violence. War is the form nostalgia takes when men are hard-pressed
to say something good about their country’.
Delillo 1984, 34
At the Nuremberg Trails, held to convict the remaining senior Nazis of war crimes,
Speer was saved from execution and instead given a twenty-year sentence mostly due
to his latter role in the war as Minister of Armaments and War Production
(1942–1945). Speer served his time in Berlin’s Spandau Prison completely isolated
from fellow prisoners, including fellow Nazi and former Reich Deputy Rudolph
Hess.27 ‘Speer entered the echoing cofﬁn of Spandau as a man of 42, at the height
of his powers, and after a meteoric career. He was released at sixty-one. But during
his captivity he wrote: more than 20,000 sheets of diary notes, authorised and
clandestine letters, scraps of autobiography. He wrote on calendar leaves, cardboard
lids, toilet paper (the traditional papyrus of the prisoner). And he succeeded in
smuggling this prodigious hoard out of Spandau despite the vigilant scrutiny of
American, Russian, British and French guards’28 (Steiner 2009, 70). Speer spent his
Ruins: capturing, forgetting
41

## S. 57

days reading and creating a garden in one of the prison’s outdoor areas into a green
oasis that became known as his ‘Garden of Eden’. Ofﬁcially denied writing paper,
Speer managed to write parts of his autobiography Inside the Third Reich by
smuggling out much of its contents via a prison orderly during his incarceration.
Towards the end of his prison sentence Speer was one of a few prisoners remaining
at Spandau (his prison number was 5). On his release in 1966 after serving exactly
twenty years, he spent the next ﬁfteen years until his death in 1981 writing his memoirs,
including Spandau: The Secret Diaries (1976). It was Inside the Third Reich published
in (1969) that made Speer a prominent biographer of the Nazi period. The book gives
a ﬁrst-hand account of Hitler and other leading Nazi personalities, became an
international best seller and was followed by further books on the Nazi era such as
his account of SS Commander Heinrich Himmler. There is a wonderful image of Speer
long after his release from Spandau prison sitting at his desk in his study in front of
a pile of his smuggled letters and notes. In my mind, this image of Speer with his pile
of notes connects metaphorically to the pile of rubble at Teufelsberg. Scufﬂing through
his papers and reaching the bottom he might come across his unrealised ruination
theory and his plans for the Military Faculty buried at Teufelsberg. The time of Speer’s
incarceration is approximately equal to the time of Teufelsberg’s construction and
ﬁnal completion (1946–1971). Perhaps over time Teufelsberg will become the site of
antiquity that Hitler and Speer desired for Berlin, calling for its excavation by future
archaeologists.
The attraction to ruins that prompted Speer to build Ruinenlust into his archi -
tecture also provided the grounds for the survival of his Military Faculty. Similar 
to Vespasian’s burial of Nero’s Domus Aurea (Golden House) in Rome in AD 68
after Nero’s suicide, Speer’s Military Faculty shares the death of Hitler’s Thousand
Year Reich. Speer’s legacy can still be seen at the Nazi Party Rally Ground
(Reichsparteitagsgelände) in Nuremberg, where his vision of ruination is partly
realised. In 1966 a young German ﬁlmmaker captured this vision through a rendering
of the brutality and attraction of the Reich’s ideological remnant.
Brutality in ruins – Kluge’s Nuremberg
The German ﬁlmmaker, writer and television producer Alexander Kluge’s 1961 ﬁlm
Brutalität in Stein (Brutality In Stone)29 begins not unlike a silent ﬁlm with the preface:
Every building bequeathed us by history testiﬁes to the spirit of its builders and
of their time, even if it long ago stopped serving its original purpose. As witnesses
preserved in stone, the surviving monuments of the National Sodalist Party revive
the memory of the epoch that culminated in the most terrible catastrophe in
German history.30
Wenzel 2012, 182
Brutalität in Stein, Kluge’s ﬁrst ﬁlm in collaboration with Peter Schamoni, Wolf
Wirth and Dieter Lemmel, opens with an architectural model of one of the many
monumental designs Speer’s ofﬁce was to produce between 1933 and 1943. The visual
imagery contained in the ﬁlm is interspersed with the voice of Adolf Hitler addressing
tens of thousands of cheering SS soldiers, party members and the public during one
of the Nuremberg rallies. The ﬁlm is a startling presentation of Speer’s architecture
42
Ruins: capturing, forgetting

## S. 58

at the epicentre of Nazi propaganda and ideology. The ﬁlm’s score moves through
the raw sound of clashing cymbals and military drumming to a performance by the
Reich Symphony Orchestra of a Brahms concerto, musically framing the character
and aspiration of Speer’s stone ediﬁces. The ﬁlm shows images of Adolf Hitler
reviewing models of his own and Speer’s urban dream for the Thousand Year Reich
in aggrandising monuments, boulevards and plazas. Kluge’s sequencing of Speer’s
Nuremberg architecture oscillates between interrogative close-ups and formulaic
horizontal and vertical shots of edges and converging lines, drawing the viewer into
Speer’s ideological and architectural aesthetics while determining the ﬁlm’s visual
narrative and meaning.
Doubtless there are argumentative structures in Brutalität in Stein that refer to
particular facts. Hitler’s dictum (‘Only the pettiest of minds can see the life of a
revolution in destruction alone. We saw it, on the contrary, in a gigantic effort of
construction.’) is played once at the beginning to images of phallic tower blocks and
later, once again, in connection with shots of Hitler’s ‘idea sketches’. The ﬁlm’s
conclusion provides tacit commentary on what resulted from Hitler’s ‘gigantic effort
of construction’: a ﬁeld of rubble (Wenzel 2012, 183).
Given the ﬁlm’s opening statement, it remains ambivalent with regard to the
ideology imbued in the architecture of the Nuremberg Rally Grounds. In his essay
Construction Site Film: Kluge’s Idea of Realism and His Short Films, Eike Friedrich
Wenzel writes:
Schamoni and Kluge are seeking a site in the present which – as the authorial
presupposition would have it – preserves signs of the catastrophe that befell
Germany in the recent past. Contrary to the expectation formulated in the
opening thesis, however, the authors fail to develop a discursive analysis. Not a
word is devoted to characterizing the buildings’ architectural style, describing
their construction, and so forth. The camera seems interested, above all, in the
buildings’ corners and edges and in their symmetrical uniformity.
Wenzel 2012, 182
As ﬁlmmakers, Kluge and Schamoni are no doubt relying on the imagery they
have captured and composed for the ﬁlm to show Speer’s Nuremberg architecture
as it is to convey their ideas: vacant, crumbling and overgrown with weeds, the
decaying remnants of National Socialism. The ‘brutality’ in Kluge’s ﬁlm is the
formation and spatial assembly of buildings capable of expressing beauty, desire and
aspiration through a highly controlled architectural aesthetic. Speer’s manipulation
of the architectural programme in design and in the assembly of materials is nothing
new in the histories of political and ideological built form for societal representation.
Given the ﬁlmmakers’ opening statement, Wenzel points towards a failure to analyse
Speer’s architecture, leaving the meaning of the ﬁlm open. ‘Although Schamoni and
Kluge had indicated at the outset that Nazi architecture was to be decoded as the
ideological formation or mirror of the fascist worldview, this programme remains
unredeemed over the course of the ﬁlm. The authors do not explain; they arrange’
(2012, 183). In my opinion, this is precisely what the ﬁlmmakers should do. Kluge’s
visual assembly employs a straightforward ﬁlmic technique that allows viewers to
decipher their own context and meaning for architecture deeply entrenched in ideology
– what its physical remains meant at the time of the ﬁlm’s making 16 years after the
Ruins: capturing, forgetting
43

## S. 59

end of World War II, and 55 years later its meaning and message now. Wenzel’s
critique of the ﬁlm continues:
The authors bring materials into contact with each other, but they remain
‘foreign’, i.e. unelaborated in the current context. In Brutalität in Stein, a
fundamental semantic difference prevails between image and sound, and this
disjunction is what allows temporal relations and differing levels of reality to be
articulated. Present and past, vision and reality are contrapuntally interwoven
by Schamoni and Kluge. And the viewer recognises National Socialism as a
phenomenon that juts into his own lived reality. Documentary integrity prevents
one from saying in what way.
Wenzel 2012, 184
Kluge’s visual and audio critique sets in motion a juxtaposition of Hitler’s
architectural ideal with the sounds of air raids and bombing that destroyed any
prospect of such a vision emerging. Cyclic in form, the ﬁlm returns to the Nurem -
berg Rally Grounds as the site and vestige of Speer and Hitler’s architectural
phantasmagorical dreamscape.
Brutalität in Stein occupies an in-between space of artistic expression, neither
qualifying nor clarifying Speer’s architectural vision. Instead the ﬁlm offers a menacing
Figure 17 Zeppelin Field Nuremberg, July 2016
Photo: Author
44
Ruins: capturing, forgetting

## S. 60

irritation towards its viewing. The ﬁlm is a detailed examination of whether an
assembly of stone imbued with an aesthetics of line and form can wholly convey the
principles of an architectural ideology. Here Speer’s study of classical form in Greek
and Roman architecture is paramount, ancient societies that successfully conveyed
their cultural and authoritarian ideologies in stone. Speer’s architecture for the
Nuremberg Rally Grounds performs the ideology of the Nazi Party as scenography;
Hitler the star actor, soldiers and the public as audience, and a visual interplay between
the architecture of stone and the atmosphere of Speer’s ephemeral architecture as
light in piercing shafts; his cathedral of light ringing the entire scene.
Kluge reveals this epic theatre as a relic. The brutality is not the stone but its
assembly that elicits both fascination and terror. The architectural aesthetic of the
Thousand Year Reich was aimed at creating the Reich’s capital Berlin as the third
cornerstone in the classical ordering of modern civilisation, following Athens and
Rome. But as we shall see, the theory was to be realised not through urban design
and architecture but through topography and landscape. Speer’s vision was to unfold
in Berlin’s post-war reconstruction, succeeding as geography to mirror the geographies
of Athens and Rome and their shared seven hills.
Seven ruins – Athens, Rome, Berlin
Claims of a sublime realisation of Speer and Hitler’s plan for Berlin to mirror Athens
and Rome can be supported with reference to their shared topography and
cartographic location of ruins within each city. Differences will become obvious,
however. Excluding Teufelsberg, which has the non-operational but still visible
American surveillance listening post on its summit, and Der Insulaner (The Islander)
with the Wilhelm Foerster Observatory, Berlin’s rubble hills are public parks. The
seven hills of Athens and Rome, on the other hand, are striatal; they contain buildings
and the layers of buildings as ruins formed over time. The relationships between the
natural geography of Athens and Rome and the constructed hills of Berlin can be
metaphorically connected through an inverse mirroring of their above- and belowground architectural histories. The hills of Athens and Rome are sites that contain
buildings of power, wealth and public assembly, whereas the rubble hills of Berlin
contain buildings unknown. The hills of Berlin, Athens and Rome can be further
associated in terms of architecture and ideology, offering another tangible layer to
the argument.
To compare the hills of the three cities, similarities and differences can be deﬁned
by noting their names and sites within each city. Besides Teufelsberg, which lies on
the outskirts of the city, Berlin’s seven inner suburban hills are: Humboldthain,
Volkspark Friedrichshain, Tempelhofer Marienhöhe, Oderbruchkippe, Dörferblick,
Der Insulaner and Rixdorfer Höhe.31 In each naming of the Trümmerberge there are
distinctions as to how each hill is describe in terms of landscape. For example, ‘Hain’
translates as grove whereas ‘Höhe’ is elevation. Furthermore, ‘Kippe’ translates as
dump, ‘Blick’ as view and ‘Volks’ as people’s. Each hill is constructed as a walk-up
park that allows the public to walk the hill and view their city from various vantage
points. As inner city public spaces, each hill contains parts of the buried city of Berlin.
Collectively, they are extended gardens with grass, trees and shrubs. Each of the
Trümmerberge is a carefully crafted landscape with its own spatial and natural
character offering rest and solitude within the urban plan of the city.
Ruins: capturing, forgetting
45

## S. 61

Vastly different to Berlin’s seven Trümmerberge, Athens’ seven hills are steeped
in a rich architectural and cultural history that has developed over millennia. The
seven hills are: Acropolis, Areopagus, Philopappus Hill (or Hill of the Muses), Hill
of the Nymphs, Pnyx, Mount Lycabettus and Mount Anchesmos (or Tourkovounia).
There are also two minor hills, the Skouze and Streﬁ, both named after prominent
ancient Athenian families. The name Acropolis is formed from two words: acros
meaning highest point and polis meaning city, which indicates a citadel, built on
elevated ground or hill; most notably, the Parthenon and adjoining temples are
located on the Acropolis hill. Philopappus Hill takes its name from the grandson of
the Greek King Antiochus IV where, upon his death in AD 116 in Rome, a monument
was erected on the Mouseion Hill and renamed Philopappus Hill. Areopagus hill, a
massive rock, is likewise formed from two words: ares is the Greek god of war and
pagus means terroir or land. Hill of the Nymphs takes its meaning from young female
deities of nature who were associated with the various landmarks where they resided;
forests, waterfalls, lakes, etc. Mount Lycabettus, the highest hill in Athens at 300
metres, was, according to folklore, where wolves (lycos) roamed. Anchesmos, now
known as Tourkovounia meaning Turkish Mountain, takes its meaning from a
nearby Turkish cemetery dating from the Ottoman Empire’s occupation of Greece
from the mid-1400s until Greece’s war of independence in 1821 and the forming of
the Greek state in 1832.
The third city in which to explore geographical and historical associations with
Berlin’s Trümmerberge is Rome. In alphabetical order, the Seven Hills of Rome are:
Aventine Hill, Caelian Hill, Capitoline Hill, Esquiline Hill, Palatine Hill, Quirinal
Hill and Viminal Hill. The names of the seven hills relate either to the city (polis) or
are derived from the Roman tradition of nomenclature to combine places and sites
with names. The naming of the Aventine Hill can be traced to an early Roman tribal
ruler King Aventinus who reigned from 854–817 BC. The Caelian Hill stems from
the word caelum meaning heaven, and also from the name of a prominent wealthy
Roman family who built its villa on the hill. The Capitoline Hill was the ancient
citadel of early Roman settlement and translates as the highest point, the word
Capitale later gaining recognition as the place of governance. The naming of the
Esquiline Hill has its origins in an oak forest that once covered the hill, and also
from an early Roman settlement when the Esquiline Hill lay outside the city’s
boundaries. The Esquiline Hill also contains the buried remains of Nero’s Domus
Aurea (Golden House) that once covered 200 acres (80 hectares) of central Rome.
The Palatine Hill takes its name from palace but also palate which, given the 
hill’s height, assumes the site of a metaphorical plateau to the sky. The naming 
of the Quirinal Hill originates from the Roman period; it contains the Quirinal 
Palace completed in 1583 that is now the ofﬁcial residence of the Italian President.
Likewise, the naming of the Viminal Hill originates from the Roman period and
contains the Palace of Viminale completed in 1911 that is now home to the Ministry
of the Interior.
The tour guide Hills of Philopappos – Pnyx – Nymphs, published by the Friends
of the Acropolis, gives an account of the history of the Athenian hills from their ﬁrst
recorded inhabitation in 2800–2100 BC to the present.32 Referring to the Melite Deme
(a Deme is an entitled area of land inhabited by a tribe that functions in a microstate capacity of the polis), the guidebook describes the area of one such microstate
belonging to the ‘Kekropis tribe on the slope of the Pnyx Hill’:
46
Ruins: capturing, forgetting

## S. 62

Essentially unexplored and covered by greenery, this part enables the visitor to
observe on the rock-cut surface the ground-plan of the houses (with corridors,
atria, andirons, staircases and reservoirs), the small paths and squares, the sacred
niches and the tombs of the cemetery. This is an open-book of city-planning, one
of the most important demes in antiquity that along with the Kolie covered the
rocky slopes of the west hills. The landscape itself became the driving force behind
the architectural planning.33
The hills of the Muses, Pnyx and Nymphs changed over time from dense sites of
inhabitation to being mostly abandoned and turned over to agricultural use during
the Middle Ages. Eighteenth- and 19th-century sketches and paintings show the hills
as barren hilltops with the exception of the Observatory (Asteroskopeion) built on
the hill of the Nymphs between 1842–1846. The transformation over time of the
hills of Athens can also be compared to the history of the hills of Rome that went
through centuries of depopulation and reuse as sites for the grazing of animals. An
example of Rome’s ﬂuctuating and fragile urban history can be seen in the Vedute
di Roma (Views of Rome), a series of etchings made by Giovanni Battista Piranesi
in 1760. Though not sited on a hill and referred to as ‘the cow pasture’, Piranesi’s
Vedute di Campo Vaccino is depicted as a ruin landscape of crumbling triumphal
arches, columns and temples.34
A comparative study of the natural geography of Rome and Athens and the
human-made geography of Berlin may ﬁnd that the building, expansion, destruction
and abandonment of Rome and Athens over millennia is also Berlin’s history.
Although on a vastly different historical timespan, Berlin is comparable in terms of
its massive building and population expansion in the late 19th and early 20th
centuries, through to its destruction during World War II and reconstruction up to
the present. As we have already seen, the Hitler/Speer urban plan for Berlin’s
thousand-year ruination was aimed at mirroring the glorious histories of Athens 
and Rome through managed ruination. Their plan, if realised, would have eclipsed
Athens and Rome but, as we now know, has resulted in mirrored geographies. The
iconic landmarks and ruins that ﬁll Athens and Rome’s hills do not ﬁll Berlin’s
Trümmerberge, yet their building is a ruins-construction.
Conclusion
In this chapter I have worked through various representations of ruins: Rubblescape
Ruinenlust, Self-Ruining, Ruin Value, Brutality of Ruins and Seven Ruins as the
starting point to the buried city. To look at the archive photographs of ruins and
people working in ruins is to view the relationships between human and material
ruination to derive the fascination and pleasure identiﬁed by Rose Macaulay in the
romantic and subliminal viewing of ruins. I have attempted to understand the
destruction of the city in each photograph; seeking the punctum and studium described
by Roland Barthes so as to ﬁnd a deeper way to grasp what is taking place. As John
Berger claims, looking and seeing are two different experiences, bringing our sight
into the ﬁeld of the image and the ﬁeld of the image into view. Looking and seeing
expose different attitudes to what affects us, as seen in the way McCullin’s capturing
of war and its victims tells as much about ourselves as about what the photographs
show. I have tried not to describe what I see, but to offer observations or ways of
Ruins: capturing, forgetting
47

## S. 63

seeing. To stay with each image for a period of time, to peer into its ﬁeld is, as Sonntag
points out, to peer into the photographer’s vision. The onlookers in the photographs,
staring at the ruins of their city, place themselves outside, detached from their
ruination. Looking at them we place them back into the ruins and conversely
ourselves. In the self-portrait, capturing oneself means engaging in a constant ruination
of the self. The ruination of the self can never be deﬁnitive of the subject or complete
in the viewing of oneself. Speer and Hitler’s spectral vision for the Thousand Year
Reich in creating a built ideology to visualise it, the testimonial of which ﬁlls Kluge’s
Brutalität in Stein. The reality of Berlin reaching the desired ruination, as geography
to rival Athens and Rome, represents how ideologies construct the self-portrait.
The immense task undertaken by the hand and the labour of the body working in
the rubble of the destroyed city presents an inseparable relationship – one is the other.
This ﬂesh and stone that is worked through the material weight of buildings brings
into conﬂict the fragility of body and city and determination in rescuing the city from
destruction. The next chapter looks at how the silence that permeated post-war
literature concerning the horrors of the Allied air war on German towns and cities
led to what W.G. Sebald called self-anaesthesia. This self-imposed amnesiac silence
and self-enforced forgetting of Germany’s civil population suffering during the Allied
air war was seen in light of a nation responsible for untold atrocities and the murder
of millions. The amnesia that cloaked German post-war society and literature
developed the cultural forgetting that would the later lead to the burial of their city.

Ruins: capturing, forgetting

## S. 66

2
Memoirs
Self-anaesthesia, cultural
forgetting
We have not only forgotten what it is to remember – and what remembering is – but
we have forgotten our own forgetting.
Edward Casey, Remembering: A Phenomenological Study, 1987, 2
In Remembering: A Phenomenological Study, Edward Casey states that forgetting
was a substantial focus in the works of Nietzsche, Heidegger and Freud: ‘Nietzsche
stressed the virtues of “active forgetfulness”, that is, the capacity to forget not merely
by lapsus but wilfully and for a purpose – so as to erase, or at least to cover over,
the scars which repeated remembering would only turn back into open wounds’1
(1987, 7). In Heidegger’s seminal Being and Time, ‘forgetfulness, even in its ordinary
forms is interpreted as more primordial than remembering’. ‘In the “leaping-away”
of the Present, one also forgets increasingly’ (1987, 8). And on Freud, he writes: ‘The
aim of psychoanalysis became, accordingly, to “ﬁll in the gaps in memory, to undo
the baneful, pathogenic effects of forgetting wherever this is possible”‘ (1987, 8).
Casey suggests a review of established philosophical enquiries into forgetting towards
a rethinking of remembering: ‘We have not only forgotten what it is to remember –
and what remembering is – but we have forgotten our own forgetting’ (1987, 2).
In his introduction to Social Amnesia – A critique of Contemporary Psychology
from Adler to Laing, Russell Jacoby refers to Theodor Adorno’s proposition that
‘the history of psychology is the history of forgetting’.2 In Jacoby’s view, amnesia
can be understood in psychological terms and via the spatial appearance of objects:
how things appear, the forms they take, the information they hold.
[w]hat is often ignored in expositions of the concept of reiﬁcation is the
psychological dimension: amnesia – a forgetting and repression of the human
and social activity that makes and can remake society. The social loss of memory
is a type of reiﬁcation – better: it is the primal form of reiﬁcation.
Jacoby 1975, 4
Mindful of Casey’s call for a review of remembering and Jacoby’s concept of social
amnesia as ‘society’s repression of remembrance – society’s own past’, this chapter
examines cultural forgetting in the ﬁrst few years of post-war German society
(1975, 5). This leads to an understanding of how burial challenges remembering, and
how a civilian population traumatised by the destruction of its cities and by the
overwhelming weight of German/Nazi atrocities could enter a psychological state of
withdrawing from or avoiding the past, manifested as social amnesia via selective
forgetting.

## S. 67

Amnesia is maintained when the things that originally triggered the memory loss
(traumatic events, emotional scarring, incomprehensible actions) are not touched on.
Attempting to overcome amnesia means recalling actions and experiences to prompt
remembering. As we discovered with Berger’s dualism of seeing and looking being
engaged with different perceptions in the use of sight, and Barthes’ punctum and
studium as different sight experiences, Casey’s binary that remembering and forgetting
are self-managed, and Jacoby’s social amnesia, impacts on recollection and conscious -
ness can lead individuals, communities and nations to self-repressed forgetting. 
In this way, social constructions of amnesia permit a stripping out of unwanted
experiences, histories and events that bruise the collective conscious. A society can
be stricken by states of amnesia; they can be synthetically cultural and artiﬁcially
political, which allows for actual state-managed forgetting. By foiling active memory,
such forgetting allows for the ‘wilful repression of things we already knew’ (Jacoby
1975, 15). In his essay The Neuro-Psychoses of Defence, Freud states that wilful
forgetting is retained in the mind as it is in the body.3 Freud’s psychological analysis
of extreme ruptured memory, for example, was based in part on his work with
traumatised and shell-shocked soldiers returning from the trenches of World War I.
Given that, in most cases, the experiences were generated within similar devastating
environments, no two experiences were alike:
I cannot, of course, maintain that an effort of will to thrust things of this kind
out of one’s thoughts is a pathological act; nor do I know whether and in what
way intentional forgetting succeeds in those people who, under the same psychical
inﬂuences, remain healthy.
Freud 1962, 48
Freud went on to develop the psychological concept of the uncanny – a psychospatial experience of the familiar and unfamiliar. One example he drew on was ‘nomans land’, the strip of ground separating enemies at the front that was both familiar,
as it could be described by the soldiers, and unfamiliar, since only the dead could
permanently occupy this space.
In Seven Types of Forgetting, Paul Connerton lists various pervasive forms of
forgetting that allow individuals, institutions, politics and cultures to suppress the
past. Quoted here in Connerton’s ordering: One, Repressive Erasure, which ‘appears
in its most brutal form, of course, in the history of totalitarian regimes’ and ‘can 
be employed to deny the fact of a historical rupture as well as to bring about a
historical break’4 (2008, 60). Two, Prescriptive Forgetting, is ‘precipitated by an act
of state, but it differs from erasure because it is believed to be in the interests of all
parties to the previous dispute and because it can therefore be acknowledged publicly’
(2008, 61). Three, Forgetting That Is Constitutive In The Formation Of A New
Identity: ‘Long-term forgetting as a process of cultural discarding in the interests of
forming a new identity is signalled by two types of semantic evidence, one the
emergence of a new type of vocabulary, the other the disappearance of a now obsolete
vocabulary’ (2008, 64). Four, Structural Amnesia, is the recording of genealogies that
‘result from a deﬁcit of information’ so that a society or ‘a person tends to remember
only those links in his or her pedigree that are socially important’ (2008, 6). Five,
Forgetting As Annulment, which Connerton suggests is opposite to Structural
Amnesia, stemming instead from excessive access or a ‘surfeit of information’ (2008,
52
Memoirs: cultural forgetting

## S. 68

65). Six, Forgetting As Planned Obsolescence, is what is ‘built into the capitalist system
of consumption’ where a timespan referred to in marketing as the ‘product lifecycle’
becomes shorter (2008, 66). Forgetting becomes part of consumption in object terms
where ‘consumer objects obey the pressures of increasing velocity’5 (2008, 67). Seven,
Forgetting As Humiliated Silence, perhaps the most relevant to the form of forgetting
explored in this chapter, ‘is manifest in a widespread pattern of behaviour in civil
society, and it is covert, unmarked and unacknowledged. Its most salient feature is
a humiliated silence’ (2008, 67). To illustrate the last type of forgetting, Connerton
gives the example of the silence in post-war German literature: ‘The horrors of the
air bombardment and its long-term repercussions have not been brought to public
attention either in historical investigations or in literary accounts’ (2008, 68).
Connerton’s seven tenets or taxonomies for forgetting constitute selective behavioural,
structural, societal, cultural, political and economic amnesia with regard to histories
and events of disturbance. Jacoby’s systematic formula for forgetting enables the
establishment of present memory; a memory that searches forwards, attempting to
ﬁll the void, and in so doing blocks what it knows from the past.
Memory, recalling the experience of an event, emerges through the memory of the
event. Each time memory is recalled it has the possibility of being re-enacted and
therefore reinterpreted. Memory, then, is the constant state of decay in it being recalled.
This chapter explores the various losses and gains, sufferings and avoidances of
forgetting that plagued post-war German society. The chapter begins with absence in
post-war German literature by highlighting three writers who dared to speak of the
suffering of civilians during the air war on German cities, moving to Sebald’s selfanaesthesia concept in answer to this absence, and ending with a concept for spatial
anaesthesia. As seen in Chapter 1 regarding Speer’s designed ruination of Berlin over
millennia, forgetting in Sebald’s self-anaesthesia concept can be maintained through
the suppression and/or ruination of literary voices to record the experiences of the air
war on Germany. The four literary works discussed below form, in my mind, ruination
literature, written at the time of destruction and decay to safeguard against forgetting.
Silent voices – the hiddenness of experience
The silence that permeated German society in the aftermath of World War II was
twofold. First, silence concerning the atrocities committed during the Nazi regime’s
mass murder of Jews and mass destruction in Eastern Europe and Russia in a war
that cost 60 million lives. The second silence, connected to the ﬁrst, left a nation
without the ‘right’ to tell of the suffering, death and destruction experienced during
the Allied air war on Germany. As we shall see, the silence (an institutional, cultural,
family and individual ‘unspeaking’) evolved via psychological silence towards a
pathological forgetting. In On The Natural History of Destruction,6 W.G. Sebald
attributes the German people’s amnesia to a form of self-anaesthesia ‘shown by a
community that seemed to have emerged from a war of annihilation without any
signs of psychological impairment’ (2003, 11). Sebald is scathing in his attack on the
deﬁciency in German literature to voice, from a German perspective, the destruction
of German towns and cities and the suffering of the civilisation population as a result.
Sebald cites only a ‘handful of writers’ that did speak out to express the effects of
three years of indiscriminate carpet bombing that lead to the killing and maiming 
of hundreds of thousands of people.
Memoirs: cultural forgetting
53

## S. 69

Apart from Heinrich Böll, only a few authors – Hermann Kasack, Hans Erich
Nossack, Arno Schmidt, and Peter de Mendelssohn – ventured to break the taboo
on any mention of the inward and outward destruction, and as we shall see, they
generally did so rather equivocally.
Sebald 2003, 19
Before exploring Sebald’s self-anaesthesia concept, it is imperative to evaluate the
writers Sebald cites to understand the ‘taboo’ many felt in writing about the German
experience in the aftermath of the war. These writers encountered difﬁculties, as many
German publishing houses preferred to stay away from such works through an
irrational fear of the former power of the Nazi regime. The incrimination German
publishing houses feared meant that works written in the immediate aftermath of
WWII had to wait up to thirty years before they were released – when German society
was ready to commit to accounting for the effects of the war it raged on other nations
and its own.
Of the German novelists who gave their voice, I will focus on Heinrich Böll’s The
Silent Angel (1950),7 Hans Erich Nossack’s The End (1943)8 and An Offering for
the Dead (1947),9 and a much later work by Alexander Kluge, Air Raid (1977)10.
Each book records the physical and psychological effects of mass aerial bombardment
on people who lived in the targeted cities – Hamburg, Halberstadt and Cologne.
They are powerful accounts of despair faced on a daily basis in a unprecedented
mechanised war of mass destruction, with ﬁrestorms up to 1000 degrees Celsius,
with burning buildings and bodies ﬁlling the air with the smell of rotting ﬂesh and,
in their hundreds of thousands, the rats and swarms of ﬂies that preyed on them11.
In this apocalyptic Dante-like world, inhabitants of bombed cities who had not
suffocated or burnt to death in their cellars would emerge from their subterranean
pits to a city in ruins. Autobiographical, as in Hans Erich Nossack’s The End, or
biographical, as in Heinrich Böll’s The Silent Angel, the literary works are accounts
of death and destruction, characters numbed into immobility, desperate relationships
and cruelty of living in the devastated city.
Hans Erich Nossack’s The End is an account as a witness to the destruction of
his city, Hamburg. Written three months after it was bombed in July 1943 (one of
more than 200 bombing raids on the city), the book opens with the line: ‘I experienced
the destruction of Hamburg as a spectator’ (1943, 1). Nossack gives a ﬁrst-hand
account of a series of devastating bombing raids that caused Hamburg’s near-total
destruction, and of the terror that engulfed its people in the ensuing ﬁrestorms.
I feel I have been given a mandate to render an account. Let no one ask me why
I presume to speak of a mandate: I cannot answer that. I feel my mouth would
remain closed forever if I did not take care of this ﬁrst. Also, I feel an urgency
to set down right away, even though only three months have passed. For reason
will never be capable of comprehending as a reality or preserving in memory
what happened here.
Nossack 1943, 2
Nossack describes the bombing raid from a safe distance of the countryside while
on a momentary break from the city, ‘the ﬁrst time in ﬁve years that I had left
Hamburg for a vacation’ (1943, 2). The idea of a vacation in the middle of a war
54
Memoirs: cultural forgetting

## S. 70

seems surreal yet it is a reminder that, even in war, life somehow can be maintained
as moments of peacetime. Nossack describes the idyllic country retreat and the
activities he and his wife Misi undertake, such as walking in the heath, collecting
wood and pinecones for cooking, reading adventure novels, going to the small village
for shopping, and dealing with the ‘madwomen’ from the adjoining neighbours
known to have a dubious reputation. ‘We love the heath, somehow we belong there,
perhaps we were born there ages ago. Others feel sick there and become melancholy.
They cannot live without time; for the heath is without time. They don’t want to
know that we were born of a fairy tale and will become a fairy tale again. We began
to forget the war’ (1943, 6). Their momentary escapism into the relative shelter of
the country is short-lived. In the middle of the night Nossack is woken by Misi to
the ‘caterwauling’ sounds of air raid sirens coming from the surrounding villages: ‘I
jumped out of bed and ran barefoot out of the house, into this sound that hovered
like an oppressive weight between the clear constellations and the dark earth, not
here and not there but everywhere in space; there was no escaping it’ (1943, 7).
Outside of the hut, Nossack describes how the ‘landscape cowered, holding its breath’
(1943, 7). A little further on in the text, he declares:
One didn’t dare to inhale for fear of breathing it in. It was the sound of eighteen
hundred airplanes approaching Hamburg from the south at an unimaginable
height. We had already experienced two hundred or even more air raids, among
them some very heavy ones, but this was something completely new. And yet
there was an immediate recognition: this was what everyone had been waiting
for, what had hung for months like a shadow over everything we did, making
us weary. It was the end.
Nossack 1943, 8
Separated by distance from the unfolding catastrophic bombing on his city, Nossack
reports that not even the country escaped the terror, where ‘[t]he earth writhed in
agony’ (1943, 15). On their return to Hamburg, he and Misi pass a trail of people
clutching bundles of possessions streaming from their burning city. Nossack employs
various tropes to describe the indescribable fascination and repulsion ‘with the
spectacle of total annihilation’ (1943, 36).
Those who were known to have experienced unimaginably frightful hours, who
had run through the ﬁre with their clothes burning, stumbling over charred
corpses; before whose eyes and in whose arms children had suffocated; who had
seen their houses collapsing right after their father or husband has gone back
inside to save something or other; all those who had spent months hoping for
news from the missing and who at the very least had lost all their possessions
in a matter of minutes – why didn’t they cry and lament?
Nossack 1943, 21
Nossack’s literary attempt to reconcile in words death and destruction, of
maddened, shell-shocked people and the ‘dispassionate manner of talking’ from
survivors, created a landscape of despair where ‘the nightmare closed our mouths to
the point of suffocation. And how could anyone have awakened us?’ (1943, 21). His
voice is an anguished cry; half scream, half asphyxiation and a questioning of his
Memoirs: cultural forgetting
55

## S. 71

role in witnessing such devastation, where ‘[w]e no longer have a past’ (1943, 23).
Throughout the book Nossack appeals to the reader to try and understand the
‘incomprehensible’ horror. Yet this is also presented as a complication of horror and
of the senses; normality clashes within a surreal landscape of the affected and
disaffected:
When Misi and I walked through our ruined district looking for our street, we
saw inside a house that stood alone and intact in the midst of a vast expanse of
rubble a woman cleaning her windows. We nudged each other, we stood as if
spellbound. We thought this was a madwoman. The same thing happened when
we saw children cleaning and raking a small front yard. . . . And one afternoon
we arrived at a completely undestroyed suburb. People were sitting on their
balconies drinking coffee. It was like a movie, it wasn’t really possible.
Nossack 1943, 23
These surrealistic scenarios of the woman washing her windows and the couple
drinking coffee on their balcony may also be explained as normal rather than
abnormal behaviour in a city that has experienced more than 200 bombing raids
over three years, and also asks what becomes normal behaviour in an environment
under continuous destruction? Through such deﬁance by people maintaining a sense
of normality in the face of overwhelming destruction, Nossack describes what the
city has become:
Rats and ﬂies were lords of the city. Insolent and fat, the rats disported themselves
on the streets. But even more nauseating were the ﬂies. They were large and of
a shimmering green; no one had ever seen ﬂies like this. They wallowed in
swarming clumps on the pavement, sat, copulating, on top of the ruined walls,
warmed themselves, bloated and tired, on splinters of window glass. When they
could not ﬂy, they would crawl after us through the narrowest of crevices, soiling
everything, and their rustling and buzzing was the ﬁrst thing one heard in the
morning. This didn’t stop until October.
Nossack 1943, 44
Nossack gives graphic descriptions of desolation, mass destruction and bloated,
burnt corpses. ‘The ﬂies were so thick that the men couldn’t get into the cellars, they
kept slipping on maggots the size of ﬁngers, and the ﬂames [of the ﬂamethrowers]
had to clear the way for them to each of those who had perished in ﬂames’ (1943, 44).
Equally graphic are the photographs by Erich Andres that form an epilogue to the
book: burnt, tangled corpses; an old man sitting on a bench cradling his head as if
avoiding the wretched scene in front of him; people with bundles ﬁlled with ‘pathetic
belongings’ climbing onto the back of trucks; others pushing carts in single ﬁle
making their exodus from the city; a soldier writing on a wall ﬁlled with notices
seeking the whereabouts of family or loved ones. Andres’s photographs of death,
destruction, despair and exodus are more than shock images; they are also part of
what cannot be described – for it is images that don’t forget. There is no escaping
the horror in Andres’s photographs, their punctum, the type Barthes describes, ‘pricks’
the viewer (as highlighted by Berger, McCullin’s photographs of the Vietnam war
did the same twenty years later). In The Body in Pain – The Making and Unmaking
56
Memoirs: cultural forgetting

## S. 72

of the World, Elaine Scarry describes a fourfold, ever-decreasing spatial metaphor
for where pain hides and where it can be found. In my mind, this connects with
Nossack’s search:
The injury which in the ﬁrst sentence is recognized as massive (by analogy, the
destruction of a city) is folded within itself until by the second construction it
seems only the destroyed house within the otherwise standing city, and by the
third only a closet within that house, and ﬁnally by the fourth a shelf in the
closet. It is not that we will cease to perceive and feel the power of the injury.
The wound on the shelf, a damaged head, a torn off arm, an open belly will
stare out at the observer by the closet door and ﬂood him with the nausea of
awe and terror, overwhelm him, bring him even to his knees as though it were
a gun rather than an open gash poised in his direction12
Scarry 1985, 77
Scarry’s sign of pain moving from the scale of the city to the inner shelf of the
closet is a similar outward to inner movement as Nossack’s experience of what he
sees and draws in with each breath. It is hard even today from the distance of time
and one’s own experience to comprehend what he found to be the ‘incomprehensible’.
Having been exposed, it becomes difﬁcult to forget his writing and the images in the
book; each is the other. The harrowing description of a traumatised woman travelling
on a train going south, for example: as she lifts her suitcase from the rack, it abruptly
opens and out spills a bundle of cloth in which she has wrapped her dead baby, that
she has been carrying for more than a week – not knowing how to let go or where
and how to bury it.
Nossack’s second book An Offering for the Dead, written four years after The
End, is a literary work that unfolds through the voice of the book’s central characternarrator. Based on an apocalyptic future, the product of a cataclysmic war, pathetic
attempts at remembering what caused it are funnelled through unquestionable
forgetting. ‘We must avoid talking about it. I have done so only because I no longer
have a name that says anything about me, and because nothing gives me a name that
compels me to imagine anything. But you are to know what I am’ (1947, 16). In
Nossack’s post-apocalyptic future, collisions of space and time are turned inside out
through disconnected narratives and small reveals:
A lid no longer quite ﬁts a pot because it is twisted, or the front of a spoon is
worn smooth from so much stirring. But we no longer notice such things, we
are used to them; after all, these minor defects developed through our living
together – yes, and all these objects are still here, and you do not know why. A
ﬂy only has to buzz, and you are done for.
Nossack 1947, 18
Part ghost, part real, Nossack’s narrator is perhaps Nossack himself, who four
years after Hamburg’s destruction is still comprehending the catastrophe.13 Nossack
strings together fragments of memory and forgetting, through various coincidences
within a desolate, abandoned city. Entering a house for no other reason than the
front door was open, the narrator wanders through its rooms, passing his hand over
objects without touching them, before coming across a room that belonged to a
Memoirs: cultural forgetting
57

## S. 73

woman. ‘I stood at her mirror and peered into it. There, I saw everything that was
behind me and everything that surrounded me. But before me there was nothing,
and I too was not in there. A human being would have cried out in shock: I am lost’
(1947, 21). The descriptions, encounters and conversations in the novel form an
inventory that oscillates between the evident and indeterminate in a surreal landscape
cloaked in fog, where human bird-like creatures roam in ﬂocks over the city.
Once, a war took place outside. The nations tried to destroy one another. That
was a long time ago. The dead can scarcely remember. They tell each other they
are better off forgetting all about it, and we will forget it; otherwise someone
will feel like trying again and will declare that he has to avenge us.
Nossack 1947, 76
Later in the book the narrator slips into a crater (a bomb crater we assume) where,
sitting by a murky pond of water at the bottom, he encounters a homeless man. The
conversation between them is absurdist, heightened by the homeless man’s desire for
female company that is turned into abstract reality when the narrator carves into the
crater’s clay wall an idealised female form that, upon its completion, allegorically
comes alive. The true sense of the book is revealed towards the end through the
narrator’s search for his family. He meets his half-brother (a dead soldier), his
forehead wrapped in a bandage, his mother (grey, old and dressed in coarse cloth)
and father, a commanding yet shadowy ﬁgure. His mother tells him about the war,
how it started and the death and destruction it wrought, and the meeting and killing
of ‘the man who started it’ (1947, 115). Although An Offering for the Dead is not
an attempt to grasp the seemly inexplicable as in The End, both books work in the
post-traumatic condition, swinging between the real, the delusional and the pitifully
indefensible.
Inside the jacket of the English version of Heinrich Böll’s The Silent Angel,
translator Breon Mitchell writes:
Similar to Böll himself, the novel’s hero Hans Schnitzler returns after the war to
his ravaged city, homeless, impoverished and emotionally drained. In place of
the picturesque avenues and friendly community he left behind, he ﬁnds a world
where identities are bought and sold, food and coal are purloined by whatever
means available and human kindness has drowned in a well of cynicism and selfinterest.14
Böll’s The Silent Angel offers a different voice to Nossack’s The End and An
Offering for the Dead. Written between 1949 and 1951, Böll’s novel is set among
the ruins of Cologne in the ﬁrst few weeks after Germany’s capitulation. Böll uses
metaphors and symbols to present a post-war German landscape of devastation
where humanity, cruelty and intolerance mix with primitive forms of survival and
the search for companionship and affection. The novel begins with the main character
Hans, who is looking at a fallen sculpture of an angel, particularly its face, which
for him momentarily appears real beyond its surface of glazed plaster. The fallen
angel might be interpreted as a metaphorical representation of the fallen or abandoned
Christian God in face of the horror, the human suffering and the murder of millions
during the war.
58
Memoirs: cultural forgetting

## S. 74

Hans shares Böll’s pre-war history as a bookseller and during the war as a soldier
in the German army. We are introduced to him as he lies in jail awaiting execution
for desertion. He avoids death thanks to his jailer, a fellow soldier whom he does
not know, who insists on trading uniforms, taking his place and forfeiting his life so
that Hans may escape. This Dickensian A Tale of Two Cities beginning sees Hans
walk out of prison as the other soldier and head to his home city of Cologne, where
the wife of the soldier executed in his place also lives. Hans tracks down the soldier’s
wife and during their brief encounter he declares to her: ‘Your husband stole my
death.’ With the destruction of the city and the experience of war, Hans has come
to believe that it is better to be dead than to be part of the living dead – the greyfaced survivors who roam aimlessly about the city in search of food, shelter and
clothing. Through the rubble-torn streets, Hans make his way home:
He found the spot where the building had stood without any difﬁculty. Perhaps
it was the number of steps he had taken from the crossroads, or some aspect of
the series of tree stumps that once had formed a tall and beautiful avenue – at
any rate something caused him to stop suddenly, look to the left, and there it
was. He recognized the remains of the stairwell, picked his way across the ruins
