towards it: he was home.
Böll 1994, 17
Böll’s novel draws on a world where the results of total war, destruction and
ruination engender apathy. Hans becomes increasingly detached from the bleak
realities of his surroundings, a city in ruins and unrecognizable to him, where nature
has taken over.
The ruins he had to walk through now were different, hills overgrown with heavy
greenery. Small trees grew on them, and thick, variegated weeds, knee high. They
were low, gentle hills, between which the streets seemed like narrow gorges;
peaceful, sunken country roads.
Böll 1994, 46
Apathetic, exhausted, hungry, carrying false identity papers, constantly drifting
and avoiding the occupying Allied Forces, Hans discerns Cologne’s varying periods
of destruction:
The date of the destruction of any particular ruin could be determined by its
overgrowth: it was a question of botany. The heap of rubble naked and barren,
raw stone, newly broken masonry piled thickly, violently, with iron beams jutting
out, showing scarcely a spot of rust. There wasn’t a blade of grass to be seen;
while in other places trees were already growing, charming little trees in bedrooms
and kitchens, close by the rusty shell of the burned-out stove. Here there was
only naked destruction, desolate and terribly empty, as if the breath of the bomb
still hung in the air. Only the tiles, those that had survived, gleaming in innocence.
Böll 1994, 81
The battle for survival is linked to the possession of food vouchers, ﬁnding bread,
a blanket and somewhere to sleep. In his city people live like rats in underground
Memoirs: cultural forgetting
59

## S. 75

cellars grey with the damp smell of ‘repulsive rubble’ and above ground, in train
stations ﬁlled to the brim with ‘ﬁlthy’ discharged soldiers and civilians waiting to
take trains, any train to another place. Brutality and lawlessness between people runs
the city, ﬁlling its days and nights.
‘What do you want?’ asked the face. . . .
‘Bread’, he said, and handed him the voucher as if he were at a counter.
‘There’s no more bread’.
He didn’t understand. ‘Bread’, he said, ‘but the num –
I’m supposed to – ‘
‘No’, said the voice with the calm matter-of-factness. ‘No, there’s no more
bread’.
Now the hands emerged from the lower depths; long hands with slender ﬁngers,
they surfaced and held the voucher that represented a loaf of bread, and the
ﬁngers tore up the voucher, but not simply tearing it once with a sharp, short
rip, but tearing it crosswise, four or ﬁve times, repeatedly, with pleasure – you
could tell that. It fell to the ﬂoor like confetti, whitish, scattering like breadcrumbs.
. . .
‘There’s your bread,’ said the voice.
Böll 1994, 82
What emerges from Böll’s discouraging landscape is a story of human compassion
and survival, for one stands a better chance to survive with someone than alone.
Hans ﬁnds solace in a kindred spirit, a woman named Regina who has lost both her
child and husband. In their shared consolation of respite and abject comfort,
destruction is ever present where everything falls apart. ‘They lay awake for a long
time, smoking, while the wind howled in the building, loosening fragments and
causing bricks to tumble, sections of plaster came sailing down from the upper
storeys, crashing to the ground and shattering to pieces’ (1994, 131). The characters
in the novel live in shadows of themselves and within the cracks of their destroyed
city. Böll’s fallen angel at the beginning of the book connects to another reading of
the angel metaphor from Walter Benjamin’s description of Paul Klee’s 1920 painting
Angelus Novus. In Theses on the Philosophy of History, Benjamin’s last major essay
before his death in 1940, he argues for the ‘struggle against fascism’ and ‘the
knowledge that the view of history which gives rise to it is untenable’. He writes:
A Klee painting named Angelus Novus shows an angel looking as though he is
about to move away from something he is ﬁxedly contemplating. His eyes are
staring, his mouth is open, his wings are spread. This is how one pictures the
angel of history. His face is turned toward the past. Where we perceive a chain
of events, he sees one single catastrophe which keeps piling wreckage upon
wreckage and hurls it in front of his feet. The angel would like to stay, awaken
the dead, and make whole what has been smashed. But a storm is blowing from
Paradise; it has got caught in his wings with such violence that the angel can no
longer close them. This storm irresistibly propels him into the future to which
his back is turned, while the pile of debris before him grows skyward. This storm
is what we call progress.
Benjamin 1940, 257
60
Memoirs: cultural forgetting

## S. 76

Benjamin’s ruinous world, ‘which keeps piling wreckage upon wreckage’, is what
has transpired in the defeat of fascism. In Benjamin’s text (as in Böll’s) the redemptive
angel is absent. In A Landscape of Events, Paul Virilio similarly sides with Benjamin’s
position by declaring: ‘Today this theological vision no longer belongs to the angel
of history. It has become the vision of each and every one of us’15 (2000, xiii). In
The Necessary Angel, Massimo Cacciari reiterates that the fact that Benjamin’s angle
has its back turned to the future is ‘is essential’ in understanding its metaphysical
character16 (1994, 56). In Memory, History, Forgetting, Paul Ricoeur asks: ‘What,
then, is for us this storm that so paralyzes the angel of history?’17 (2004, 500). Böll’s
answer to the angel as theological saviour lies crippled in the ruins of destruction, a
historical religious relic offering no deliverance.
Written much later than the books by Böll and Nossack, Alexander Kluge’s Air
Raid was ﬁrst published in German in 1977. Updated and translated into English in
2014, it gives his account of the air raid on his city of Halberstadt. Kluge was thirteen
when Halberstadt was bombed on 8 April 1945, a month before the end of the war
in Europe on 8 May 1945. The bombing of Halberstadt wasn’t planned by Allied
Bomber Command, being a replacement due to dense cloud cover and low visibility
over the original target. Rather than return with their load of high explosives and
incendiary bombs, the planes were instead diverted to the defenceless provincial city
of Halberstadt. More than 200 Lancaster bombers dropped their arsenal on the city,
virtually destroying it in a single raid. Such strategies were not uncommon for Allied
Bomber Command, led by the instigator and defender of its carpet-bombing strategy
on Germany’s cities and civilian population, Sir Arthur (Bomber) Harris.18
Published more than thirty years after the destruction of Halberstadt, Kluge’s book
is similar to Nossack’s The End in that it gives a ﬁrst-hand account of the destruction
of the city and the terror experienced by its inhabitants. Kluge’s book begins with
brief accounts of incidents and people during the bombing raid. ‘The tower observers,
Frau Arnold and Frau Zacke’, who, as air raid wardens on the lookout for bomber
aircraft from the vantage point of the church tower, try to raise the alarm while
desperately sheltering as bombs explode around them. Another example is ‘the
unknown photographer’, a camera seller who saves what he can from his burning
store and, in ﬂeeing from his burning city, takes pictures as pieces of evidence ‘to
record from this distance the burning town, his hometown, at this moment of
misfortune’19 (Kluge 2014, 7). The photographer is caught and arrested by German
soldiers for photographing too close to an underground armaments factory, forced
to hand over his camera and ﬁlms (for he might be a spy, and in any case photographing
the effects of bombing raids was a criminal offence). Although Kluge is unsure what
happened to the photographer, he tracks down one of the rolls of ﬁlm he shot and
includes six grainy black and white images of his city’s destruction in the book.
Kluge weaves his stories through a spatial annotation of Halberstadt from
impassable, rubble-torn streets, burning buildings and smoking cellars, building a
terrain of the destroyed city. He draws from multiple perspectives on the air war
including stories from Allied pilots, technical bombing strategies, ﬂight formation,
drawings of bomb types and general war information from various sources, archives
and his own experience.20 Kluge also inserts interviews with air commanders, journal -
ists and witnesses displaying the strategic mechanisms, psychology and indifference
of bomber pilots achieved by synthetic separation of the attacker in the air from the
victim on the ground.
Memoirs: cultural forgetting
61

## S. 77

Conclusion
Exploring the cultural, racial and political aspects of Operation Desert Storm in
Kuwait in 1991 and the beating of the African-American Rodney King by police the
same year, the American ethnologist Allen Feldman suggests that their mediated
reportage resulted in a sustained cultural anaesthesia. Feldman explains cultural
anaesthesia as: ‘the banishment of disconcerting, discordant, and anarchic sensory
presences and agents that undermine the normalizing and often silent premises of
everyday life’21 (1994, 405). Everyday life among the ruins of Berlin, Hamburg,
Cologne and many other towns and cities had become normalised over the three
years and thousands of bombing raids. The populations of these cities had ‘grown’
into the destruction of their city, becoming a ‘normal’ part of living in the city as
Nossack attests on his return to Hamburg. Cultural anaesthesia, Feldman writes, stems
from ‘a post-Holocaust and late-capitalist modernity, the quantitative and qualitative
dissemination of objectiﬁcation increases the social capacity to inﬂict pain on the
Other – and I would add – to render the Other’s pain inadmissible to public discourse
and culture’22 (1994, 406).
By publically voicing their harrowing war experiences, Böll, Nossack and Kluge
were exceptions to the silence of Germany’s post-war literature. Sebald attributed the
silence that prevailed in post-war Germany to a form of self-anaesthesia. Comparable
to the social amnesia Jacoby describes, Sebald’s amnesia is an enforced production
for forgetting; social, cultural and political. Böll, Nossack and Kluge’s accounts alone
cannot speak for the nation. Sebald was criticised for not referencing the many accounts
that were to surface later when Germany, through the conﬁdence of the ‘economic
miracle’ of the 1960s and 1970s, was able to speak out about its suffering in the war.
The next section looks at Sebald’s self-anaesthesia concept and how forgetting formed
the psychological silences that conditioned post-war German society.
Sebald’s self-anaesthesia – underwriting amnesia
Known for his novels that occupy a space between ﬁction and nonﬁction, such as
The Rings of Saturn, The Emigrants, Vertigo, Austerlitz and the poem After Nature,
W.G. Sebald’s On the Natural History of Destruction sits squarely among works
covering war and history. Nevertheless, Sebald’s account of destruction and war
pursues his quest to express the human condition. In her introduction to The
Emergence of Memory – Conversations with W.G. Sebald, editor Lynne Sharon
Schwartz writes: ‘Sebald dwells always on the same large themes. His favourite is
the swift blossoming of every human endeavour and its long slow death, either through
natural or man-made disaster, leaving a wealth of remains to be pored over, not to
mention vast human suffering’23 (2010, 18). Engrossed by the human endeavour
between the self and the world, Sebald’s works move from the cosmic arena, as in
The Rings of Saturn, to the terrains of exodus from tyranny, as in The Emigrants.
Shadowing Sebald’s thoughts, Schwartz asks: ‘Why might we not have “appointments
to keep in the past” just as we do in the future? But in our collective amnesia, we
erase time as we go, forgetting what deﬁnes us. He has not forgotten; he pieces
together the shards to remind us’ (2010, 19).
On the Natural History of Destruction began as a series of lectures collectively
titled Luftkrieg und Literatur (Air War and Literature) given in Zürich in 1997.
According to Dagmar Barnouw, On the Natural History of Destruction took its title
62
Memoirs: cultural forgetting

## S. 78

from Solly Zuckerman’s 1945 project to report on the destruction of Cologne.
Overwhelmed by the experience, he (Zuckerman) never wrote the report, realising
that he did not have the language to describe the utter desolation of that ‘ravaged’
city’24 (2008, 45). Sebald was able to use Zuckerman’s title thanks to the passage of
time and a different perspective on the presence of ruins, rubble and human misery
so total, unimaginable and overwhelming, beyond human comprehension and
language. First published in German in 1999 and translated into English in 2003,
Sebald’s lean volume placed him at the forefront in voicing the absence in post-war
German literature of the effects of the Allied bombing campaign on 131 German
towns and cities during World War II. In her chapter Rings of Smoke from the book
The Emergence of Memory – Conversations with W.G. Sebald, Ruth Franklin makes
the point that as a German writer (Sebald had been living in the UK for more than
thirty years, where he was Chair of European Literature at the University of East
Anglia) and, given his moral credibility, ‘in the ﬁrst two chapters of his book – that
is, the portion delivered as lectures – Sebald mentions the Holocaust only obliquely,
and no other form of German aggression during World War II at all’25 (Franklin
2010, 232). Perhaps Franklin is looking for the constant apology that has marked
much of German post-war cultural and political society, and has set her barometer
that any marking of German suffering can only be viewed within the context to that
of the extermination of the Jews, the killing of millions, the destruction of humanity,
His literary work, especially The Emigrants and Austerlitz, shows him to be
unique among German writers in his understanding of the catastrophe that befell
the European Jews. Indeed, only a writer with Sebald’s moral standing with regard
to the Holocaust could have dared write such a book as this one. And yet parts
of Luftkrieg und Literatur are weirdly lacking in this sensitivity. One hesitates
to accuse Sebald of something so crass as ‘moral equivalency,’ but the suspicion
of such a confusion cannot be avoided. On the very ﬁrst page of the book Sebald
calls the Allied bombings of Germany during the war ‘an act of extermination
(Vernichtungsaktion) unique in history up to that point.’ Later he refers to the
‘incineration’ (Einäscherung) of the city of Hamburg. He knows as well as anyone
what those words imply.
Franklin 2010, 23
In making this critique of Sebald, Franklin points out ‘one could argue that the
Holocaust is simply not his subject here, that he is writing about an entirely different
aspect of the war, and that to do justice to the Holocaust as well would have required
an entirely different book’ (2010, 233). Inescapable and inseparable as the Holocaust
is from Nazi Germany and as it is for Germans today, On the Natural History of
Destruction is written with a tempered anger towards the absence of a German
perspective on the suffering of the civilian population. Germans could not claim to
be victims of the war when witness to mass extermination of Jews and a war that
cost more than 60 million lives. Sebald’s focus is ﬁrst to attack the silence of postwar writers, second to critique the handful of writers who did, third to deﬁne the
strategy of aerial mass destruction, and fourth to ask: ‘How ought such a natural
history of destruction to begin?’ (2003, 33). Sebald refers to the horror of air war
with descriptions from Böll and Nossack’s ﬁrst-hand accounts; the stench of burnt
corpses, swarming ﬂies and the horrors of ﬁrestorms:
Memoirs: cultural forgetting
63

## S. 79

Sebald refers to these images at the end of his ﬁrst chapter, which contains his
most powerful descriptions of Allied air war, indeed a sublime ‘natural history’
of heretofore unknown forces of destruction: mountains, tidal waves of ﬁre,
hurricanes of ﬂames, burning skies fusing heaven and hell. He was not interested
in the people who experienced these horrors but in the hyperphysical effects of
total air war that he reconstructed in a few carefully chosen and rendered details:
ﬁelds of ruins sculpted by ﬁre, piles of grotesquely shrunk purple corpses cured
in its unearthly heat, puddles of their congealing fat. Isolated in their literally
‘brilliant’ representation, they point to the surreally clear incomprehensible mass
transformation of the familiar and normal. Writing about air war in the late 1990s,
Sebald believed himself to be the ﬁrst writer to really see and represent the
monstrous scale and truth of a destruction ultimately justiﬁed by a monstrous
German past.
Barnouw 2008, 45
As Barnouw suggests, Sebald sees his role as exposing the self-imposed literary
suffocation by most German writers who dared not to tell of the German suffering
in the face of the death camps. Who in any case would listen? Sebald was one year
old at the end of the war and, like many children growing up in post-war Germany,
experienced the blanket of silence that had fallen over the war:
I spent my childhood and youth on the northern outskirts of the Alps, in a region
that was largely spared the immediate effects of the so-called hostilities. At the
end of the war I was just one year old, so I can hardly have any impressions of
that period of destruction based on personal experience. Yet to this day, when
I see photographs or documentary ﬁlms dating from the war I feel as if I were
its child, so to speak, as if those horrors I did not experience had cast a shadow
over me, and one from which I shall never entirely emerge.
Sebald 2003, 69
As a teenager, Sebald was exposed without explanation ‘to what happened’ by a
televised war documentary. It shook his teenage years, for he had found out he had
not been living with his (German) truth of the past. The silence he endured is where,
much later in his life, he was able to develop the thoughts that would lead to his Zurich
lectures to On the Natural History of Destruction and his self-anaesthesia concept.
The cultural and socio-political absences expressed in Sebald’s theory illustrate a
society consumed by guilt for the murder of millions at the very moment when it
was faced with the immense task of reconstructing its devastated cities.
The destruction, on a scale without historical precedent, entered the annals of
the nation as it set about rebuilding itself only in the form of vague generalizations.
It seems to have left scarcely a trace of pain behind in the collective consciousness,
it has been largely obliterated from the retrospective understanding of those
affected, and it never played any appreciable part in the discussion of the internal
constitution of our memory. 
Sebald 2003, 3
Besides the writers explored earlier in the chapter (Böll, Nossack, Kluge), Sebald
includes Herman Kasack, Arno Schmidt and Peter de Mendelssohn as other writers
64
Memoirs: cultural forgetting

## S. 80

who ‘ventured to break the taboo on any mention of the inward and outward
destruction’ (Sebald 2003, 11).
There was a tacit agreement, equally binding on everyone, that the true state of
material and moral ruin in which the country found itself was not to be described.
The darkest aspects of the ﬁnal act of destruction, as experienced by the great
majority of the German population, remained under a kind of taboo like a
shameful family secret, a secret that perhaps could not be privately acknowledged.
Sebald 2003, 9
This unresponsive detachment on the part of writers and German society as a
whole, neither recording nor discussing their suffering in the Allied air war, contrasted
with the mountains of literature produced by the victorious Allies and Russians
detailing their suffering. When accounts were provided, they ‘did not alter the fact
that the images of this horrifying chapter of our history have never really crossed the
threshold of the national consciousness’ (Sebald 2004, 11). Sebald’s criticism of any
substantial recording from a German perspective effectively created the collective
absence in German literature. Sebald references Nossack’s account of the destruction
of Hamburg where ‘[T]he ﬁre now rising 2,000 metres in the sky, snatched oxygen
to itself so violently that the air currents reached hurricane force.’ On the ground in
the raging ﬁres of their city, ‘[t]hose who had ﬂed from their air-raid shelters sank,
with grotesque contortions, in the thick bubbles thrown up by the melting asphalt’
(Sebald 2003, 27). Sebald borrows from Nossack’s account of what he sees when he
returns to Hamburg after the last raid on the city. He also refers to Böll’s account
of ‘what we found when we came home’, which proves on closer inspection to be
‘an instrument already tuned to individual and collective amnesia, and probably
inﬂuenced by pre-conscious self-censorship – a means of obscuring a world that could
no longer be presented in comprehensible terms’ (Sebald 2003, 9).
Besides the literary and societal self-enforced taboo, Sebald challenges the air war
strategy of indiscriminate bombing of towns and cities that in many cases brought
no military advantage. His critique of the Allies’ use of incendiary bombs that
unleashed ﬁrestorms, burning cities and people, is nevertheless located within German
atrocities of mass destruction and extermination. Sebald’s enquiry is a wider discourse
on air war and mass destruction, which the German Luftwaffe used to great effect
in their blitzkrieg strategy and which the Allies perfected en masse. Sebald asks, ‘How
ought such a natural history of destruction to begin’:26
[T]he question of whether and how [the ﬁre-bombing of cities] could be justiﬁed
was never the subject of open debate in Germany after 1945, no doubt mainly
because a nation which had murdered and worked to death millions of people
in its camps could hardly call on the victorious powers to explain the military
and political logic that dictated the destruction of German cities.27
Sebald 2003, 13
Post-war reconstruction in clearing the rubble from bombed cities – a working
through destruction where physical action (working through materials) – supplanted
the public necessity to raise the issue of German suffering during the war.
Memoirs: cultural forgetting
65

## S. 81

From the outset, the now legendary and in some respects genuinely admirable
reconstruction of the country after the devastation wrought by Germany’s
enemies, a reconstruction tantamount to a second liquidation in successive phases
of the nation’s own past history, prohibited any backward view. It did so through
the sheer amount of labour required and the creation of a new, faceless reality,
pointing the population exclusively towards the future and enjoining on it silence
about the past.
Sebald 2003, 6
Besides Böll’s The Silent Angel and Nossack’s The End, the handful of other
German works Sebald acknowledges include: Arno Schmidt’s Schwarze Spiegel (Black
Mirror), Hans Erich Kasack’s Die Stadt hinter dem Strom (The City beyond the River)
and Peter de Mendelsohn’s Die Kathedrale (The Cathedral). These writers’ attempts
to record the incomprehensible from a German perspective do not escape Sebald’s
criticism where, in his eyes, their work oscillates from real and concrete descriptions
of the fate of German civilians to the ‘shameful’ employment of poetic tales and
allegories as a reinforcement of their literary capacity. Even Nossack’s The End, which
Sebald holds perhaps in the highest esteem, does not escape criticism.
The ideal of truth inherent in its unpretentious objectivity, at least over long
passages, proves itself the only legitimate reason for continuing to produce
literature in the face of total destruction. Conversely, the construction of aesthetic
or pseudo-aesthetic effects from the ruins of an annihilated world is a process
depriving literature of its right to exist. 
Sebald 2003, 53
In subsequent years, and in a very different climate in the national consciousness
of German society and literature, these early literary attempts would be joined by
contemporary works including: Arno Schmidt’s Aus dem Leben eines Fauns (Scenes
from the Life of a Faun, 1953), Hubert Fichte’s Detlevs Imitationen ‘Grünspan’
(Detlev’s Imitations ‘Grünspan’, 1971), Hans Magnus Enzensberger’s Europa in
Trümmern (Europe in Ruins, 1990), Gunter Grass’s Im Krebsgang (Crabwalk, 2002)
and Klaus Schmidt’s Die Brandnacht (The Fire Night, 2004), to name a few. Of
Sebald’s selected few German writers who dared to speak out about the suffering
and devastation, it is Böll he singles out:
Of all the literary works written at the end of the 1940s, probably only Heinrich
Böll’s Der Engel schwieg [‘The Angel Was Silent’] gives some idea of the depths
of horror then threatening to overwhelm any who really looked at the ruins
around them. Reading it, one is immediately aware that this, of all novels, a tale
which seems marked by irremediable gloom, was more than readers of the time
could be expected to take as Böll’s publishers and probably Böll himself thought.
Sebald 2003, 10
Given Sebald’s analysis of German literary silence on a civil population’s suffering
during the war, there is nevertheless opposition to his self-anaesthesia concept of
German literature and societal silence. In Germans as Victims?: Thoughts on a Post-
Cold War History of World War II’s Legacies, Robert G. Moeller points out that:
‘Clearing away the rubble did not mean forgetting; recovery and reconstruction were
66
Memoirs: cultural forgetting

## S. 82

measures of how successfully Germans, East and West, had overcome the misery of
the war and the immediate post-war years’28 (2005, 162). In German literature as in
German reconstruction, I believe self-anaesthesia and forgetting operated simul tan -
eously; self-anaesthesia describes the enforced silence and forgetting; to turn away from
the past to gather the ruination of their cities, pile it up and bury it and forget it.
Sebald seems not to be unsettled with unpacking the human trauma that resides
in the bodies and minds of individuals and society more generally, potentially shielding
the visibility of external grieving. The immense force and resources the Allies
committed to the destruction of German cities was also a war on civilians. From the
Allies’ point of view, the carpet-bombing of German cities was aimed at breaking
the morale of the people; the meeting point of total destruction and a nation
(Germany) committed to total war.29 Moeller takes the same line as Sebald where
forgetting and silence are concerned:
The acknowledgment of German ‘crimes against humanity’ was also sometimes
paralleled by claims that German victims had suffered no less than what Germans
had inﬂicted on others. When an East German account of the bombing of Dresden
published in 1955 referred to Germans immolated in that city’s ﬁery ‘hell,’ it
established the equivalence of the crimes of the Allies and the crimes of the Nazis.
Moeller 2005, 162
One way to understand the trauma experienced by the German civilian population
can perhaps be metaphorically framed by its burial within each individual, a space
that according to Aristotle lies buried inside bodies. By identifying the lack of postwar German literature that accounts for German suffering, Sebald is also pointing
to the indiscriminate war where, like most wars, civilians are the predominant
casualties. Once again the wall of suppression is encountered in the German army’s
decimation of Eastern European and Russian civil populations.
As the conscious act of self-suppression, Sebald’s self-anaesthesia ‘engendered by
feelings of shame and a wish to defy the victors, was to keep quiet and look the
other way’ (Sebald 2003, 30). As an example of looking ‘the other way’, Sebald cites
the Danish reporter Stig Dagerman who, on a train from Hamburg in the autumn
of 1946, distinguished himself as a foreigner because ‘no one looked out of the
windows, and he was identiﬁed as a foreigner himself because he looked out’ (2003,
30). Sebald also references the American journalist Janet Flanner’s article about
Cologne in The New Yorker: ‘Through its clogged streets trickles what is left of its
life, a dwindled population in black and with bundles – the silent German people
appropriate to the silent city.’ This reinforces the blanketing silence witnessed by a
passive foreign onlooker.30 Sebald writes, ‘that reserve, that instinctive looking away
are the reasons why we know so little of what the Germans thought and observed
in the ﬁve years between 1942 and 1947. The ruins where they lived were the terra
incognita of the war’ (2003, 31).
In W.G. Sebald – Image, Archive, Modernity, Jonathan J. Long points out that
Sebald’s ‘attacks on Germany as lacking history and memory became increasingly
strident in his later years, they also began to look increasingly out of kilter with the
vigorous memory debates that have intensiﬁed markedly since German Reuniﬁca -
tion’31. Long also remarks that Sebald ‘ignores decades of philosophical, literary and
political attempts to address the Nazi past’ (2007, 69). On the public release of his
Memoirs: cultural forgetting
67

## S. 83

Zurich lectures, Sebald received a fair share of positive support from the German
press and from ordinary Germans in what he described as ‘a rough and ready
collection of various observations, material and theses’ (2003, 68). Towards the end
of On the Natural History of Destruction, he argues that trauma may have inhibited
many ordinary people, let alone writers, from writing about their experiences. Sebald
cites Hans Dieter Schäfer’s many attempts at writing on the destruction of Berlin and
Wolf Biermann’s recalling the ﬁrestorm in Hamburg, during which they found it hard
to ‘bring themselves to go back over the their traumatic experiences, for reasons
probably to be sought, partly in the subject itself, partly in the psycho-social
constitution of those affected’ (Sebald 2003, 92). Sebald’s self-anaesthesia cannot, in
my mind, be separated from the traumas that led society to turn its back on the past
and look to the future. Forgetting is both individual and institutional. Forgetting is
not opposite to remembering. Forgetting instead actions memory to fall into neglect
dismembering memory. Forgetting is a pause in dealing with trauma, and through
the pause the trauma submerges into the body and buries deep within individuals,
societies and governments. To unearth forgetting is to understand institutionalised
forgetting and the ﬂesh and bone hiddenness within individuals. Sebald explores both.
In material terms, Germany has engaged since the fall of the Berlin Wall in postmemory through a memorial-building programme to indelibly place the atrocities of
the Nazi regime during World War II into the German and world public domain. The
National Memorial to the Murdered Jews of Europe in Berlin, designed by American
architect Peter Eisenman (and originally Richard Serra), is positioned between the
city’s main tourist, political and commercial centres: Brandenburg Gate, Reichstag
and Potsdamer Platz. After many years of debate on the aesthetics of the design (and
memory) in the German parliament under the leadership of the then-Chancellor
Helmut Kohl, an agreed settlement of the aesthetics of the memorial was ﬁnally
accepted. Consisting of 2711 concrete stelae (gravestone pediments) placed on a grid
within an undulating ground plane, the experience of the memorial overwhelms the
body, reprising the sense of oppression and insecurity. There is no place to rest; the
memorial requires a state of constant awareness of others as you walk through it.32
Given that no single memorial can memorialise the remembrance of the mass-murdered
Jews of Europe, attempts at such memorialisation can be viewed as succeeding through
failure. In her article (Re) Covering the Past, Remembering the Trauma: the Politics
of Commemoration at Sites of Atrocity, Lisa M. Moore argues: ‘While museums and
memorials struggle to stabilize the past, memory remains unsettled, politically fraught,
and perpetually haunted by forgetting’33 (2009, 60). In this context, memorial and
failure can be argued as compatible, a question raised by James E. Young in his book
The Texture of Memory when he asks: ‘How do memorials employ time and
memory?’34 (1993, 15). Marc Augé reveals where the destructive-creative force of
remembering and forgetting claiming; ‘Oblivion is the force of memory and
remembrance is its product’35 (2004, 21). Citing Eisenman’s memorial as an example
of failure for remembrance is to bring to the surface the attempt to materialise the
incomprehensible. It would seem that Böll, Nossack and later Kluge may have been
faced throughout their writing with a continual sense of failure, as Hans Dieter Schäfer
and Wolf Biermann were. It is, in human terms, a matter of being able to record rather
than out of the necessity to document what needs to be told.
In 1985 the French ﬁlmmaker Claude Lanzmann released his ﬁlm Shoah
(Holocaust) that he had started work on eleven years previously.36 Lanzmann’s epic
68
Memoirs: cultural forgetting

## S. 84

documentary, covering ten hours and thirteen minutes of interviews with survivors,
witnesses and direct and indirect accomplices of the Holocaust, distinguishes itself
from other ﬁlm documentaries in that it contains no archive footage. Of the many
scenes that have stayed in my memory, that I seem or choose not to forget, two stick
closest to the surface. One scene, ﬁlmed in a small Polish village, sees Lanzmann
knocking on the front door of a house where the Jewish welcoming symbol still
remains carved into the wood. Lanzmann asks what happened to the original residents
of the house, pointing to the carved symbol. The reply from the woman, who is not
Jewish, was simple enough: The Jewish family was evicted when the Nazis occupied
the village and afterwards the house was given to her family. Further into the
conversation we ﬁnd out from the woman that the Jewish women were beautiful,
wore ﬁne clothes and had the eye of the Polish men, much to the disdain of their
wives and girlfriends. In the disappearance of the Jewish women (children and men),
Polish men turn their attention back again towards their wives and girlfriends. The
Jewish people from the village were loaded into sealed compartments on the back of
small trucks and driven around a forest clearing outside of the village. The carbon
monoxide from the exhaust was pumped into sealed compartments, killing them;
their bodies were then unloaded and buried in mass graves. In the Polish woman’s
explanation, forgetting is wrapped in ridding the village of the men’s blindness,
forming her dark vision of history.
Another scene is ﬁlmed in a barber’s shop in the Israeli capital, Tel Aviv. It shows
two barbers cutting hair, one of whom talks about his experience at Auschwitz. He
was one of the barbers who cut the hair of Jewish women, men, boys and girls before
they were gassed. Past and present visually collide in this scene. The recalling by the
barber of him cutting the hair of the dead as he is seen cutting hair in his shop is
one of two scenes that were re-enacted for the ﬁlm. During the scene, the barber has
an emotional breakdown and begs Lanzmann to stop ﬁlming. For more than thirty
years this barber has placed his terrifying experience somewhere in mind and body
as he has continued his profession that arguably also led him to survive the death
camp. Lanzmann remains vigilant, telling the barber that the story has to be told so
that future generations will know what happened. These two scenes exemplify two
different ﬁelds of forgetting. The Polish family’s acquisition of the house and the
disappearance of the Jewish women are explained through rivalry, systemic Nazi mass
murder, creating disappearance, leading, it must be added, to a ‘willed’ forgetting.
The barber, on the other hand, lives with his history where forgetting is constantly
enacted yet buried so that there can be some form of working in his profession in
the everyday. Trauma does not allow forgetting – for many it is not an option –
forgetting what can never be forgotten.
Conclusion
Towards the end of On the Natural History of Destruction, Sebald is at pains to
point out that his concept of self-anaesthesia is a literary one, aimed at giving a voice
to those who suffered yet have remained silent as part of an overall institutionalised
political and cultural absence of memory. ‘I repeat, I do not doubt that there were
and are memories of those nights of destruction; I simply do not trust the form –
including the literary form – in which they are expressed’ (2003, 79). Where they
were expressed in the early years of the Federal Republic, according to Sebald, was
Memoirs: cultural forgetting
69

## S. 85

in ‘encouraging the will to reconstruction’ (2003, 79). In the next section, the ‘will
to reconstruction’ in the aftermath of the war also encouraged a redrawing of the
original urban plan of many towns and cities. When driving through German cities
like Hamburg, Berlin, Darmstadt, Dusseldorf, Essen, Frankfurt or Kassel, no expert
knowledgeable of urban design is needed to see past and present urban plans colliding
in a single urban time. Reconstruction in many German cities amounted to getting
rid of centuries of progressive urban planning, remaking the city and its history. The
renewal programmes that swept across Germany as part of the Marshall Plan created
many cities ﬁlled with featureless architecture and banal residential environments.
Cities that could be rid of their past would also be cities that had exorcised the war
from their physical environments and, in so doing, from their minds. Taken together,
these exorcisms of the past expressed a new German nation that was looking forward
through urban reconstructions of forgetting. This, as we shall see, gave rise to the
urban ﬂaw; the policy of building, to build out destruction of the city that would set
in motion the conditions for spatial anaesthesia.
Spatial anaesthesia – the urban ﬂaw
The history of civilization, from the destruction of Carthage and Jerusalem to
the destruction of Dresden, Hiroshima, and the people, soil, and trees of Vietnam,
is a tragic record of sadism and destructiveness.
Eric Fromm, Anatomy of Human Destructiveness 
(1973, 166)
Fromm’s Anatomy of Human Destructiveness is a disturbing realisation of the human
capacity for destruction. This ‘tragic record of sadism’, the shattering of bodies and
cities, hinges on the human ability not only to inﬂict pain but also to forget pain that
allows repetition. Emerging from this tragic history, humanity can be recuperated as
destruction of cities moves into reconstruction, creating the foundation for spatial
anaesthesia. Out of destruction, modernism was deployed to evade the past, creating
anesthetised shifts in urban design and architecture to enable new forms in rebuilding
national identity in post-war Germany.
The cultural and institutional forgetting that also inﬁltrated post-war reconstruction
could convert or ‘un-Germanise’ Germany’s cities and towns and rebuild them with
attention to the international style of architecture and urban design. Destruction gave
the opportunity to experiment on large-scale, untested urban programmes that would
transform destroyed cities into modern cities embracing the future. By looking for -
wards, planning policies, identities and communities created urban and archi tectural
collisions at the intersection between the existing plan and the introduced model.
Cases real and proposed for massive urban change in European cities in the mid-
19th and 20th centuries were based on aesthetics, as much as on social and political
aspirations. In Paris, urban planning renewal was epitomized by Baron Haussmann’s
erasing of old slums to construct major boulevards, or Le Corbusier’s unrealised 1925
Plan Voisin for central Paris: a gigantic urbanism of highways lined with massive
housing blocks for a city untouched by the destruction of WWII. In his urban
planning treatise The City of Tomorrow and its Planning, Le Corbusier argues the
theory behind his Plan Voisin scheme with the following cynical explanation:
70
Memoirs: cultural forgetting

## S. 86

It would be a good thing if part of the centre of Paris belonged to foreigners. 
It would be a good thing for millions of international capital to be employed in
raising these gigantic towers of glass in the centre of Paris, and for a part of this
centre to belong to Americans or Germans; for would they not then take good
care that it was not destroyed by long-range guns or bombing airplanes? So
perhaps there may lie a remedy against War in the Air in the internationalization
of the centre of Paris. America would not permit its destruction, Germany would
take good care not to harm it! We all know that great wars are created by the
great capitals.37
Le Corbusier 1987, 296
According to Le Corbusier, the ‘rules’ for modern town planning need to be
systematic: ‘For the desire to rebuild any great city in a modern way is to engage in
a formidable battle. Can you imagine people engaging in a battle without knowing
their objectives?’ (1987, 160). Le Corbusier’s reference to building without objectives
is relevant to many of the urban reconstruction nightmares unleashed on German
towns and cities. In post-war Germany, large swathes of past urban fabric in cities
like Berlin, Hamburg, Cologne, Essen and Frankfurt, to name but a few, were erased
to make way for modernist planning models on an unprecedented scale. An aerial
photograph of central Hamburg in 1948 shows a series of white lines drawn over
existing streets and rubble-cleared vacant lots to illustrate the planning of major new
roads that cut into the heart of the city. Old city plans dissected and wedged between
new infrastructure demonstrate a readiness to erase existing urban plans that have
led (and continue to lead) to deep divisions within the city and between people.
Such planning disasters were not restricted to post-war German reconstruction.
The modernisation of existing cities by building entirely new extensions established
various spatial characteristics in American, British, European, Australian and Asian
cities. Densely concentrated tenement blocks, vast suburban sprawls and superconnecting highways were visual signs of urban growth and economic success. Tied
to economics, social compartmentalisation and separation, the future city responded
to the burgeoning middle class, dividing rich from poor, workers from industrialists,
and community representation from central authority, while ethnic groups were con -
centrated rather than integrated. Worldwide, post-war cities encouraged free enter -
prise and no action was taken against measures that would later lead to ﬂawed
urbanities. Modernist aesthetics in urban design and architecture had come far in
such a short time (pre- and post-World War I), and Germany’s bombed cities provided
the opportunity to become a leader in the ﬁeld. The revitalisation of Germany’s
destroyed cities was also viewed as counteracting the Nazi past in the belief that
reconstruction could rebuild time to point towards the future and in doing so break
with the past. The reconstruction of buildings and districts that could be saved was
incorporated into the construction of new buildings. With negotiating the past in the
urban shifts between reconstruction and building anew, the urban ﬂaw of frag menta -
tions between past and present emerged. It was a policy of urban spatial forgetting
that connects to the literary amnesia that Sebald saw operating at the same time.
Through the metaphor of the ﬂaw, Sebald’s self-anaesthesia concept can also be a
guide in developing a concept of spatial anaesthesia.
A ﬂaw generally relates to something containing a fault. A ﬂaw, then, is a defect,
a failing, an error and a blemish; it is contrary to perfection, embodying instead
Memoirs: cultural forgetting
71

## S. 87

imperfection. Conversely, things or objects are sometimes described as ﬂawless.
Flawless (without ﬂaw) is a descriptive term that can be projected onto the ‘perfect’
object; it denotes the absence of something (a ﬂaw) constituting a quality (perfection).
There are sometimes hidden ﬂaws in objects we buy and take home, only to discover
on closer inspection that the product’s function or appearance is impaired. Such
experiences are everyday occurrences. Between our preconceptions and our visual
capacity to seek out imperfection, there are problems connected with how we
experience and see an object and the object we want to see.
The object of war, its destructive capacity, is to kill people and destroy objects.
The object of reconstruction is to bring people together and rebuild objects (buildings)
as a single therapeutic act; so that people, like devastated towns and cities, can be
physically restored or ‘put back together’. On a macro level this is conducted through
the resurrection of past urban plans, and on a micro level through the reconstruction
of individual buildings. The ﬂaw comes into being when the elements that go into
making something are not aligned, in sequence or working together. In the transition
from destruction to reconstruction, various alignments in the time and history of the
city become un-sequenced. As discussed above, this time–history relational ﬂaw gave
new opportunities to re-stitch cities. In the case of Berlin, split by different nations
holding sectors of the city, the Russians controlling East Berlin and the Allies West
Berlin, planning became far more convoluted.
Reconstruction planning required vast economic forecasting and investment. The
American economist George Marshall was given the task of preparing and delivering
a strategy for the economic and planning backbone of German reconstruction in what
became known as the Marshall Plan. In a speech delivered at Harvard University in
1947, Marshall outlined the case for ﬁnancial investment from the American people
where ‘political passion and prejudice should have no part’:
In considering the requirements for the rehabilitation of Europe, the physical loss
of life, the visible destruction of cities, factories, mines, and railroads was correctly
estimated, but it has become obvious during recent months that this visible
destruction was probably less serious than the dislocation of the entire fabric of
European economy. For the past ten years conditions have been highly abnormal.
The feverish preparation for war and the more feverish maintenance of the war
effort engulfed all aspects of national economies. Machinery has fallen into
disrepair or is entirely obsolete. Under the arbitrary and destructive Nazi rule,
virtually every possible enterprise was geared into the German war machine. Longstanding commercial ties, private institutions, banks, insurance companies, and
shipping companies disappeared through loss of capital, absorption through
nationalization, or by simple destruction.38
Marshall Harvard Lecture 1947
Marshall’s plan for Germany and, more widely, Europe, was to achieve political
stability through economy, turning around the devastation consuming Germany and
most of Europe.
It would be neither ﬁtting nor efﬁcacious for our Government to undertake to
draw up unilaterally a program designed to place Europe on its feet economically.
This is the business of the Europeans. The initiative, I think, must come from
72
Memoirs: cultural forgetting

## S. 88

Europe. The role of this country should consist of friendly aid in the drafting of
a European program and of later support of such a program so far as it may be
practical for us to do so. The program should be a joint one, agreed to by a
number, if not all, European nations.
Marshall Harvard Lecture 1947
The American investment that fed into German reconstruction created the resources
to carry out the clearing of the rubble from Berlin’s streets. Given the scale of the
destruction, the speed of clearing rubble for rebuilding created the ﬁrst stage for spatial
anaesthesia. In In the Wake of War, Jeffry M. Diefendorf positions the relationships
between Nazi atrocities, the destruction of German cities and post-war urban
reconstruction and, similarly to Sebald, includes himself in these fraught connections:
I do not mean to detract from the horror of the Third Reich or the crime of Nazi
persecutions when I point out that this era lasted only 12 years and that this fact
made it easier for some Germans, themselves unaffected by the persecutions, to
emerge from it desiring only to forget and rebuild, as if the Hitler years were
some sort of natural disaster or, in the words of one commentator, ‘an industrial
accident, whose consequences one can and must erase as quickly as possible
through the restoration of former conditions’39
Diefendorf 1993, xvi
The task of rebuilding German towns and cities can be considered a way of
working towards emancipation from the past, liberating people and city through a
combination of restoration and new urban conﬁgurations. In the case of Berlin, the
ﬂuid state of the city’s destruction had already been accounted for, where a ‘systematic
reconstruction planning began as early as 1943 within an organization in Albert
Speer’s ministry’ (1993, xiv). In 1946 the German architect Hans Scharoun40 was
appointed by the Soviets as Berlin’s ﬁrst post-war chief urban planner as Director 
of the Municipal Department of Building and Housing. Scharoun, a leader in
contemporary urban planning and ‘an important proponent of modern architecture’,
observed that ‘the mechanical opening up [of Berlin] by the bombing and ﬁnal battle
gives us now the opportunity for a generous, organic, and functional renewal’41
(Diefendorf 1993, xv). In the climate of Berlin’s mass destruction, contending political
ideologies, economies and reconstruction practices were sped through Berlin:
‘[h]ousing construction, town planning, repairing of historic monuments, clearing
rubble, restructuring government ofﬁces, and rewriting building law all happened
simultaneously’ (1993, xx).
Scharoun set up his planning body, known as the Scharoun Collective, and
appointed Reinhold Lingner as Director of Landscape Planning. The Scharoun
Collective set about designing a new vision for Berlin based on a radical contemporary
plan ‘in the form of an undulating ribbon or band with much high-rise housing’
(Diefendorf 1993, 60). The Scharoun Plan, as it became known, was largely based
on abandoning the old city by proposing a rhizomatic urban thread along the River
Spree that separated living and working environments yet interconnected with
freeways. Strongly inﬂuenced by the Athens Charter42 developed by the 1933
International Congress of Modern Architecture (CIAM 4), Scharoun’s urban-scape
scheme was viewed as a failure for not addressing the urgent necessity of providing
Memoirs: cultural forgetting
73

## S. 89

solutions to the acute housing shortage in Berlin. Reconstructing the previous urban
plan and buildings that could be saved was viewed as a cheaper and speedier solution
than creating a whole and distinctly new model for the city. Under the title Berlin
Plan: Erster Bericht (First Report), Scharoun’s plan was exhibited in the damaged
Hohenzollern Palace along with proposals by American city planners:
Both sets of plans were put on public display in an exhibition mounted in a usable
area of the old Hohenzollern Palace in August 1946. Upon the Russians’ insistence
that Scharoun be dismissed because he was too apolitical for their tastes, the all-
Berlin legislature ousted him as chief planner in October 1946. The new city
government chose Karl Bonatz as his successor. Bonatz rejected Scharoun’s
approach as too impractical and opted instead for the plans developed in the
American sector.
Diefendorf 1993, 250
The decision to exhibit the Scharoun Plan, that also included ﬁve conﬁguration
models for prefabricated family homes in the ruins of the Kaiser’s Berlin residence,
is a strange choice. This location either positions the contemporary plan realised by
Scharoun as a counterpoint to traditionalism or, conversely, makes use of the palace
as a type of institutional support mechanism from the past to promote a contemporary
city plan of Berlin for the future. Neither strategy worked, however, much to the
dismay of the Scharoun camp including Otto Wagner and Walter Gropius. Bonartz
retained Lingner in his position, who continued his work to incorporate the city’s
vast amount of rubble (55,000,000 cubic metres) into his overall strategy.43
Subsequent plans were produced under Bonartz’s direction, including the Walter
Moest 1947 Zehlendorf-Plan for the Berlin suburb of Zehlendorf and the Berliner
Stadtverwaltung Plans A and B from 1946 until 1948, when the Bonartz Plan, a
variation of Plan B, was submitted. As all the plans covered the whole of Berlin, they
immediately became obsolete when West Berlin was cut off from the rest of Germany
by the 1948 Russian blockade that resulted in the formal split of the city.44
After the blockade, the Urban Planning Law (Gesetz über städtebauliche Planung)
was instigated for West Berlin in 1949. Backed up by the Zoning Plan (Flächen -
nutzungsplan) of 1950 and the General Development Plan (Generalbebauungsplan)
from the same year, one of the explicit goals was the ‘greening’ (Durchgrünung) of
the city in order to create healthier living conditions for Berlin’s inhabitants.45 In
1950, Lingner presented his Urban Landscape Concept (Stadtlandschaftskonzept)
aimed at dumping the rubble outside the city at locations where it would appear as
part of the existing glacial valley geography, thus also countering the problem of dust
clouds that formed over the city’s residential quarters when rubble was moved and
dumped within the city’s environments. Paramount to Lingner’s concept was the
topography, the geological condition as much as the biological constitution of the
soil. All designated rubble sites needed to have a low groundwater table so that there
would be no contamination seeping into the city’s water supplies, and as sand and
gravel were rare post-war building commodities all sites were examined for their
natural resources beforehand. Lingner also ensured that the city’s existing areas of
parks and gardens (most were destroyed) would not become wholesale dumping
grounds for rubble. With limited funds and a shortage of fuel, Lingner also needed
to plan for a series of inner-city rubble hills, each to become a curated urban glacial
74
Memoirs: cultural forgetting

## S. 90

valley wood (Urstromtal wald). In short Lingner put all of his gardening and landscape
skills into practice.
The planning and execution of the Marshall Plan, Berlin’s urban plan, the clearing
and dumping of rubble, reconstructing buildings, infrastructure and services in a
simultaneous retention and erasure – the urban ﬂaw settled into Berlin. Given the
national and individual self-suppression within German society and literature,
reconstruction could redirect a nation consumed by trauma and guilt but which ‘left
scarcely a trace of pain behind in the collective consciousness’ out from the depths
of destruction46.
Buckling Berlin’s physical reappearance to a new emergent urban plan, dependent
on the disappearance of parts of the old urban plan, recreated the city (like many
other German cities) as semi-historical, connected to both past and present. This
pulling between the two produced the many urban and architectural ﬂaws that scar
present-day German cities, not least Berlin. There is a sense of history moving,
turning back on the past that Benjamin describes in Angelus Novus. As an urban
casualty of this war, Berlin’s destruction and transformation to a reconstructed city
developed a selective and eventually ﬂawed connection to its past where its presence
is neither historical nor contemporary but instead out of sync with the natural
chronology of time and development of the city.47 The piecing together of Germany’s
destroyed cities worked towards reforming their identities that were more
representational and ﬂawed rather than actual and consistent. But in view of the
reality and scale of the task, was there any alternative?
The task of rebuilding Berlin was aimed at developing a new visual identity of a
city looking towards the future, yet still resting on its urban past. Reconstructing the
destroyed city is a dialogue with its physical causality48. ‘The trick then is to overturn
established conceptual oppositions and hierarchies through patient analysis while
simultaneously releasing new concepts, that can no longer be and never could be
included in the previous regime, thereby disorganising the entire inherited order and
invading the entire ﬁeld’49. Reconstructing Berlin’s urban plan was fraught from the
beginning; from the planning on paper that either involved the retracing or removal
of streets and building lots, the discord between the modernists and traditionalists and the forgetting and physiological scarring. Within these contradictions, the
Dionysian rule of destruction and creation50, the speculative role of rebuilding 
the city involved a double imagination; ﬁrst its ascending optimism from town to
metropolis, and second its receding into an unimaginable plan of oblivion. 
In Architecture and Nihilism: On the Philosophy of Modern Architecture, Massimo
Cacciari writes: ‘Imagination is not a wandering or an endless duty, but quite the
contrary: it is the analysis, the postulation, and the construction of the abstractformal model of shock’51 (1993, 59). Cacciari forms the opinion that ‘the problem
of nega tive thought becomes that of the relation between the realm of life and the
realm of forms’ (1993, 67). Here begins a model in which to rethink the reconstruction
of the destroyed city. The jettisoned Scharoun Plan for Berlin was conceived as a
ribbon along the River Spree to create the positive form for a new positive city. The
destruction of the city, the nihilism associated with it was Scharoun’s vision of hope
for the city, but reconstruction was carved out of the negative as in nihilism: ‘The
negative is indeed the force here, since it negates the existence of alternatives to this
process, and since it postulates this process as being without consolation’ (Cacciari
1993, 62).
Memoirs: cultural forgetting
75

## S. 91

Conclusion
The urban ﬂaw can be recognised when walking through Berlin, Hamburg, Dresden,
Cologne or Frankfurt – dislocations between past and present are acutely present in
all these reconstructed cities: buildings jump between times and materials, streets and
roads cut through city centres, districts composed of high-density housing and
abandoned public spaces. The dislocations between the new and the reconstructed
city ﬁx the spatial anaesthesia – urban planning that faced forward while also being
pulled back. The physical tremor from bombing raids leading to the collapse of many
of Germany’s cities, combined with the physical and psychological scarring of its
inhabitants, would redevelop towards a place for forgetting the past – an institutional,
individual and spatial anaesthesia. German cities took on human conditions of
trauma, and in the case of Berlin in particular the scarring of its destruction ignited
the disﬁguring of its urban future. Hans Scharoun and his planning team saw the
opportunity to remake Berlin and by doing so address the marginalisation and
exclusion zones between districts and social groups towards a new inclusive model.
This opportunity and many others were missed. Berlin post-uniﬁcation had the
opportunity again to mount a new urban condition for the city. Instead, much of the
planning was left to capital-driven urbanisation. Many districts continue to suffer;
Berlin’s Mitte district and the privatisation of Potsdamer Platz are cases in point.
The next chapter explores the rubble mountain of Teufelsberg, its planning,
designing and construction. Teufelsberg is a building of sorts. An anti-building that
escapes time and history, a buried city built out of amnesia – spatial anaesthesia
materialised. The destruction of Berlin required new design processes and practices
to deal with the mounds of rubble. As Director of Landscape Planning, Reinhold
Lingner was to play an important role in planning the massive task of burying the
rubble – the Trümmerberge and Teufelsberg rubble hills of Berlin grew from this
plan. It was the modus operandi to structurally deal with the effects of destruction
towards a spatial reconstitution of the city.52 This spatial reconstitution arrived at a
process of interweaving the urban ﬂaw with burial and amnesia, creating a material
‘transvaluation of values’53 and new geographies and leisure playgrounds in the city.
