## S. 96

3
Burial
Abandoning the city, physical
forgetting
Interring and disposing – human and material
In Death and Salvation in Ancient Egypt, Jan Assmann examines the connections
between life and death in Egyptian culture as a constant preoccupation that was socially
and symbolically woven into daily rituals. Living was to be in the awareness of death
and death was a means of continuing life into an afterlife. The building of monuments
in which to inter the dead also amounted to their continued presence among the living.
Assmann suggests that in Egyptian culture, ‘life displaces death, just as death annihilates
life’1 (2001, 11). Preparing for death intensiﬁed the mani festations the Egyptians
cultivated for the afterlife, placing death at the ‘origin and the center of culture’.
Death must have continually preoccupied the Egyptians – with the construction
of pyramids for kings and huge burial monuments for high-ranking ofﬁcials,
accompanied by the decoration and outﬁtting of these tombs, cenotaphs and
commemorative chapels, with the preparation of statues, stelae, offering tables,
sarcophagi, wooden cofﬁns and Books of the Dead, with the procurement of
mortuary offerings and the conducting of mortuary rituals – and we wonder how
a society that so constantly and in so many ways made death the object of all
possible actions supposedly did not accept death.
Assmann 2001, 17
In spite of their cultural preoccupation with death, the Egyptians ‘hated death, and
in a sense, they built their tombs as a countermeasure to it’ (Assmann 2001, 18). Spread
along the Nile from Alexandria to Aswan, the monuments and tombs that enshrined
the Pharaohs, high-ranking ofﬁcials and the wealthy elite have continued their
remembrance over millennia.2 The Great Pyramids at Giza on the outskirts of Cairo
still survive intact 4500 years later. Fearing death yet preoccupied with building for
death, the Egyptians also developed a culture of preservation of the dead. The Egyptian
practice of embalming the body for the afterlife was paramount in preserving the form
and identity of the dead, without which ‘death would be an absolute opposition:
isolation, termination, end, disappearance, darkness, ﬁlth, defectiveness, distance from
the divine, decomposition, dismemberment, dissolution, in short, all that constitutes
the opposite of those radiant images of a transﬁgured existence’ (Assmann 2001, 18).
Preserving the body necessitated removing ‘everything “foul”, that is, everything
perishable that could represent a danger to the goal of achieving an eternal form’3
(Assmann 2001, 32). Removing the threat of decomposition by removing the body’s
organs (including the brain, that was sucked from the skull) metaphorically mirrored
the interior encasements in the monuments and tombs housing the dead.

## S. 97

Customs and beliefs similar to those underscoring the death culture at the core of
Egyptian society are also found in ancient Greece and Rome, where death was
governed by laws controlling ceremony and ritual, as well as the disposal of the dead.
In Greek custom, the journey to the afterlife was a descent into the underworld ruled
by the god Hades. For Romans, the underworld was ruled by Orcus. In Burial
Customs, the Afterlife and the Pollution of Death in Ancient Greece,4 Francois Pieter
Retief and Louise Cilliers write:
During the moment of death, the soul (psuche) leaves the body via the mouth
or, if appropriate, via an open war wound. According to Homer other elements,
namely the thumos (from the heart) and the vital spirit (aion) in the case of young
people, also escape from the body but play no further role. The corpse that
remains simply decomposes and is of no further importance.
Retief and Cilliers 2005, 44
Greek and Roman culture considered the dead body a form of pollution, and those
who came into contact with it became polluted. ‘Everyone touching a corpse or
entering a house in which there had been a death was contaminated, as were all those
who touched them’ (Retief and Cilliers 2005, 50). The pollution that was seen to
seep from the dead was so powerful that neither people, objects, the home or
neighbourhood escaped its toxicity. ‘The house (or equivalent area) affected by a
death was regarded as polluted. Water in the house was automatically contaminated,
as was the ﬁreplace in certain regions. When a death occurred on the street, that
whole zone of the city had to be puriﬁed’ (2005, 48). The miasma surrounding the
dead (death smell, cold and clammy skin, sunken gaunt face, wasting body) transferred
to the undertaker, who, in constant contact with the dead, was always polluted.
Excluded from society to live near the burial grounds and cemeteries located on the
periphery of Roman and Greek cities, or in underground catacombs below the city,
the undertaker was a noxious yet living body of death.
The home and district of death and those who came in contact with it were cleansed
by a combination of inhumation and nature. ‘The corpse was buried wrapped in a
cloth, sometimes enclosed in a cofﬁn. In the case of cremation, the ashes (in a
container) were also buried in a grave. According to Cicero (Leg. II.25, 63), grain
was traditionally strewn over the grave after it had been ﬁlled in’5 (Retief and Cilliers
2005, 54). Pollution would be expelled from the dead body via the seepage of its
ﬂuids into the earth; through plants sown over the grave, the pollution was drawn
up via the roots, carried through branches and into the veins of leaves where it would
be breathed out and carried away on the wind. The polemic of ‘pollution and purity’
surrounding the dead’s family would ﬁnally be resolved after their return from the
grave, where ‘they were puriﬁed with ﬁre and water in the ritual of sufﬁtio, before
their reintegration was complete’6 (Bradley 2012, 31). The process of puriﬁcation
(the seeping of pollution into soil, the drawing up and cleansing through roots and
leaves) metaphorically connected the dead to the changing of the seasons. In autumn
and winter ﬂora withers and lies dormant, in spring and summer it sprouts and
ﬂowers. Purifying the dead in tune with these lifecycles ensured an image of the dead
in partnership with nature.
The differences between the culture of death in Egyptian society that sought
inclusion yet maintained fear, and the Greek and Roman culture of peripheral
82
Burial: physical forgetting

## S. 98

exclusion, offer a starting point for thinking about human burial and the buried city.
The pyramids, tombs, altars, sarcophagi and stelae that characterise Egyptian, Greek
and Roman burial culture offer various associations between the buried city and the
burial of the dead. Human burials and the buried city both inter something that has
died, but to opposite ends: the monument-grave is designed for remembering the dead
person, while the buried city is designed for disposing of and forgetting the destroyed
city. An elevated death mound covered with the nature of the forest, the buried city
is tomb, altar and sarcophagus where ‘isolation, termination, end, disappearance,
darkness, ﬁlth, defectiveness’ lie buried. The processional funerary architecture aligned
with geographical sites and major arterial roads in Greek and Roman cities still leaves
its mark, overshadowing the abhorrence of the interred decomposing body. In The
Dominion of the Dead, Robert Pogue Harrison writes: ‘Like human dwelling, the
afterlife needs to take place in. If humans dwell, the dead, as it were, indwell – and
very often in the same space’7 (2003, x). While the hill of Teufelsberg marks the
ground on which the buried city lies in, it nevertheless gives no sign of being funereal,
processional or scenographic. Not composed of the dead, yet polluted with destruc -
tion, the buried city became a humic architecture; fragments of concrete, brick,
mortar and stone. Harrison considers the idea of humic (dead matter) architecture:
I would expect a humic architecture of this sort to be vertical in the downward
and not only upward sense, and its rising to be more of an emerging than a
soaring. I would expect it to be mortal and metrical, haunted in its visible aspects
by the earth in which the existential and historical past ultimately come to rest.
By that I do not mean that it would be funereal, commemorative, or selfconsciously pious toward the has-been, but that its building would sink in to
and emerge out of the recesses of mortal time, or out of the subterranean abodes
of the dead, in whom the ‘spirit’ of place ultimately resides.
Harrison 2003, 35–6
The monumental interments and expulsions of the dead in Egyptian, Greek and
Roman burial provide an indication of how the destroyed city would be expelled
from Berlin, interred at Teufelsberg so that reconstruction could begin. The sowing
of grains and plants on the graves of Greek and Roman dead can likewise be
connected to the planting of saplings over the grave of the buried city. The expulsion
of the destroyed and dead city to the outer boundaries of Berlin poses the question,
more than 70 years later: What might be the afterlife of the buried city at Teufelsberg?
Harrison’s concept of a humic architecture (the marking of ground through the
placing of the dead) is also connected to the marking of law. As the dead mark 
the ground where they lie, this also preserves ownership of that claim to ground into
the future. ‘A humic foundation is one whose contents have been buried so that they
may be reclaimed by the future. [. . .] In a broader sense it means to store, preserve,
and put the past on hold’ (2003, x–xi). As Teufelsberg marks the ground in which the
buried city lies, it nevertheless lies unclaimed.8 Historically cities have claimed the
ground on which they stand, building over the foundations of previous buildings 
that connect the sites of the dead to the places of the living. ‘For as long as we think
of our houses, cities, and nations as merely places to live, and not as places to die,
those houses, cities and nations can never become homes or take their stand within
the limits of containment from which all shelter and placehood ultimately derive’
Burial: physical forgetting
83

## S. 99

(2003, 35). Harrison’s assertion that homes, as much as cities, are sites not only for
the living but also for the dead assumes that burial equals building as a means of
claiming ground by human occupation. In another of Harrison’s works, Forests: In
the Shadow of Civilization, he further links burial and claiming of ground by suggesting:
Burial guaranteed the full appropriation of the ground and its ultimate
sacralisation. Through burial of the dead the family deﬁned the boundary of its
place of belonging, rooting itself quite literally in the soil, or humus, where
ancestral fathers lived underground . . . Burial preserves in its soil the essence of
humanity. So while the universal institutions of humanity derive their law from
the sky, they must ultimately take root in the ground. This is a paradox indeed,
for by turning toward the openness of the sky humanity commits its essence to
the enclosure of the earth.
Harrison 1992, 7
Through its disappearance, the buried city at Teufelsberg is denied any passage
to the afterlife by transformation or remembrance. Becoming part of the geographical
landscape, it disappeared not as a grave but as a forest. Over time, the pollution 
of the destroyed city would be drawn up through the roots of trees and via their
canopy of leaves, expiring as ether in the air. The Egyptian, Greek and Roman prac -
tices of burial displayed both reverence and fear of death and the dead. As much as
seeking the gaze of the living, the monuments were built as a type of gaze of the
dead from the grave. The monumental undertaking of burying the destroyed city,
which ensured its invisibility and removal from the public eye, would also give access
to walking its terrain. Located 17 kilometres from the centre of Berlin, the buried
city lies in an unmarked grave claimed by nature and visited by the public. As
geography and landscape, the buried city is experienced above ground, a place to go
into nature. Not formed over time, but a mimicking of nature which – like any other
park – provides the public with pleasure and relaxation.
Conclusion
In 1784 the French architect Étienne-Louis Boullée planned a funerary memorial to
capture – through building – the English physicist and mathematician Sir Isaac
Newton’s laws of motion and gravity. Boullée’s unrealised project, Cenotaph for
Newton, consisted of a colossal sphere set on a massive plinth that could convey the
scale of Newton’s universal conception of the cosmos. Having its origins in Greek
(kenos meaning empty, taphos meaning tomb), Boullée’s grand design for Newton is
comparable in scale to both the Great Pyramid of Giza and the Great Hall (Grosse
Halle) in Berlin conceived by Albert Speer for the Nazis. Boullée’s empty tomb for
Newton was designed to enrapture the public within a Newtonian universe that could
provide two distinct experiences. During the day, the vast interior would draw light
through holes punctured in the sphere arranged to symbolise the cosmos. At night,
at the centre of the dome’s ceiling, an armillary sphere (a globe of intersecting metal
bands representing the relationships between the sun, earth and planets, as well as
the latitude and longitude of the universe) would appear radiant with light. Below
this, positioned centrally at the base of the dome, was to be Newton’s sarcophagus.
Though empty, for Newton’s body lies interred at Westminster Abbey, the sarcophagus
84
Burial: physical forgetting

## S. 100

metaphorically symbolises Newton’s resting place, appropriately within the universal
realm of his discoveries, and in France rather than his native England. Boullée was
one of the key architects of the Ancien Régime, and the size of his memorial to Newton
showcased advances in building technology that could now exponentially scale up
interior volumes to the monumentality of ancient Egypt, Babylonia, Greece and
Rome.9 Structurally at odds, Boullée’s interior universe (a massive void) and the buried
city (a massive mound) connect metaphorically via their inner and outer surface shells.
Higher than the Great Pyramid of Giza or Speer’s Great Hall, Boullée’s homage to
Newton was nevertheless designed to disappear, its aspiration to capture the universe
appearing as scenography. Combining geography and landscape, the buried city
appears camouﬂaged as a forest. Conceived by a young German landscape architect
who after WWI was responsible for designing and maintaining German war graves
and who during WWII was an expert in camouﬂaging military installations, the buried
city became landscape architecture in the form of a biomorphic vision.
Building out – the Urstromtal in biomorphic vision
Devastated and depopulated urban landscapes, coupled with the huge task of
reconstruction after the end of the war, created a forum for rethinking existing
traditions of urban planning. Returning to Jeffry M. Diefendorf’s In the Wake of War,
he states that the ‘consciousness of reconstructing bombed cities faded away and was
replaced by a conceptualization of urban change as part of a broader and more general
process of growth and modernization’ (1993, 11). The scale of devastation of
approximately 131 German cities and towns was ‘so great that in some cases authorities
proposed abandoning the bombed cities entirely and building new cities, perhaps
underground to ensure protection from air raids’ (1993, 11). Diefendorf’s detailed
logistical account of the equipment, human resources and labour costs (calculated in
Reichsmarks/RM) for clearing rubble and for salvaging and processing building
materials is astounding, absorbing and disturbing in terms of scale and enterprise.10
A national association of experts on rubble processing (Deutsche Studien gesell -
schaft für Trümmerverwertung) was founded to collect information and dis sem -
inate advice. Many cities had ambitious plans for rubble salvaging. Münster calculated that about 40% of the rubble could be salvaged: 10% in intact building stones,
2% in usable sandstone and about 30% in whole bricks or pieces of brick that
could be processed into wall blocks or rooﬁng tiles. Ministerialrat B. Wedler, the
leading proponent of rubble processing in Berlin, presented the following argument
for a massive commitment to processing in that city. He estimated that it would
take 25 years to clear Berlin’s rubble using 42,000 workers continuously. This
would cost RM 2.6 billion. Utilising hand-sorting techniques, materials worth RM
450 million could be salvaged. However, if 35 efﬁcient rubble processing plants
with an hourly capacity of 25–30 cubic metres were built, then rubble clearance
over the same 25-year period would require the labour of only 2,600 workers,
half of whom would be women, and the total cost would be RM 650 million.
Because the value of the salvaged and reprocessed building material would be RM
650 million, the rubble clearance would actually pay for itself. (The complicated
political situation in Berlin in fact prevented any such massive program from being
implemented.)11
Diefendorf, 1993, 27–8
Burial: physical forgetting
85

## S. 101

Diefendorf provides tables of outgoings and incomings to comprise a set of statistics
that illustrates the efﬁciency German planners applied to the task of reconstruction.
Calculations were accurately drawn up, providing business-type planning whereby
‘the rubble clearance would actually pay for itself’ through recycling of materials and
stressing the numbers of female workers involved, ‘probably because women would
be paid less than men’ (1993, 296). This reﬂects the lack of recognition women
received in post-war reconstruction. Women pulling rubble from bombed-out build -
ings, cleaning mortar from bricks and pushing Trümmerbahnen (small railway wagons
set on temporary tracks) laden with debris may have appeared to deserve less pay
than the men, but the efﬁciency of their work enabled reconstruction to proceed at
a pace where ‘by the end of December 1945, Berlin had already salvaged 50 million
bricks, enough to put up 2,500 dwellings’12 (1993, 28).
Diefendorf’s many tables contain some astonishing numbers, but they do not
provide a tangible visualisation of the millions of cubic metres of rubble. This is
provided by the two images he uses to illustrate how many truck- and trainloads of
rubble were pulled from Berlin’s streets and transported to construct the city’s rubble
hill. Although toy-like in their graphic idiom, these train-set images provide a refer -
ence point in visualising the scale involved. Sifting through 20 years’ worth of memos
between the departments responsible for carrying out Teufelsberg’s construction, 
one senses a collective and cooperative relationship in undertaking this massive task.
Figure 18 Enttrummerung im Bezirk Tiergarten, August 1945-Juni 1950 FRep.290-II-1408
Landesarchiv Berlin
Photo: Gert Schutz
86
Burial: physical forgetting

## S. 102

Some memos, on the other hand, indicate a back-and-forth correspondence of noncompliance, where each department raises its concerns over diverting scarce resources
to Teufelsberg’s construction, thus jeopardising other reconstruction projects of equal
signiﬁcance.
The early conception of Teufelsberg and Berlin’s inner city Trümmerberge as sites
for the burial of the city’s destruction was part of the ﬁrst stages of reconstruction
planning. As Berlin’s ﬁrst Chief of City Planning, architect Hans Scharoun presented
his concepts at a public exhibition in 1946 at the bomb-damaged Royal Palace in
the centre of the city. His visionary plan was largely based on abandoning much of
Berlin in favour of planning a modern city situated along the River Spree. The
response was divided between Berlin’s planning traditionalists, who advocated
retaining and reconstructing, and modernists like Walter Gropius and Otto Wagner,
who viewed Berlin’s destruction as an opportunity to conceive a new urban model
for the city. Scharoun’s plan failed to capture the public’s imagination, nor did it
gain the support of the Allies or the Russians; as a result, it was abandoned and
Scharoun was ﬁred. His successor, Karl Bonartz, set about instigating a rationalist
reconstruction strategy in line with the wishes of the Allies, especially the Americans,
who were providing huge ﬁnancial resources under the Marshall Plan for Berlin, and
the Russians who were concentrating their efforts on the eastern sector of the city.13
Bonartz retained the Scharoun-appointed landscape architect Reinhold Lingner as
Head of the Berlin Main Ofﬁce for Landscaping (Leiter des Berliner Hauptamtes für
Grünplanung) who continued to develop his plans for managing the city’s vast piles
of rubble, then estimated at 55 million cubic metres. In 1949 the Law for Urban
Planning (Gesetz über städtebauliche Planung) was launched, forming the basis of West
Figure 19 Transportleistung in der Enttrummerung Nov. 1950 FRep.290-II-1416
Landesarchiv Berlin
Photo: Gert Schutz
Burial: physical forgetting
87

## S. 103

Berlin’s urban model in a city divided since 24 June 1948 by the Russian blockade.14
Backed by the Zoning Plan (Flächennutzungsplan, 1950) and the General Development
Plan (Generalbebauungsplan, 1950), one of the explicit goals of both measures was
the Greening (Durchgrünung) of the city to ensure healthier living conditions for
Berliners.15 There was a general consensus by Bonartz, Lingner and the associated
planning authorities that clouds of ﬁne dust (Feinstaub) forming over the city as a
result of the continuous removing and dumping of rubble at inner suburban sites posed
a serious health hazard to the city’s inhabitants. In 1950, Lingner presented his
Landscape Concept (Stadtlandschaftskonzept) that advocated the dumping of rubble
away from Berlin’s residential quarters in sites outside the city where the rubble could
be integrated with the existing natural geography (Urstromtal).16 Located 17 kilometres
outside of Berlin and by far the largest of these sites, Teufelsberg is a consequence of
Lingner’s rubble planning concept, which by that stage was already underway.
In Lingner’s concept, the topographical and geological conditions of the site and
the biological constitution of the soil were factors of paramount importance. Sites for
rubble dumping were selected for their low groundwater level to avoid contamination from the rubble sweeping into the city’s water supplies, while sites containing
then scarce building materials, such as sand and gravel, were avoided so they could
be mined.17 This also allowed Lingner to deﬂect calls to expand dumping on sites
including the city’s existing parks and gardens. In the face of ﬁnancial constraints, he
secured outside dumping sites, individually curating the landscaping of Berlin’s rubble
hills (Trümmerberge) in keeping with the natural landscape (the Urstromtal). Lingner’s
rubble and greening strategies were integral to his biomorphic vision (biomorphistische
vision) in reconstituting landscapes from destruction. This vision aimed to place the
population’s well-being ﬁrmly in the hands of landscape architecture, connecting city
and burial with geography and reforestation towards a physiological and humancentred antidote to the horrors of war.
Lingner was a trained gardener (gelernter Gärtner) and had been Head of the
German War Graves Commission (Leiter der Amtlichen Deutschen Kriegsgräber -
fürsorge) in Belgium in the 1920s. He was only in his mid-twenties at the time of his
appointment, and one might assume this to have been his formal professional training
in landscape architecture.18 In 1933 when the Nazis came to power, he was dismissed
for his anti-fascist views (he was also married to a committed communist) and began
to work as a freelance landscape gardener in Germany and abroad. During World
War II, he was drafted into Organization Todt led by Fritz Todt who, as Minister for
Armaments and Munitions, undertook the civil engineering and military building
programmes of the Third Reich such as the autobahn and later the bunkers of the
Atlantic Wall.19 Besides his experience with war graves, Lingner also specialised in
camouﬂage plantation (Tarnpﬂanzungen) and, on being reinstated into the German
military, was sent to the Eastern Front to camouﬂage military installations. The
parallels to be drawn between Lingner’s earlier jobs at the War Graves Commission
and as a camouﬂage expert engaged in hiding military installations and his later work
as one of the architects of Teufelsberg and Berlin’s inner city Trümmerberge cannot
be understated. Combining his knowledge of landscape and plantation with his bio -
morphic vision, Lingner would use his knowledge of cultivating landscapes for the
dead (war graves) and concealing military installations with plants to create the camou -
ﬂaged ruin landscapes of the buried city. With this spatial anaesthesia for forgetting
the destruction of the city through burial, Berlin’s destruction was laid to rest.
88
Burial: physical forgetting

## S. 104

The various maps and models included here were produced and dated after
commencement of Teufelsberg’s construction, making up for a lack of documentation
detailing its planning that would shed light on the design processes employed by
Lingner to achieve his biomorphic vision concept. From an architectural, building
and spatial perspective, it is worth considering how the surface area of the site was
calculated in relationship to the amount of rubble to be buried. The site chosen within
the Grunewald forest in the outer western district of Wilmersdorf consisted of an
already substantial section of ground cleared for Speer’s Military Faculty complex.
Lingner had originally proposed a site lying a few kilometres south of Teufelsberg,
known as Paulsborn. In the report Greening the Rubble Dumps (Begrünung der
Restschutthalden) presented to the Committee on Debris Recycling (Ausschuss für
Trümmerverwertung) in 1947, Lingner had already begun to see the rubble hills as
a model for public use and seasonal entertainment. Lingner was to declare that ‘a
large toboggan and ski hill is planned at Paulsborn in the Grunewald’20.
The shift from Paulsborn to Teufelsberg next to the Teufelssee (Devil’s Lake) may
have been for two reasons. First, the Teufelsberg site was better suited to his natural
geography concept (Urstromtal) and second, the site contained Speer’s unﬁnished
Military Faculty. This second fact may seem to conﬂict with Lingner’s biomorphic
vision of a landscape of therapeutic well-being, prompting a review of his landscape
theory. As the intellectual centre of Nazi militarisation, Speer’s Wehrmacht Faculty
building was an ever-present symbol of the recent Nazi past. A number of attempts
to blow it up had failed due to its superior construction. It could thus be speculated
that the selection of the Teufelsberg site over Paulsborn would provide the oppor -
tunity to bury this remnant of Nazi Germany under the buried city. A more complex
reading of the site may be given by involving Lingner’s anti-fascist and pro-communist
politics to propose that Speer’s building remain intact to serve as a cautionary
reminder of the evils of National Socialism for future generations. To date, I have
seen no documents that prove such a thesis and as such it remains purely speculative;
it is a thesis I arrived at by reading Lingner’s character and social politics into his
landscape architecture, and from his reports to various municipal bodies21. According
to undocumented speculations, he may also have proposed that the destruction of
Berlin be remembered and experienced via a sectional slice through Teufelsberg that
would partly reveal the scale of the rubble mountain and serve to remind future
generations of the destruction of war22.
Linking Speer’s Wehrmacht Faculty building and Lingner’s humanist politics as
embodied in his landscape practice is problematic in terms of how the two men viewed
death and the site of the dead in relationship to the city. Sharing the ancient Greek
and Roman preference for separating the dead from the living and urban context,
Speer’s proposals for Germany’s Third Reich capital Germania located cemeteries
outside of the city. Lingner, on the other hand, viewed death and the place of the
dead as integral to the life of the city. In his 1947 report, Aufgaben und Ziele der
Grünplanung (Mission for Green Planning), he highlights the distinction between his
and Speer’s views23:
In Speer’s plan, cemeteries would have been huge installations on the periphery
or outside the city limits. This would have placed excessive burdens on trafﬁc
due to the need for long bus and train rides, making it more difﬁcult to visit
graves. In our urban landscape there is a place for cemeteries; they are not foreign
Burial: physical forgetting
89

## S. 105

bodies, being organically connected to other green spaces. Paths and avenues cross
them, and loosely grouped trees and shrubs turn solemn burial grounds into scenic
landscapes.
Lingner 1947
Locating cemeteries within inner city urban environments rather than on the
periphery, Lingner afﬁrms his belief that the city is a shared composition between
urban density and landscape, the living and the dead, combining this with the rational
argument of avoiding unnecessary trafﬁc congestion. We can imagine Lingner’s ﬁrst
site-visit to Teufelsberg, seeing Speer’s Wehrmacht Faculty and picturing the rubble
mountain of the buried city within and around it. This calls for a speciﬁc sensibility
in visualising landscapes. Is it the same as that required to visualise a building, a
town or a city? This may remain in the realm of speculation, but it should be kept
in mind when considering the massive building/un-building project at Teufelsberg.
Constructing Teufelsberg would call for a mental rendering of a non-existent
landscape on the site, an act of projection like that required of a painter. The paintings
of the 19th-century German artist Caspar David Friedrich, such as Mountain
Landscape with Rainbow (1810), The Wanderer above the Sea of Fog (1818) and
Village Landscape in Morning Light (1822), depict landscapes of a distant mountain,
a man perched on top of a hill and a solitary tree in a ﬁeld. Referring to Friedrich’s
Figure 20 Teufelsberg Wilmersdorf Grunewald Abriss der Wehrtechnischen Fakultät,
11.12.1951 Landesarchiv Berlin F Rep.290–172814 Berlin Landesarchiv
Photo: Friedrich Seidenstucker
90
Burial: physical forgetting

## S. 106

1828 painting Trees and Bushes in the Snow, Joseph Leo Koerner in his book Casper
David Friedrich and the Subject of Landscape argues that Friedrich’s formal focus
is on the experience of landscape rather than on depicting any actual landscape or
location:
The grove and thicket themselves betray nothing of their speciﬁc geographical
site. Indeed Friedrich’s radical reduction of the visual image to an isolated
fragment of insigniﬁcant nature functions precisely to obscure the landscape’s
legibility as topography or ‘view’.24
Koerner 1990, 16
The ﬁgures in Friedrich’s landscape paintings often stand with their backs turned,
taking in the same vista seen by the viewer. Whether lit by moonshine, the sun or
winter light, ‘these anonymous subjects mark the landscape before them as “something
viewed”‘ (Koerner 1990, 89). By connecting ﬁgures within the landscape to their
viewing of the beyond, as if looking beyond the painting itself, Friedrich creates a
personalised view of the landscape both from behind them and through their eyes.
Humankind and nature in simultaneous connection and contention – the human in
nature and nature within the human.
Lingner’s idea of landscape architecture involved uniting the public with nature even
in a distressed landscape such as the buried city. Lingner sought a nature that could
transcend destruction not by forgetting it, but by creating digniﬁed tomb craft
(gediegenes Grabmalhandwerk). Teufelsberg provided him with the opportunity to
formulate a landscape from the destruction of the city that could transform the wellbeing of its citizens, both present and future. Carved from this biomorphic vision of
nature and human well-being, his curated natural geography and forest would be fulﬁlled
as a landscape crypt and forest dedicated to the destruction of the city. This remaking
of the dead, cleared city as a natural living environment would place nature and
landscape architecture at the highest degree of art, an artistically ﬂawless (künstlerisch
einwandfrei) natural experience.
Good model graves and sculptor training programmes will create a culture of
tasteful funerary sculpture. We have designed models for wood and stone grave
markers and passed them on to the trade as examples. At the new cemeteries,
we will allow only technically and artistically ﬂawless graves, and, in collaboration
with the city’s Dept. of Adult Education and the Academy of Fine Arts on
Kaiserallee, we have launched a school for funerary sculptors. Its brief is to train
craftsmen who see beyond the individual grave to a larger unity, responsibly
shaping the cemetery as a whole. Great emphasis is placed on literacy.25
Lingner 1947
Lingner’s ‘tasteful funerary sculpture’ not only links back to his time at the German
War Graves Commission, but also suggests a correct burial culture involving artistic -
ally ﬂawless graves. Lingner’s polemic on grave culture connects with his biomorphic
vision through a landscape of reconciliation, a deep and meaningful landscape that
could transcend the evils of the Nazi past and provide a transformation aimed at
societal and individual well-being. As a grave for the dead city, Teufelsberg connects
to the polluted dead body feared by the Greeks and Romans that was puriﬁed by
Burial: physical forgetting
91

## S. 107

sowing crops, just as the pollution from the buried city ﬁltered through the planting
of the forest. Both body and city dissolve into the ground and into the air – each a
releasing of trauma. Human burial becomes city burial.
The task of constructing Teufelsberg was not simply a matter of dumping and
piling up rubble. The hill’s design and construction was a complex and rational
process. Like many of Berlin’s inner-city Trümmerberge, Teufelsberg was built from
the outside in. Rubble would be ofﬂoaded from trucks from the outer circumference,
ﬁlling the inner void over time. Conceived on a grand scale, Teufelsberg’s construction
took 25 years and saw 26 million cubic metres of rubble dumped on the site. Taking
on epic proportions in the history of building, Teufelsberg would become twenty
times greater in volume than the Great Pyramid at Giza. Imagining Teufelsberg
required Lingner and his associates to turn away from the city of Berlin so as to
orientate the location of the rubble hill on the site. Metaphorically, such a turning
of backs reiterates Walter Benjamin’s reading of Paul Klee’s 1920 painting Angelus
Novus, where the ‘storm irresistibly propels him into the future to which his back
is turned while the pile of debris before him grows skyward’26 (Benjamin 1968, 257).
It also connects to the ﬁgures in Friedrich’s landscape paintings who look beyond
the scene to the horizon. Lingner’s biomorphic vision, it could be suggested, places
the human into the landscape and further beyond the horizon; landscape as inner
and outer well-being and as therapeutic repair bringing the history of destruction
back into the human.
The origins of the rubble mountain at Teufelsberg may be identiﬁed from a number
of sources. Maps of the site would have indicated overall terrain and conditions, and
as scaled representations they could provide the surveyor with accurate measurements
of site coverage and perhaps (calculated using the amount of rubble in cubic metres)
height. ‘Elevation is the least durable element of urban form’, suggests Denis Cosgrove
in his essay Cartocity Mapping and Urban Space; in his view, mapping the city is a
form of ‘urban archaeology’ that ‘reconstructs urban places through peeling back
successive surfaces, like the pages of an historical atlas, and mapping the stratigraphy
of material deposits stretched across the former urban space’27 (Cosgrove 2006, 148).
The 1952 coloured map of Teufelsberg details the hill’s topographic lines in increments
rising to 120 metres. Titled Debris Dump (Restschuttkippe), this 1:1000 map
demarcates the territory of the buried city while also indicating, through gradient
colouring, its topography: gradients of green outline the buried city’s circumference
at the base, moving inwards in gradients of brown that get darker towards the highest
point of the hill. Taking on an appearance of an island ﬂoating in the sea, the map
presents the buried city in winding concentric lines indicating its topographical rise
like any other natural geography. By inverting its representation, the map could also
present a plan for an open-cut mine; a spiralling hollow of drilling down, where the
apex becomes the lowest depression, the maximum extraction depth of ore. Spatial
theorist Edward Soja suggests that spatial representations of ground such as maps
are ‘another form of environmental or geographical determinism’. He asks: ‘How
can ﬁxed and dead urban geographies shape the dynamic development of social
processes, social consciousness, social will?’28 (Soja 2003, 275).
Thinking through Soja’s ‘geographical determinism’, it could be a way to
understand what Lingner may have been asking himself: how can landscape turn
‘dead urban geographies’ into ‘dynamic development of social processes, social
consciousness, social will?’ Not knowing what processes he used to superimpose his
92
Burial: physical forgetting

## S. 108

biomorphic vision on reality, how he went about imagining the vast inﬂation of the
ground caused by millions of cubic metres of rubble, relay the limitations of
cartography’s factual recording of ground. In The Agency of Mapping: Speculation,
Critique and Invention, James Corner suggests that ‘devices such as frame, scale,
orientation, projection, indexing and naming reveal artiﬁcial geographies that remain
unavailable to human eyes. Maps present only one version of the earth’s surface, an
eidetic ﬁction constructed from factual observation’29 (1999, 215). Yet, the twodimensional coloured map of Teufelsberg conveys a three-dimensional inﬂation of
ground still earthed as ground. As Corner attests, mapping is a speculative measure -
ment of the earth’s surface as much as it is a quantitative representation revealing
geographical spatial compositions of landscape, towns and cities. ‘Mapping is neither
secondary nor representational but doubly operative: digging, ﬁnding and exposing
on the one hand, and relating, connecting and structuring on the other’ (1999, 225).
By the time the 1969 map of Teufelsberg was drawn, the 1952 map of the rubble
hill had become a constructed reality. Aptly named Debris Storage Place (Restschutt -
lagerplatz), the new map offers more intricate details of topography, outlining the
American listening station at its apex and tracing paths that wind around the hill.
Both maps ratify the successful disappearance of the buried city; other than the title
there is nothing to suggest the contents of Teufelsberg. With the browns and greens
of the ﬁrst, plus the detailed buildings and pathways of the second, the two maps
Figure 21 Resschuttkippe am Treufelssee, 16.06.1952 F Rep.290-A-9177–3
Photo: Landesarchiv Berlin
Burial: physical forgetting
93

## S. 109

connect in my mind to Lingner’s biomorphic concept of creating from the rubble a
human-made natural landscape. To further stress the naturalism of Teufelsberg,
Lingner proposed early in the planning stages that the height of the buried city would
allow for a ski-jump (Sprungschanze) and a toboggan run (Rodelbahn), which actually
feature on the 1969 map. ‘In this sense, mapping is returned to its origins as a process
of exploration, discovery and enablement. . . . Like a nomadic grazer, the exploratory
mapper detours around the obvious so as to engage what remains hidden’ (Corner
1999, 225).
The forest at the Teufelsberg site, some of which had already been cleared for Speer’s
Wehrmacht Faculty, was replanted on an elevated ground-plane covering the buried
city. This swollen earth of concrete and brick and human-germinated forest is the
realisation of Lingner’s landscape vision of well-being out of destruction and annihi -
lation. To return to The Dominion of the Dead, Harrison ascribes the annihilation of
human imagination to the eschaton of the earth’s demise:
Here a landscape of retrospection sponsors a transhuman or even inhuman vision
of the eschaton, understood not only as the end of history but as the surcease
of the earth itself – that same earth where history’s ruins leave behind their trace.
Figure 22 Restschuttlagerplatz Teufelsberg, Mai 1969 F Rep.290-A-9177–7
Photo: Landesarchiv Berlin
94
Burial: physical forgetting

## S. 110

A truly extreme or self-consuming vision of annihilation takes the form of the
earth’s demise, for the forces of destruction, when pushed to their comic extreme
in the human imagination, not only destroy all that human labor builds in time,
they also destroy the supporting element of time, namely the land on which we
erect our worlds.
Harrison 2003, 3–4
Harrison’s ‘surcease of the earth itself’ can be also applied to Teufelsberg. As well
as becoming a playground with tobogganing and skiing, the buried city was enveloped
back into the forces of destruction with the building, at the height of the Cold War,
of the American Military’s Listening Station in 1963. Consisting of two spheres and,
between them, an extruded section capped by a dome reaching a height of 45 metres,
the Listening Station appears ostentatiously phallic. Taking advantage of the elevation
the buried city provides, Western Europe’s furthermost eastern surveillance was
tasked with tracking the communications and military movements of the Soviet
Union’s forces. As such, the Teufelsberg installation would have been a primary 
target in the event of a war between East and West. It seems that the buried city 
can desist from being a part of Berlin’s history, but not from its roots of destruction.
As a remilitarised site, Teufelsberg would again be connected to its origin of violence
– destruction in this case perpetrating destruction.30
The envisioning, planning and construction of Teufelsberg allowed the trans -
formation of the destroyed Berlin into an under-earthed buried city. As a space of
Figure 23 Teufelsberg Radarstation der US Streitkrafte F Rep.290-C8795, Sommer 1976
Photo: Landesarchiv Berlin
Burial: physical forgetting
95

## S. 111

destruction amassed as geography, Teufelsberg calls for a new philosophical
engagement with the buried city within the contemporary discourse of human
geography. In What is Philosophy?, Gilles Deleuze and Félix Guattari argue that
philosophy is not singular, regular or in union with locational, social, political or
cultural entities, but severed from its setting, embracing universality. ‘Philosophy is
a geophilosophy in precisely the same way that history is a geohistory’31 (1994, 95).
Deleuze and Guattari’s geohistory is formulated by philosophical universalities
inherent in an ancient Greek idealist worldview of culture and history. Taking this
on board, it could be assumed that the geohistory of Teufelsberg is geophilosophy
of human destruction and burial. The geohistory of Berlin’s reconstruction speaks to
the geophilosophy of Teufelsberg’s construction. Inseparable, Berlin and Teufelsberg
shape both the city’s geography and its reconstruction, forming a single binding
history. The city is reconceptualised as livable in and livable on – each a claim that
transforms the other.
Although concepts are dated, signed, and baptized, they have their own way of
not dying while remaining subject to constraints of renewal, replacement, and
mutation that give philosophy a history as well as a turbulent geography, each
moment and place of which is preserved (but in time) and that passes (but
outside time). What unity remains for philosophies, it will be asked, if concepts
constantly change?32
Deleuze and Guattari 1994, 8
Strongly inﬂuenced by Fernand Braudel, one of the founders of the French
philosophical school of historiography, Deleuze and Guattari’s geophilosophy is an
ever-forming geopolitical world history. In A Thousand Plateaus – Capitalism and
Schizophrenia, they incorporate into their collective thinking an undoing or rejection
of history stemming from the formulaic productions of writing history. Citing the
German philosopher Friedrich Nietzsche’s antagonism to the production of history,
they write:
Nietzsche opposes history not to the eternal but to the subhistorical or super -
historical: the Untimely, which is another name for haecceity, becoming, the
innocence of becoming (in other words, forgetting as opposed to memory, geog -
raphy as opposed to history, the map as opposed to the tracing, the rhizome as
opposed to arborescence).33
Deleuze and Guattari 1987, 296
Regarding the buried city, the haecceity Deleuze and Guattari refer to can be linked
to the universality of histories involving the destruction of cities throughout time:
ancient cities like Carthage, Thebes, Mycenae, Sodom and Gomorrah; the con ven -
tional and atomic warfare destruction of cities during WWII; today’s destruction of
Aleppo and Gaza City; and the predictable destruction of other cities to come. The
destruction of human-built urban environments has been realised through everincreasing achievement in devising technologies of destruction. As the site of the buried
city, Teufelsberg recounts the history of destruction by virtue of its relationship with
human burial, and this geohistory, I would argue, has yet to be written. Teufelsberg’s
haecceity contains both history and a geophilosophy of the human capacity for
96
Burial: physical forgetting

## S. 112

destruction. Reinhold Lingner’s acute understanding of the natural geography in which
the city is buried (Urstromtal) inherently speaks of his philosophical and phenom -
eno logical understanding of the effect that landscapes have on the human psyche and
well-being. Lingner’s biomorphic vision strongly connects a geophilosophy to what
the buried city represents below and above ground. Writing the geohistory of the
buried city means claiming it as the grave of Berlin, thus marking ﬁrst its locational
history and then its universality.
Conclusion
In many ways, the buried city is a return to the beginnings of humankind’s connections
with the forest. Historically, culturally and mythically signiﬁcant within German
history, the forest provided shelter and refuge, protecting German tribes north of the
Rhine from centuries of Roman invasion. Referring to the writings of the Roman
historian Tacitus (AD 56–unknown), Giambattista Vico asserts that the origins of the
division of forests between German tribes were deﬁned by the placement of their
burial urns, ‘which we may view as the origin of the later distinctions between cities,
peoples, and ultimately nations’ (Vico [1744] 1999, 8). Tacitus’s observations of the
German tribes, recorded in Agricola and Germania (AD 98), support Vico’s claim
regarding law emerging from the forest, drawing extensively on the rhizomatic
relationships between burial, ground and law through both rational and mythical
dimensions. ‘Through protracted settlement and the burial of their ancestors, they
came to found and divide the ﬁrst dominions of the earth. The lords of these domains
were called giants, a Greek word which means ‘sons of the earth, or descendants of
the buried dead’ (Vico 1999, 9). Without acknowledging a speciﬁc German tribe such
as the Osi, Treveri, Nervii or Batavi, Tacitus gives what can only be a general account
of German burial customs:
In burial there is no ostentation: the single observance is to burn the bodies of
their notables with special kinds of wood. They build a pyre, but do not load it
with palls or spices: to each man his armour; to the ﬁre of some his horse also
is added. The tomb is a mound of turf: the difﬁcult and tedious tribute of a
monument they reject as too heavy on the dead. Weeping and wailing they put
away quickly: sorrow and sadness linger. Lamentation becomes women: men must
remember.34
Tacitus 1914, 301
For the Romans who viewed the earth as their dominion, ruling its provinces to
their will, the German tribes’ settlements within the chaos nature of the natural forest
provided their protection (especially those north of the Rheine) and to some degree
independence. Merging Tacitus’s observations with his own contemporary appraisal,
Vico establishes the principles of human evolution as a series of staged progressions.
‘Here is the order of human institutions: ﬁrst forests, then huts, next villages, later
cities, and ﬁnally academies’ (Vico 1999, 98). Now divided from Berlin as the burial
urn of the destroyed city, the buried city invites the contemporary historian to reestablish its origins in the histories of cities. Summoning the mythological beginnings
of the world where gods roamed a Greek centred ‘geohistory’, Vico places burial at
the core of the human recording of ground.
Burial: physical forgetting
97

## S. 113

The buried city remains as a plan for a city of emergence, with new laws and
patterns yet to be mapped. Challenging and extending Cosgrove and Corner’s concepts
of mapping indeterminate space, the buried city remains unclassiﬁed. From city to
buried city, destroyed Berlin’s transition is tied to the silent self-anaesthesia diagnosed
by Sebald. Teufelsberg’s construction was a success story in hiding the visible effects
of war. The public undertaking of building the rubble mountain can be viewed as a
working through of trauma and materiality in erasing the history of the destroyed
city.
Erasing the city – materiality of trauma
Photographs taken during the construction of Teufelsberg show much dirt, rubble,
bricks and other building materials, along with trucks, truck drivers, shovels,
handshakes and beer. The photographs document the various milestones in removing
the rubble from Berlin’s streets and its carting and dumping at Teufelsberg. The
photographs also show a sense of satisfaction in the faces of the individuals and
government representatives involved. The celebratory mood conveyed in the female
truck driver smiling broadly perched on the bonnet of her truck, reﬂected in a man
dressed in suit and coat reaching up to shake her hand, is underscored by a sign
framed in laurel leaves attached to the truck’s grille. Containing a single digit followed
by a number of 0s and, at the end, m3, the sign indicates in millions of cubic metres
the amount of rubble carted to Teufelsberg. The year is 1957 and the amount
displayed is 10,000,000 m3. This, the ﬁrst in a series of photographs of Teufelsberg’s
construction, is followed by pictures of trucks with signs showing 21,000,000 m3
(1968) and 25,000,000 m3 (1971), each sign framed by laurel leaves, a handshake
and beer.
The institutional and literary silence referred to by Sebald, ‘shown by a community
that seemed to have emerged from a war of annihilation without any signs of
psychological impairment’, can be witnessed, in my view, in the various photographs
of Teufelsberg’s construction. Collectively forming a united mission to bury their city,
the photographs also display a melancholia that would deﬁne post-war German
culture. According to Sigmund Freud, melancholia is ‘a reaction to the loss of a loved
object’35 (Freud 1957, 245). His observations on human behaviour resulting from
the loss of valuable connections between self, others and objects are pertinent in
relating the buried city to human burial. Freud’s investigation of melancholia, where
‘the object has not perhaps actually died but has been lost as an object’, is nevertheless
relevant in thinking about Teufelsberg and the people who built it over 25 years.
Informed by the individual’s death instinct, melancholia is materially summoned over
those years of construction. In stark black and white, the photographs show a work
ethic that characterises a nation in the depths of erasing its past so that it can redirect
and overcome melancholia by turning towards the future.
The visible trauma and the labour of the women and men working in the ruins
of their destroyed city combine to form a receptivity for collective mimesis. Walter
Benjamin’s concept of the cultural conditions allowing an individual to assimilate
society’s collective idealism, later expanded by Theodor Adorno’s mimetic concept
regarding uncritical relationships between individuals and society, are part of the
hidden trauma in the day-to-day burying of the city. In Camouﬂage, Neil Leach puts
forward the idea that ‘the potential loss of self bring[s] us to the question of how
98
Burial: physical forgetting

## S. 114

Figure 24 Teufelsberg Frau Anatoniazzi und Bausenator Rolf Schwedler F Rep.290–56575,
14.11.1957
Photo: Landesarchiv Berlin

## S. 115

mimesis, in its demands for an assimilation with the inanimate world, reveals a link
with the death instinct36. Paraphrasing ﬁlm theorist Miriam Hansen, he writes that
the action of mimesis ‘involves the slippage between life and death, the assimilation
of life-less material (as in the case of the chameleon) or feigning death for the sake
of survival’ (Leach 2006, 25). The speed applied to ridding Berlin of destruction and
building anew underlies the hiddenness of trauma in post-war German consciousness.
In Trauma, Absence, Loss, Dominick LaCapra suggests that trauma is enacted
between movements of absence and loss:
Indeed in post-traumatic situations in which one relives (or acts out) the past,
distinctions tend to collapse, including the crucial distinction between then and
now wherein one is able to remember what happened to one in the past but
realize one is living in the here and now with future possibilities.37
LaCapra 1999, 699
LaCapra proposes the idea that ‘the very ability to make distinctions between
absence and loss (as well as to recognize its problematic nature) is one aspect of a
complex process of working-through’ (1999, 699). Both absence and loss, it can be
assumed, are played out in each of the photographs from the moment of their viewing.
The conversion of absence into loss gives anxiety an identiﬁable object – the lost
object – and generates the hope that anxiety may be eliminated or overcome. By
contrast, the anxiety attendant upon absence may never be entirely eliminated
or overcome but must be lived with in various ways.
LaCapra 1999, 707
The collection of archive photographs contained in this book mark moments in
the daily work of burying the city. Each marks a day in creating the absence of the
city and, with each material contact, a connection to trauma is enacted. Contact with
the materiality of destruction enabled a collective therapy of hiding trauma within
the body.
Everyone is subject to structural trauma. But, with respect to historical trauma
and its representation, the distinction among victims, perpetrators, and bystanders
is crucial. ‘Victim’ is not a psychological category. It is, in variable ways, a social,
political, and ethical category. Victims of certain events will in all likelihood be
traumatized by them, and not being traumatized would itself call for explanation.
But not everyone traumatized by events is a victim. There is the possibility of
perpetrator trauma that must itself be acknowledged and in some sense worked
through if perpetrators are to distance themselves from an earlier implication in
deadly ideologies and practices. Such trauma does not, however, entail the
equation or identiﬁcation of the perpetrator and the victim.
LaCapra 1999, 723
In Haunting Legacies – Violent Histories and Transgenerational Trauma, Gabriele
Schwab suggests that trauma ‘as a mode of being violently halts the ﬂow of time,
fractures the self, and punctures memory and language’38 (2010, 42). Schwab connects
trauma with cryptography, deciphered through a metaphorical spatial volume inside
100
Burial: physical forgetting

## S. 116

the body where the memory of trauma experienced lies buried. ‘A crypt could thus,
for example, be built as a tomb for one’s lost self’ (2010, 45). Schwab’s body-crypt
analogy works by way of sealing traumatic experiences inside oneself, giving rise 
to an inability to mourn. Schwab asserts that ‘there can be collective crypts, com -
munal crypts, and even national crypts’ (2010, 46). The trauma within the civil
population that resulted from the destruction of German cities would become a
national task in burying it deep within the individual’s consciousness, for there could
be no mourning for the perpetrators of the war and the terror they inﬂicted.
Referencing Alexander and Margarete Mitscherlich’s The Inability to Mourn:
Principles of Collective Behavior, Schwab reafﬁrms the German people’s inability 
to mourn, accompanied by an ability to work through trauma by their physical
interactions with materials.
Alexander and Margarete Mitscherlich were the ﬁrst to theorize the inability to
mourn experienced by the German people after World War II. The frantic
Wiederaufbau, the rebuilding of Germany after the war, was a manic defence
that covered an equally desperate construction in the national psyche, the secret
erection of a crypt in which the Germans buried what they had lost yet denied
having lost: their sense of themselves as a human people.39
Schwab 2010, 46
Figure 25 Trümmerfrauen bei der Pause, 1946, F Rep.290–38741
Photo: Landesarchiv Berlin
Burial: physical forgetting
101

## S. 117

The Mitscherlichs’ reference to the ‘frantic rebuilding of Germany’ suggests a
society hurtling through trauma with the pace of reconstructing its towns and cities.
Through the process of rebuilding they were also building-out the horrors of the Nazi
past, thus regaining ‘their sense of themselves as a human people’. Schwab’s human
crypt metaphor is a spatial turn in how to think about Teufelsberg, not just as the
burial chamber of the destroyed city but also as a society’s ‘national psyche’.
Referencing Nicolas Abraham and Maria Torok’s The Shell and the Kernel, Schwab
points to their theory of cryptonymy that traces different ways and forms of inner
meaning of words that hide within the intention of language. ‘“Cryptonymy” refers
to operations in language that emerge as manifestations of a psychic crypt, often in
form of fragmentations, distortions, gaps, or ellipses’ (2010, 45). As the burial site 
of the destroyed city, Teufelsberg contains absence and loss in the millions of frag -
ments of the former city. In Death, Memory and Material Culture, Elizabeth Hallam
suggests that ‘the tensions, ambiguities and contradictions integral to material
environments that have undergone a form of “trauma” or dislocation, can be worked
out through further interactions with material objects in social practice’40 (2001, 103).
Teufelsberg’s cryptonymy is the trauma of its landscape. It combines melancholia,
trauma and national psyche into a distressed landscape – the buried city. ‘The crypt
contains the secrets and silences formed in trauma. Accordingly, the secret conceals
a trauma whose very occurrence and devastating emotional consequences become
Figure 26 Enttrummerung eines Ruienengrundstuckes, 1947 F Rep.290–173732,
Landesarchiv Berlin
Photo: Friedrich Seidenstucker
102
Burial: physical forgetting

## S. 118

entombed and consigned to internal silence by the sufferers’ (Schwab 2010, 45). As
the site of the remnant city, Teufelsberg transforms the hiddenness of trauma and
material destruction through burial. As landscape formed from material destruction,
Teufelsberg separates its trauma through the surface of its forest from its foundations
of destruction. The trauma on which Teufelsberg is built could be revealed and
accessed by scraping back its surface. Undertaking an archaeological investigation
into the ground, unearthing once lived spaces, would open the invulnerable crypt to
the vulnerability of national psyche that performed the burial of their city.
Conclusion
In his essay Forensic Architecture: Notes from Fields and Forums, written for
dOCUMENTA (13) held in the German city of Kassel in 2012, Eyal Weizmann
describes the transformation of architecture where ‘buildings undergo constant
deformations: structures are said to “behave” in response to forces, and buildings
are said to “perform” (or mis-perform) in relation to program’ (2012, 7). The
‘deformations’ placed on the city of Berlin – the destruction of its buildings – exposed
the city to a constant state of trauma. As the physical material memory of Berlin’s
destruction, the buried city performs geography through a natural variation of the
topography surrounding the site. Like any geographical mass, the buried city created
is in constant movement in and between ‘fragmentations, distortions, gaps, or ellipses’.
It suggests the ground of the buried city in a constant state of movement, forming
and reforming:
It takes years for trapped air bubbles to make their way between paint layers
and structure; the path and rate of their crawl depending on larger environmental
conditions and their constant ﬂuctuations; walls gradually bend and ceilings sag.
Deterioration and erosion continue the builders’ processes of form-making.
Cracks make their way from geologic formations across city surfaces to build -
ings and architectural details. Moving within and across inert matter and built
structures, they connect mineral formations and artiﬁcial constructions. They
appear and disappear, continuously translating force contradictions into their lines
of least resistance. The structural pathology of a building is a diagram that records
the inﬂuence of an entangled and potentially inﬁnite political/natural environment,
registering year-on-year temperature changes, almost imperceptible ﬂuctuations
in humidity and pollution, which are themselves indications of political
transformations, patterns, and tendencies.41
Weizmann 2012, 7–8
Weizmann offers a possible prelude to a writing of the buried city. His suggestion
that ‘deterioration and erosion continue the builders’ processes of form-making’
allows for a rethinking of Teufelsberg not as the dead-city-site but a site under trans -
formation. Each photograph of Teufelsberg’s construction builds towards a covering
over of the trauma of the destroyed city. Through their material connection with the
rubble, I have suggested that the men and women who cleared it are self-portraits
of Berlin’s ruination. Similarly, the workers building Teufelsberg could be viewed in
the positive light of self-achievement in the burying of their city. The clearing, carting and dumping of the rubble form the passages to the materiality of trauma; the
Burial: physical forgetting
103

## S. 119

workers ‘entombed and consigned’ in their work ‘to internal silence’ within their
bodies. The materiality of trauma hides within the body-tombs of the people working
through the destruction of their city. The men and women involved in constructing
Teufelsberg performed the role of the undertaker on the periphery of an ancient city.
A city razed to the ground, Teufelsberg is Berlin’s Carthage that put a destitute people
back in charge of their city. For many, their work was a constant reminder of the
pollution the Nazi regime had wrought on their city and nation. Speeding through
reconstruction was a way to physically eliminate the pollution. Post-war recon -
struction afforded little time to confront either national or personal trauma. To work
through trauma, emotionally and psychologically, was to still be in the grasp of war
and the horrors unleashed. Burying the city created a pathway to the future, to remove
destruction and to hide trauma.

1 For an in-depth account of the practices of Egyptian burial practices see Jan Assmann,
Death and Salvation in Ancient Egypt, trans. David Lorton (Ithaca, NY and London:
Cornell University Press 2001).
2 Napoleon’s 1798–1801 military expedition to conquer Egypt created an unforeseen
outcome in an extensive documentation of the ancient monuments of Egypt. Through
sketches and maps, Napoleon’s army engineers, draftsmen and artists known as savants
recorded the monuments where they were found; many buried beneath mounds of sand.
Highly detailed in their execution, the drawings are exquisite in their depiction and proved
a great hit with the French public when published under the title Description de l’Égypte
in 1809. The early archaeological excavations of sculptures and objects during the
expedition not only led to their wholesale removal and transportation back to France but
also to a revival of ancient Egypt, its history and spectatorship in the Le Grande Tour of
the 19th Century. Napoleon’s expedition was a military failure. Nevertheless, in
‘conquering’ the cornerstone of ancient civilization he returned victorious to the French
public and crowned himself Emperor of France. See Gillispie, Charles C. and Michel
Dewachter, Monuments of Egypt, the Napoleon Edition (New Jersey: Princeton
Architectural Press, 1987).
3 Further to his description of removing ‘everything perishable’ from the body, Assmann
also outlines the following process: ‘The dead body as membra disiecta was thus a
metaphor, a metaphorical image of death that served as a foil for the image of life, into
which the dead body was to be transported by carrying out the embalming ritual. The
initial, surgical-anatomical phase of this seventy-day process was not free of violent
intrusion. The body was of course not cut into pieces, but for the purpose of preserving
it, it was treated anything but ‘conservatively,’ in the medical sense of the term. The brain
was ﬁshed out through the nose, and the internal organs were removed through an incision
in the side. Only the heart was wrapped up and set back in place; the other organs were
placed in canopic jars, vases with lids in the form of an animal or human head. The
remaining soft tissue and ﬂuids were dissolved by a solution of natron and resin and pumped
out of the body through the anus. This ﬁrst phase was carried out in the name of
puriﬁcation’. See Jan Assmann, Death and Salvation in Ancient Egypt (Ithaca, NY and
London: Cornell University Press, 2001).
4 See Francois Pieter Retief and Louise Cilliers article in ‘Burial Customs, The Afterlife and

was further exacerbated by the fact that Berlin’s parks and gardens were all destroyed and
that every remaining tree in Berlin’s large central park the Tiergarten was chopped down
