## S. 124

4
Disappearance
Planting the forest, natural
forgetting
Walking Teufelsberg is to be a part in overwriting the history of Berlin. It is also the
built reality of proposals post-1945 that ‘advocated bulldozing the rubble, planting
grass and shrubbery, and allowing a new natural landscape to emerge’ (Diefendorf
1993, 11). Returning to my photographic archive, images of women and men, boys
and girls show them planting saplings in the hard ground at Teufelsberg, portraying
a powerful scene of a people within the ravaged garden. From these saplings, trees
now cover Teufelsberg’s surface in a canopy of green. Years later, other images show
people taking a Sunday stroll or ﬂying a kite on Drachenberg (Kite Hill). These
seamless displays of enjoyment on the surface of Teufelsberg shadow the history of
the site, its history of ruin and destruction towards appeasement with history.
Following Chapters’ 1, 2 and 3 interpretations of the critical, cultural and physical
practices of forgetting, Chapter 4 combines them to forward the concept of natural
forgetting. With the completion of burying the rubble from 16,000 buildings forming
a hill rising to 120 metres, Chapter 4 focuses on Teufelsberg’s upper surface and the
planting of the forest. The chapter establishes how the planting of a forest hides 
the city of Berlin to rewrite its devastation. The chapter explores then not burial 
but the surface of burial. As much as clothes cover the surface of our own bodies,
and as much as skin hides and, in some ways contains, the internal working of our
bodies, the surface treatment of Teufelsberg is essential to understand the completeness
of burying destroyed Berlin.
Covering over – the psychotope of destruction
One of the ﬁrst things I noticed on moving from Melbourne to the former East Berlin
district of Friedrichshain in the winter of 1996 was the streetlight at night, or more
precisely, the lack of it. In the dim light from the few operating streetlamps, the heavy,
grey, stucco-rendered facades that covered almost every building created a shadowy
atmosphere.1 It was gloomy, bleak, claustrophobic, eerily attractive – and the patchy
lighting was sufﬁcient to get me home. The mood of Friedrichshain’s streets, the
shadowy play of light and dark, recalled German expressionist ﬁlms of the 1920s
like Robert Wiene’s The Cabinet of Dr. Caligari or Fritz Lang’s Die Nibelungen.2
These stark streetscapes contrasted with the over-lit suburban streets of Melbourne.
The private and public funds that ﬂowed into many of Berlin’s eastern districts
after German reuniﬁcation transformed their grey facades and dimly lit streets.
Twenty-ﬁve years on, these same streets are now lined with renovated buildings
painted in heritage pastel colours and amply illuminated by working streetlamps.

## S. 125

Before this makeover, there was something captivating about walking Friedrichshain’s
barely lit streets that made me aware of the night sky. Along with having no difﬁculty
in adjusting to the darkness and visual aesthetic of those streets, I felt more visually
connected to the overall environment, the ground, the buildings and the sky. This
crepuscular scene made me think of a time without electricity, of oil lamps and candles,
and of writers, composers and craftspeople working by their ﬂickering light. Walking
through Friedrichshain’s streets now lined with buildings painted in pastel shades
tells me that those evenings of dim shadows and night sky are irrevocably lost.
The other visual marker I became aware of when I moved to Friedrichshain was
the absence of trees. I became a little obsessed with the new plantations of young
linden, chestnut, maple and plane trees during the mass renovation of buildings, streets
and districts. Before, I hadn’t fully appreciated what trees do to a city. Of course, I
had always seen trees in cities. In Delhi they offered shade and hope in recycling the
carbon dioxide from the overwhelming smog. In New York’s Central Park they gave
a personal scale in contrast to the towering colossi of steel and glass. But it hadn’t
really clicked in my mind how much trees add visually and aesthetically to the city’s
environs and sense of well-being. Years later, having moved to another country, I
returned to Berlin during the summer, an experience I hadn’t had for more than ﬁve
years. Driving from the airport to my home in Prenzlauer Berg, I was overwhelmed
by the amount of trees and their green presence. Following the layout of streets and
the in-between space of the median strip, trees dominated the view from the window
of the moving car. Given that this greening of the built environment is a humanmade and controlled forest-landscape, the visual presence of the trees exerted a visual
territory equal to any of the buildings.
In his book Landscape and Memory, Simon Schama begins with a search for a
forest in the wilderness of north-eastern Poland, an area known as the Puszcza. ‘I
had come to Poland to see this forest. See what, exactly, I wasn’t sure’ (1995, 24).
Schama’s search was to ﬁnd one of the oldest natural forests in Europe, yet one that
was also riddled with human history from centuries of European conﬂict.
The ﬁelds and forests and rivers had seen war and terror, elation and desperation;
death and resurrection; Lithuanian kings and Teutonic knights, partisans and
Jews; Nazi Gestapo and Stalinist NKVD. It is haunted land where greatcoat
buttons from six generations of fallen soldiers can be discovered lying amidst the
woodland ferns.
Schama 1995, 24
As Schama suggests, the Puszcza woodland has been the site of struggle between
‘death and resurrection’ but also of inhabitation and cultivation of people living within
the realm of the forest; landscapes are ‘culture before they are nature, constructs of
the imagination projected onto wood and water and rock’ (1995, 61). Later in the
book he looks at the German forests of the Rhine and, as was the case with Vico,
turns to Tacitus’s observations of the German tribes north of the river who dwelled
within the forests that provided them with food, shelter and protection. With their
knowledge of this domain, the German tribes were able to blend into the dark-light
of the forest and stalk the approaching Roman Legions, defeating them by stealth
and surprise.3 ‘The country in general, while varying somewhat in character, either
bristles with woods or festers with swamps’4 (Tacitus 1948, 141). The woods Tacitus
110
Disappearance: natural forgetting

## S. 126

Disappearance: natural forgetting
111
refers to are part of the Hycerian Forest, the extents of which were unknown. ‘At
least one map of Roman roads, attributed to Castorius, showed “Allemania” at the
perimeter of the known world, terminating in a barrier of trees’ (Schama 1995, 83).
Tacitus’s ﬁrst-hand observations of the German tribes in their forest habitat yield
a history of the visible and the invisible. Visible, for where the forest is no longer
present and, with Roman victory, fear no longer felt, he can observe and record what
he sees. Invisible, for the forest remains a mystery to those unable to decipher in its
shadows, streams and terrain the coexistence of the human and natural worlds,
something at odds with the Roman self-image. For the Romans, nature, like their
dominions, needed to be under constant control. Yet, as the natural condition of the
forest is chaos (i.e. it exists by the laws of nature, not by laws imposed by humankind),
it has moved within the human built world. Desecrated to make clearings for villages
and towns, the forest has also fought back to retake ground it once occupied. In
Forests, The Shadow of Civilization, Robert Pogue Harrison tells of Rome’s
ﬂuctuations between city and forest over millennia of disappearances and
reappearances.
As the city disintegrates from within, the forests encroach from without. The
ancient city of Rome, whose destiny so preoccupied and fascinated Vico, was
eventually claimed by the forests, ﬁrst by analogy, then in the form of forestpeoples from the north, and ﬁnally the vegetation belt itself.
Harrison 1992, 13
Partly through the lens of Tacitus, the descriptions of human relationships with
the forest offered by Harrison, Schama and Vico, be it the Puszcza of north-eastern
Poland or the Hycerian Forest of the German tribes, all reﬂect the historical and
cultural signiﬁcance of forests from ancient to modern times. But distinctions can be
made between the three writers: for Harrison, the forest is the ever-present landscape
surrounding the city, ready to regain its ground; as discussed in Chapter 3, he posits
the forest as a foundational, earthed relationship between human and ground via the
spatial marking and ancestral claims of burial. Schama, on the other hand, is interested
in the story of the forest, foregrounding its manifestations in the histories of cultural
exchange between people and nature, landscape and representation in art and
literature. Vico’s interest focuses on the laws governing the forest that were abandoned
and re-established in the settlements emerging from the forest to the open ﬁeld. Vico
believes that the inherent chaos of the forest, its natural plan, corresponds to the law
of the forest as reorganised in the establishment of towns and cities. What links their
concepts is the mythopoetical hold that forests and landscapes have had on European
political, social and cultural development.5 Schama takes multiple, winding paths
through the forest and upon reaching a clearing offers multiple views of what has
been experienced. Harrison tells of landscapes of contestation; between earth and
sky, cultivation and dwelling, burial and ancestral claiming of ground. Vico positions
the forest as the ﬁrst site of human existence from which it emerges into the clearing,
forming new patterns for ordering new societies.
In the German language Wald (forest) and Welt (world) are interchangeable in
relation to the territory of the forest. Forest becomes a substitute for the ‘world of
the forest’, a world unto itself. According to Vico, ‘ﬁrst there were forests, then
cultivated ﬁelds and huts, next small houses and villages, thence cities, and at last

## S. 127

academies and philosophers’ (1999, 15). The exodus of communities from the forest
into open clearings brought new opportunities for the ordering of societies. No longer
connected to the nature of the forest, controls on space and living were imposed on
people in exchange for protection. By ﬂeeing the world of the forest, the common
binary that bound people together was now forfeited to the power of individuals and
ownership over ground. By exchanging the random nature of the forest for planned
dwellings within walled towns, the forest became excluded as the primal source of
life and, over time, the people were cut off from the founding core of their myths. As
villages grew into towns and towns into cities, the emergence of law would transform
itself again into the radical chaos of the constructed metropolis.
As one of the primary thinkers and exponents of Radical Constructivism, Ernst
von Glasersfeld developed his concept of viability (Viabilität) – the study of the viable
potential of things, objects or entities. He uses the idea of the path as a metaphor
for the potentiality of variables in traversing the forest terrain:
A blind hiker who wants to reach the river on the other side of a not too dense
forest can ﬁnd many paths between the trees that lead to his destination. Even
if he were to walk through the forest a thousand times and memorise each of
his chosen paths, he would have not a picture of the forest but a network of
paths that will take him to his destination by avoiding the trees in the forest.
From the viewpoint of the hiker, whose experience consists only of walking and
occasionally colliding with trees, this network is no more and no less than a
representation of all the possibilities for getting to the river that he has realised.
Assuming the forest doesn’t change too fast, the network shows the hiker where
he may walk. But it tells him nothing of the obstacles between which these
successful routes lie, apart from the fact that they occasionally hindered his
walking. In this light, the network ‘ﬁts’ the real forest, but the surroundings
perceived by the blind hiker contain neither trees nor forest of the kind visible
to an external observer. Instead, they consist only of steps successfully executed
by the hiker and steps that were blocked by obstacles.6
Glaserfeld 1992, 19
Glasersfeld’s concept of viability, as illustrated by the blind hiker’s sensing space
rather than looking while navigating his way through the forest, is an account of the
variable functions of logic and knowledge. In an article in Theater der Zeit, the
German playwright Heiner Müller (1929–1995) tells the story of a ﬁght between
Bulgarian partisans and Nazi fascists in a forest, with both ﬁnally being driven out
by its protector – the forester.
Perhaps I might tell a joke about this: ‘From the diary of a Bulgarian partisan.
Monday: we drive the fascists out of the forest. Tuesday: the fascists drive us
out of the forest. Wednesday: we drive the fascists out of the forest. Thursday:
the fascists drive us out of the forest. Friday: the forester drives us and the fascists
out of the forest.’ One must therefore look very carefully at the foresters!7
Müller 1996, 19
Müller’s ethical and morally superior protector of the forest, who does not takes
sides, expels all invasive conﬂicts foreign to the forest domain. Similarly, without
112
Disappearance: natural forgetting

## S. 128

causing any damage, Glasersfeld’s blind hiker, unable to see the trail, forges through
the forest taking one of multiple possible pathways by ‘bumping into trees’. Müller
and Glasersfeld’s allegorical tales create a spatial mindset concerning the psychological
ties between human and nature, protection and destruction, navigation and sensing8.
In Psychotope, Between Human and Nature, the German nature psychologist Norbert
Jung refers to the work of German-American architect Richard Neutra, suggesting
that the psychotope, a certain spatial mindset of being, is present in the design and
construction of his architecture.
The experience that places have a ‘spirit’ or ‘atmosphere’ (as it was called by the
philosopher Hermann Schmitz and explained by the scientist and philosopher
Gernot Böhme in 1995) is not tied to ancient cultures or shamanic, non-conscious
(subliminal) perception. In the modern architecture of the 20th century, the
architect Richard Neutra dealt, on the basis of his observations and experiences,
with the mental and neurophysiological effects of interiors, exterior architecture,
integration into the environment, city skylines and built spaces in general. He
found that every built (environmental) structure, as well as every landscape, has
a speciﬁc mental and emotional resonance, pleasant or unpleasant, thus creating
psycho-physiological changes in humans, such as arousal or harmonization.9
Jung 2014, 2
According to Jung, ‘the psychotope is a place where mental processes occur’. Jung
also explores the concept of the biotope, which is ‘a place where organic processes
occur’ (2014, 3). Jung’s psychotope and biotope theories of the human and nature,
where the organic life of nature is associated with the logic of the mind, formulate
the mind in nature and vice versa. Jung points out that the psychotope and biotope
of Neutra’s architecture embrace the biorealism existing between architecture and
nature.
Neutra referred to this architectural style as ‘biorealism’. The link between
architecture and the experience of nature is us ourselves: our inescapable natural
tendencies to respond psychophysically and emotionally to all environmental
structures.10
Jung 2014, 2
Jung’s remarks on the psychological resonance of Neutra’s architecture can also
be related to The Inability to Mourn. As mentioned in Chapter 3, Alexander and
Margarete Mitscherlich suggest that the inability to mourn is caused by the
psychological impact of signiﬁcant events in curtailing our emotive and sensory
abilities. Similarly re-enforcing the Mitscherlichs’ theory, in his book Force Fields
Martin Jay suggests ‘that mourning as a complete working through of lost material
is itself a utopian myth. That is, the hope of ﬁnding a means to transcend the
repetition and displacement characteristic of apocalyptic melancholia is necessarily
doomed to failure’11 (1993, 97). The psychological conditions that prevailed in postwar German societies amounted to an inability to mourn when faced with the
overwhelming horrors committed by the Nazi regime.
As a former Head of the German War Graves Commission in Belgium and as a
camouﬂage expert on the eastern front during WWII, Reinhold Lingner was well
Disappearance: natural forgetting
113

## S. 129

Figure 27 Schttkippe an der Teufelsseechaussee Anfahrtsstrasse und Kontrollhauschen
Teufelsberg Wildersdorf Grunewald, 30.4,1957, F Rep.290–53748

## S. 130

aware of how landscape and plantation can be used both to remember the war dead
and to mask the destructive forces of military installations. His biomorphic vision,
siting rubble debris within naturally existing landscapes where the organic life of the
forest takes hold, incorporates both the biotope and psychotope in the transformation
of landscapes of trauma into landscapes of well-being: embracing the biotope would
bring about a return to the chaotic nature of the forest, providing both well-being
and new ways of negotiating these terrains of destruction and trauma as metaphorical
spaces with regard to the recent horror of the war.
The national self-suppression of emotive and sensory abilities in post-war
reconstruction would, by default, be worked through in clearing rubble from the
country’s towns and cities. The psychotope (mental processes) deployed in working
through ruination could allow the biotope (organic forms) to emerge from the buried
remnants of mass destruction. Eventually, and within the realms of nature, the
biotope would cloak destruction, removing it from visibility. Arguably, both psycho -
tope and biotope prevailed in Berlin’s post-war reconstruction. The construction of
new human-made landscapes for the disappearance of the destroyed city was to
become the deﬁning achievement in removing the scars of the war. Through the burial
of rubble and the planting of nature, a psychological characteristic could be threaded
within the reconstructed city through landscape architecture, achieving a different
kind of mourning, one through nature. As an example of designed mourning, the
Figure 28 Schttkippe an der Teufelsseechaussee Anfahrtsstrasse und Kontrollhauschen
Teufelsberg Wildersdorf Grunewald, 30.4.1957, F Rep.290–53749
Photo: Landesarchiv Berlin
Disappearance: natural forgetting
115

## S. 131

landscape of Teufelsberg forms the disappearance and absence from sight and mind
of destroyed Berlin to establish the psychotope and biotope of the city as geography.
In 1952 Lingner published a book containing his biomorphic landscape theories.
Titled Landschaftsgestaltung (Landscape Design) and co-written with his wife Alice,
Lingner ﬁrst pays homage to one of the founders of modern landscape architecture,
the 19th-century landscape architect and designer of Berlin’s Tiergarten, Joseph Peter
Lenné.12
Berlin’s famous Tiergarten park, whose trees sadly fell victim to the war, was
also created by him (Joseph Peter Lenné). He was a visual artist with an extra -
ordinarily ﬁne sense of the nature of his materials – living plants. Such creations,
just as much as architectural structures, express the level of culture of our
people.13
Lingner 1952
Lingner’s tribute to Lenné and his ‘extraordinarily ﬁne sense of nature’ is intended
not only to identify his practice with Lenné’s but also to put landscape architecture
on a par with sculpture or painting. Lingner’s view of landscape architecture as an
art form may well have been shaped during his time at the War Graves Commission
and his meeting with the sculptor Käthe Kollwitz. ‘There he met Käthe Kollwitz,
whose son was buried at Roggeveld cemetery and who had created the sculpture
Grieving Parents for him. Together they looked for the right location for the
sculpture’14 (Michaels 2011, 23). Lingner saw landscape as more than the inanimate
matter of earth, plant and geography. For Lingner it was an organism bound up with
the living, ‘to heal wounds struck by human hand in the landscape’15. Lingner’s
reference to landscape and wound is key to his translation of the psychotope in
repairing the city and its inhabitants. It was a joint exercise of reconciling trauma’s
experienced through an exchange between nature and people that would heal by
concealing man-made destruction. In essence, Lingner placed his trust in nature’s
capacities as psychotope and biotope to cloak the destructive nature of humankind.
In Natur und Industrie im Sozialismus: Eine Umweltgeschichte der DDR, Tobias
Huff cites Lingner’s landscape concept as integral to East Germany’s socialist ideology
of the time. Huff describes his ideas and concepts as embracing multiple connections
between landscape, the city and people. ‘Throughout his professional life, Lingner
dealt with parks and green spaces, cemeteries, rest homes and the planning of urban
spaces’16 (2015, 47). On the founding of the German Democratic Republic in 1949,
Lingner, a committed socialist married to a committed communist, moved to Berlin’s
eastern sector and began planning the Trümmerberge and landscaping of green spaces
in that part of the city. At the heart of Lingner’s philosophical landscape concept
was a deep social connection between landscape and people that sought a ‘resolution
of the conﬂict between man and nature’ (2015, 40). Yet Lingner’s landscape vision,
that enabled disappearances of destruction from the public realm through designed
landscape, met with hostility from those who claimed that such strategies created
romantic notions out of the city’s ruination.
What might appear as a sensible objection was formulated in July 1948 by the
East German magazine Bildende Kunst [Fine Arts] with the aim of launching a
campaign against ‘romanticizing and demonizing ruins’ since such phenomena,
116
Disappearance: natural forgetting

## S. 132

in the author’s view, were expressions of a perverse bourgeois relationship with
destruction. The theme of rubble was dominating the studios; people tended either
to ‘derive aesthetic and sentimental appeal from ruins’ or to distort them via a
demonic or ghostly slant. As ‘almost welcome components’, he wrote, the ruins
were used to ‘to decorate a morbid, self-psychoanalyzing inner life’.17
Embraced and nurtured under the new East German regime, Lingner’s urban
landscape concept sought a link to Marx and Engels’ economic model of an integrated
egalitarian society, grounding landscape within the political and social ideologies of
the GDR.
The concept of landscape diagnosis and the life of its creator, Reinhold Lingner,
were inextricably linked to the establishment of the East German state. Lingner
tried to develop his landscape planning in dialogue with Marxist theory. In doing
so, he was guided by ideas on the relationship between humans and the
environment derived from his reading of Marx and Engels.18
Huff 2015, 39
Ahead of his times in the practice of landscape architecture in both the sociopolitical and public arena, Lingner was later to be credited as a pioneer. ‘Lingner
was at least ten years ahead of West Germany’ (2015, 43).
Figure 29 Künstlische Aufschüttung aus Trümmerschutt Teufelsberg Wildersdorf
Grunewald, 20.9.1966 Landesarchiv Berlin F Rep.290–115667
Photo: Hans Seiler
Disappearance: natural forgetting
117

## S. 133

An important advocate of this interpretation of a planned economy was Reinhold
Lingner. His landscape diagnosis allows an analysis of the spiritual physiognomy
of the SED leadership in the post-war period. To the highest party committees,
he presented a plan, based on Marxist positions, that made concrete proposals
for shaping the relationship between humans and nature in the GDR.19
Huff 2015, 43–4
Under Lingner, the post-war period was a transformative landscape, from
destruction to reconstruction, via nature. Nature could not only transform the sites
of destruction but also be a restorative place of well-being for the people. The positive
subliminal affects of nature on human well-being had previously been tested by the
German composer Richard Wagner, with an avenue of trees and shrubs on the long
walk up to the Bayreuth Festival Theatre. Wagner believed such a walk in nature
would allow a different connection to time and space, as a way of forgetting the day’s
events and preparing to experience the psychological resonance of his musical dramas.
In This is the Garden of Eden (Det is der Garten Eden), Igor J. Polanski refers to
the psychology of concealing Berlin’s destruction in a biological melding of building
and landscape. Seen in the light of Jung’s psychotope and biotope of mental and
organic processes, Polanski informs us that Lingner’s biomorphic vision was a
balancing act, combining landscape and people in a natural physiological exchange.
Figure 30 Teufelsberg Wilmersdorf Grunewald, 20.09.1966 F Rep.290–115668,
Landesarchiv Berlin
Photo: Wolfgang Albrecht
118
Disappearance: natural forgetting

## S. 134

The primary objective of the Berlin Parks Programme was thus to create
‘biological balance between building and landscape’. To achieve this, he planned
huge areas in the middle of the city for ‘balanced forest organisms’, created under
bio-sociological conditions as ‘solid natural communities of trees’ while leaving
enough space for extensive lawns. At the higher margins of the glacial valley, in
the Friedrichshain, Humboldthain and Hasenheide parks and at Westend, hills
of rubble were planned.20
Polanski 2006, 321
Polanski points to the psychological conditions operating in the ruins of post-war
German cities and the transformation of the rubble from destroyed buildings into
landscapes of nature. ‘There were several million cubic metres of rubble in Berlin. It
was used to raise artiﬁcial hills that highlight the natural movements of an otherwise
inexpressive landscape’ (2006, 327). Cloaking destruction was as much a natural
reconditioning of the destroyed city as towards the future city under reconstruction.
But this project of cloaking destruction through burial faced many obstacles that
were not political but rudimentarily material. Not only was there an acute shortage
of building materials for reconstruction, there was also an acute shortage of topsoil
to cover the rubble hills and on which to plant trees and shrubbery. The shortage of
topsoil required selecting plants that could grow in the least amount of soil possible.
Given this constraint, Lingner developed his planting concept for Teufelsberg based
on an interwoven system of plants not dependent on high-nutrient soil or constant
watering to ﬂourish.21 Writing in Der Bauhelfer in 1947, Lingner outlined his planting
concept under the title ‘Tasks and Aims of Green Space Planning’:
We can draw on the experience of a specialist in the afforestation of rubbish
dumps and slag heaps. In his experience, it may be possible to plant trees in the
rubble without applying topsoil. [. . .] Strangely, one of the most important
pioneer trees is the alder. It is able to send roots down to great depths and to
draw nitrogen from the air, similar to the papilionaceae family; it is extremely
frugal and the mild, rich humus formed by its leaves prepares the ground for
more demanding trees that will follow. The alder is the nurse of the future forest.22
Lingner 1947
Constructed landscapes as ‘nurse of the future forest’ were central to Lingner’s
biomorphic vision that relied on nature to constitute the health of the city. Berlin’s
chaotic post-war housing shortage produced a new group of subterranean urban
dwellers living in cellars. With many buildings reduced to empty shells, Berlin’s
residents would disappear from their catastrophic landscape into underground tombs,
emitting signs of life via the smoke belching from the protruding pipes of woodburning stoves. As the disappearance of the destroyed city gained speed in tandem
with the appearance of burial landscapes, Lingner’s ‘nurse of the future forest’
likewise gained ground in the well-being of the people. Speeding the clearance of the
destroyed city was tied to speeding the reconstructed city; disappearance and
reappearance was worked simutaneouly into an aesthetic for natural forgetting.
In the Aesthetics of Disappearance, Paul Virilio connects speed and appearance
using the speeding car on the highway or the train moving rapidly through the
landscape as examples of an optical experience where vision falls to the unseen.23
Disappearance: natural forgetting
119

## S. 135

Whether falling between frames of ﬁlm or into the passing landscape, Virilio’s
aesthetics of disappearance are meshed with the technologies of the machine where
speed dissolves seeing into the disappearances of vision. In another work, Landscape
of Events, Virilio describes the relationship between landscape and time:
A landscape of time in which events suddenly take the place of relief, of vegetation;
in which the past and the future loom up together in all their obvious simultaneity;
a place where nothing follows on from anything else any more and yet where
nothing ever ends, the lack of duration of the perpetual present circumscribing
the cycle of history and its repetitions.24
Virilio 1990, x
A supreme example of designing for disappearance can be found in the military
bunkers on the coast of Normandy, famously documented by Virilio in his book
Bunker Archaeology. Built by slave labour under the Todt Organization led by Fritz
Todt, and after his death in 1942 by Albert Speer, the bunkers are the physical
remnants of the Third Reich’s Atlantic Wall defence system that stretched along the
coasts of Norway, Denmark, the Netherlands, Belgium and France. Virilio describes
these obsolete ramparts as ‘cryptic architecture’ of a once impenetrable system that
would ‘became the marker for the evolution of Hitlerian space’25 (1994, 57). Designed
to blend into the coastline of sand dunes and grassy knolls, over the last 70 years
the massive concrete bunkers have shifted from their locations by ‘titling’, ‘sinking’
Figure 31 Bunkers of Normandy Coast, Summer 1998
Photo: Author
120
Disappearance: natural forgetting

## S. 136

and ‘disappearing’. Appearing as giant forms of encrusted Mollusca ‘tilted into the
sand like the skin of an extinct species’, their architectural program designed for killing
has been transformed into sublime biological formations.
These concrete shelters ceaselessly proliferated and got thicker, an almost
botanical sign of a constantly increasing pressure, of a constantly more ‘rigorous’
climate. In the end these bunkers obtained the role of the prestige monuments,
witnessing not so much the power of the Third Reich as its obsession with
disappearance.
Virilio 1994, 57
Captured in stark black-and-white photographs, Virilio theorises their changing
form as an anthropomorphy and zoomorphy of botanical specimens: anthropomorphy
in the way the bunkers take on human characteristics, zoomorphy in the way he
associates them with animal forms. Virilio states that each bunker ‘is spun from a
network under tension with the landscape and, through the landscape, with the region
in its expanse. It is an invisible and immaterial network that escapes our gaze and
enables the bunker to hide from view’ (1994, 44). Virilio’s anthropomorphic and
zoomorphic terminologies can also be applied, I would argue, to the buried city: a
characteristically human shaping of landscape, its animal qualities provided by the
fauna of the forest, the buried city’s tension with the ‘invisible and immaterial’ and,
I would add, the dead and the living, creating a living composite environment between
the anthropomorphy and zoomorphy of human and nature.
Conclusion
Cloaked in chainmail and plated metal, the body of the warrior knight disappears
in the protection of his impenetrable exterior shell. This body-architecture, restrictive,
clumsy and intimidating armour of the soft ﬂesh, formed the ‘prosthesis that served
to shore up a disrupted body image’26 (Foster 1991, 68). Once pierced by spear or
arrow and bleeding inside, the warrior knight crashes to the ground in folds of shiny
metal to die in his armoured crypt. Later this crypt will be stripped from his body,
exposing his open wounds and, when buried, his ﬁnal disappearance from the ﬁeld
of battle. Millions of bodies have disappeared from battleﬁelds in this way, returning
the ﬁeld, forest or hill to the placid and silent spaces of nature. Such a movement
can also be seen in the forested landscape covering the buried city. Drawing a parallel
between the ﬁne warrior knight’s cloak of armour covering his body and the forest
covering Teufelsberg reminds us of the death and destruction that lies with the buried
city. Through the placidity of its ﬁeld, forest and hill of rubble, where nature has
taken over, Teufelsberg transforms the remnants of battle and destruction by
overwriting its disappearance, allowing the ground above to be naturalised for leisure
and entertainment.
Placidity of the park – the nature of leisure
In The Critique of Everyday Life, Henri Lefebvre explores the social, political and
cultural relationships between work and leisure. Framed by his conception of Marxist
ideology, Lefebvre argues that work is the labour of ‘productive activity’ and leisure
Disappearance: natural forgetting
121

## S. 137

‘involves passive attitudes’27 (1991a, 32). His differentiation between work and leisure
is nevertheless dependent on the type of leisure engaged in, describing watching movies
as a passive activity whereas photography draws on ‘active attitudes’ in the framing
of the subject. Leisure, he insists, is both an individual and communal activity where
people interact with others, their environment and the world in general. ‘At ﬁrst,
leisure gives rise to an undifferentiated global activity that is difﬁcult to distinguish
from other aspects of the everyday (family strolls on Sunday, walking)’ (1991a, 32).
Human involvement with what he describes as ‘leisure machines’ (radio, television,
etc.) enacts activities that do not need the body. ‘It creates new types of play and
reforms old ones, sometimes conﬂicting with other activities, sometimes overlapping
(in the camping holiday, work and leisure are barely distinguishable, and everyday
life in its entirety becomes play)’ (1991b, 33). The late-19th-century development of
recreational time in European societies that would later crystalise in the mass
entertainments of the 20th century brought new systematic organisation for the
production of leisure. Out of the destruction of Berlin, new landscapes would form
in and outside of the city. Radically shifting the city’s topography, leisure could be
factored into the very process of planning, turning the buried city into sites of
recreation.
The construction of Teufelsberg on the outskirts of Berlin created a new geography
where, from its summit, the public would have a panoramic view of reconstructed
Berlin in the distance. The surface that maintained the separation between the buried
city below and the overlay of adventure and play above paciﬁed the death and
destruction from which it was made. As designed geography, the disappearance of
the buried city created leisure activities that could erase it from the memory of the
city: Sunday strolls on its paths, ﬂying kites from the top of Kite Hill (Drachenberg),
tobogganing down its steep slopes. These new activities would transform the grave
of Berlin into the living organism joining nature and humans that Reinhold Lingner
had intended. ‘Liberation and pleasure’, Lefebvre suggests, are the ‘essential
characteristics of leisure’ (1991b, 33). The above-the-surface activities of public
playground and new habitat for wildlife brought human and animal to the geography
and forest of the buried city.
The division between the human and its self was – and remains – as deep, as
tragic, as necessary as the division between man and nature. The one is the
corollary of the other. Man, a being of nature, forever united inseparably with
nature, struggles against it. He dominates it and imagines he can separate himself
from it, through abstraction, through self-consciousness – something only attained
by painful effort.
Lefebvre 1991a, 180
Lefebvre’s suggestion of the ‘division between the human and its self’ points to
the way Teufelsberg’s human-made nature divides nature from its self. Teufelsberg
is not only the grave of the destroyed city, but also a composite of nature. Recognisable
as nature, it avoids the tragedy of its foundation of mass destruction. To walk
Teufelsberg is to be in the pleasure and leisure of its nature in the absence of the
buried city. In The Production of Space, Lefebvre asserts that leisure is the right of
the body to imitate nature. ‘In the areas set aside for leisure, the body regains a certain
right to use, a right which is half imaginary and half real, and which does not go
122
Disappearance: natural forgetting

## S. 138

beyond an illusory “culture of the body”, an imitation of natural life’28 (1991b, 203).
Teufelsberg thus succeeds in rendering absent not only the buried city, but also its
violence, via its ‘imitation’ of ‘natural life’.
Walking Teufelsberg is not to walk Berlin’s recent history of destruction. Yet, to
the inquisitive historical mind – knowing what one is walking over – the scale of the
journey becomes a profound experience. From destroyed city to placid nature of the
park, Teufelsberg constructs a new social space for the urban dweller. ‘The more a
space partakes of nature, the less it enters into the social relations of production.
There is nothing surprising about this; the same holds true after all for a rock or a
tree’ (Lefebvre 1991b, 83). Since it is stone, brick, concrete and trees, the striking
thing about walking Teufelsberg is how nature naturalises the buried city into
obscurity.
The successful disappearance of the destroyed city has allowed Teufelsberg to
become an additional asset in Berlin’s trade in Cold War tourism. Due to the
invisibility of the Cold War battleﬁeld, the visible US listening station complex at the
apex of the rubble mountain has emerged as an international tourist attraction, a
visible, tangible relic that epitomises the covert communications tapping conducted
by the US during the Cold War. Today, an entrepreneurial group of young Berliners
offers guided tours of the abandoned buildings; the spectacle of the shredded plastic
that once covered its spherical structures, its grafﬁti and echoing spaces, coupled with
Figure 32 Teufelsberg, Blick nach Charlottenburg auf das Olympiastadion und das
Corbusier Hochhaus, 11.08.1976 F Rep.290–192987 Landesarchiv Berlin
Photo: Ludwig Ehlers
Disappearance: natural forgetting
123

## S. 139

the spectacular view from the main building’s roof back towards the city of Berlin,
have made Teufelsberg a permanent feature on the tourist map. The guided tours of
the listening post tap into Berlin’s war tourism market that includes (to name just a
few): the ﬂak tower in Humbolthain park; the ruined Kaiser Wilhelm Memorial
Church (Gedächtniskirche) near the Zoological Garden; the Berlin Wall; the Soviet
War Memorial in Treptow; the New Guardhouse (Neue Wache) on Unter den Linden;
the Jewish Museum on Lindenstrasse; and the Memorial to the Murdered Jews of
Europe. In Back to the Front – Tourisms of War, American architects and theorists
Elizabeth Diller and Ricardo Scoﬁdio and their contributors review the attraction
exerted by battlegrounds and sites of war. Fifty years after the D-Day landings, Back
to the Front questions how tourism and spectatorship repurpose sites of war as
ongoing sites of re-enactment of war.
Tourism and war appear to be polar extremes of cultural activity – the paradigm
of international accord at one end and discord at the other. The two practices,
however, often intersect: tourism of war, war on tourism, tourism as war, war
as targeting tourism, tourism under war, war as tourism are a few of their
interesting couplings.29
Diller and Scoﬁdio 1994, 19
Figure 33 Ruin, US Listening Post Teufelsberg, Sept. 2015
Photo: Author
124
Disappearance: natural forgetting

## S. 140

The absence of the buried city from Berlin’s tourism of war can be attributed to
its successful camouﬂage. ‘Camouﬂage can be as tactical for the tourist as it is for
the soldier’ (Diller and Scoﬁdio 1994, 24). Because the buried city does not ﬁt the
proﬁle of the ‘classical’ site of war tourism, as it cannot be seen or experienced, it
can also not be constructed as a war attraction. Diller and Scoﬁdio point out that
‘one of tourism’s most popular attractions is, in fact, the battleﬁeld on which war
has been waged. Solemn sites of war seem incongruous with the tourist’s presumed
desire to indulge in carefree pleasures and amusements’ (1994, 25). Displaced from
the battleﬁeld of Berlin, Teufelsberg was designed from the outset as a distraction to
hide its origins so it could become a good-natured site for pleasure.
As designed geography, the buried city offers an array of sporting amusements on
its surface. Toboggan runs, ice-skating, jogging, kite ﬂying, hang-gliding, sightseeing
and winding paths – the new inhabitants of the buried city rise and fall over its designed
tourism of nature. A strong memory from a visit to Teufelsberg was reaching the
treeless plateau known as Drachenberg (Kite Hill). From the top of the hill I saw a
hang-glider jump from its steep slope, his yellow sail moving in drifts and turns as it
ﬂoated in the air. At the same time, in the middle of the hill, an elderly Muslim man
with arms raised to the sky turned in a circle as he prayed and, at the same time, two
young children were running to get their kites airborne. The connection between the
hang-glider, the praying man and the kite ﬂyers’ seamless embrace of air captured in
my mind the transformation of the buried city from its roots of destruction to the
Figure 34 Teufelsberg Prayer and Parachute, Summer 2004
Video Still: Author
Disappearance: natural forgetting
125

## S. 141

many purposes nature possesses for humankind. The leisure, pleasure and spiritual
landscape the buried city affords to its visitors are also evident in the various archive
photographs (many not included in this book) that show people with sledges gliding
over the snow and ice, meandering along its paths, sitting on park benches, looking
east to view Berlin, ﬂying kites, walking dogs and pushing prams. There is nothing
perverse in turning destruction into pleasure, for it is naturally cyclic. The ﬁne surface
that separates the buried city from its forest, paths and hilltops is also what links it
into a seamless place of nature. This successful rendering of the surface plane has
allowed Teufelsberg’s unnatural human-made geography to deny its own underground
nature. Teufelsberg converts the buried city’s origins of destruction into a synthetic
nature of leisure. It is a paradoxical transformative nature, from the unknown under -
ground to the tourism of war and the mysteries of nature. Nature releases Teufelsberg
from the destroyed city, severing its link with the guilt of Berlin’s destruction. Surface
is what enables Teufelsberg to be pleasurable.
Conclusion
Another way of understanding the surface of Teufelsberg that separates destruction
from leisure is via the example of the interaction of remembrance and play in Peter
Eisenman’s National Memorial to the Murdered Jews of Europe.30 Covering 1.9
hectares, the memorial comprises 2711 stelae (gravestone-like concrete blocks)
arranged in a systematic grid in an undulating topography. At their lowest around
the periphery of the Memorial, the stelae increase in height towards the middle as
the ground they stand on sinks. The unease of each stela’s inward lean, the porosity
of the space between their masses, immerses the body as it moves through the narrow
pathways. The stelae exert a spatial disorientation through avoidance of space yet
trigger spatial conﬂicts, activating a dramaturgy of occurrences, accidents and
vagrancies between one’s body and other unseen bodies. In Eisenman’s Text as Zero,
notably inﬂuenced by Baudrillard’s concept of simulacra, he suggests that ‘in an age
of simulation perhaps the one remaining example of a vanishing authenticity is
violence, the destruction of the “frame”, the de-framing of reference’31 (Eisenman
1993, 42).
Engaging this notion, Eisenman’s Memorial sets in motion a constant stream of
disappearances and reappearances of bodies among the stelae. Importantly, drawing
a connection between Teufelsberg and the Memorial acknowledges that informal
recreational play is part of the overall experience of both sites. In Eisenman’s
Memorial, children run and play while adults also perform their versions of ‘hide
and seek’, both perhaps implying ‘misuse’ or even a ‘ruining’ of the remembrance of
the holocaust. But it is the liveliness of both adults and children that actuates the
Memorial, realising an active collective memory. Appearing and disappearing within
the stelae, coupled with the constant attention needed to avoid others, constitutes
the ocular and interactive encounters that dominate the experience of the Memorial.
This speciﬁc appearing and disappearing can be connected to the buried city’s
disappearance and appearance through its undulating terrain of nature that provides
various activities for leisure and play. Teufelsberg, it is worth considering, is not a
memorial; it is not authored for remembering the destroyed city of Berlin or its burial.
Lefebvre points out the constant oscillation between labour and leisure: ‘So we
work to earn our leisure, and leisure has only one meaning: to get away from work.
126
Disappearance: natural forgetting

## S. 142

A vicious circle’ (Lefebvre 1991b, 40). While Eisenman’s Memorial seeks to confront
(the Holocaust) via an undulating sculptured topography of gravestones and
uncontrolled encounters between bodies, Teufelsberg avoids such confrontation via
burial below ground and, above ground, via the placidity of the park. If an event is
not actively remembered, it becomes forgotten. This is what conveniently happened
to the Wehrmacht Academy buried under the rubble of Berlin. Suggesting that this
was a conscious act, then, what lies buried forms our natural forgetting. To remember
the buried city is to ask what would emerge from its tomb.
Out of the forest – the nature of law
Mythopoetically, the forest is a fantastical place.32 A source of fairy tales and myths,
the forest also contains unknown imminent danger. Deep within the woods, the
treetop canopy ﬁlters the sunlight that falls in luminous hues on the soft, mossy
ground, playing on the senses of the wanderer. In the eeriness of this wooded domain,
it is not hard to imagine Hansel and Gretel’s fear and adventure of being lost and
their hunger on reaching the gingerbread house where the wicked witch resides, enticed
by the smell. The Grimms’ fairy tale, published in 1812, is a classic tale of the forest,
where children ‘must simply learn how to survive in a world ﬁlled with danger and
excitement’33 (Zipes 2006, 201). The makings of myths were also tied to the making
of laws of the forest ‘where children are socialized to respect laws out of fear’ (2006,
158). Vico tells us that ‘myth is deﬁned as vera narratio, true narration’ (1999, 159).
The joining of myth and law was to bring the ‘the age of the gods’, ‘the age of the
heroes’, ‘and ﬁnally, the age of men’ (1999, 22). Within the chaos of the forest, the
laws of nature would also be the same laws that would govern human settlement.
In his treatise Germania (AD 89), Tacitus recognised that within the realm of the
forest, unbounded by spatial dimensions yet geographically demarcated by glades,
rivers and ravines, the Germanic tribes organised their communities. A millennium
and a half later, Vico was to draw on Tacitus’s observations for his theory of natural
law born of the forest. In Book 4, The Course of Nations from New Science, Vico
tells the history of human settlement from sites of dwelling and burial that marked
the ground; three kinds of natural law – divine, force and reason – came into being.34
The ﬁrst natural law was divine, by which people believed that their lives and
affairs depended on the gods, whom they regarded as responsible for all things.
[922]
The second was heroic law, or the law of force. This law was tempered by
religion, for only religion can keep force within the bounds of duty, even when
there are no human laws or none strong enough to restrain it. This is why
providence ordained that the naturally ﬁerce early peoples should obey religion.
In this way, they naturally acceded to force; and while still lacking power to
reason, they measured right by fortune, and practised divination by the auspices.
This law of force is the law of Achilles, who judges everything by the point of
his spear. [923]
The third is the human law which is dictated by fully developed human reason.
[924]
(Vico [1725] 1999, 400)
Disappearance: natural forgetting
127

## S. 143

In the forest, the chaotic forces of nature, fear and the unknown rule human
settlement. In the city, nature is designed, law supposedly more organised, and
community shaped by buildings rather than by the natural terrains of the forest.
According to Vico, law emanated from the forest and when settlement moved onto
open ground those laws of the forest were adapted to regulate the protection of the
village, town, and ﬁnally city. The ‘origins and institutions of the nations’, Vico writes,
came in three stages: ‘(1) the mythical age; (2) the dark age, which all have despaired
of knowing; and (3) the historical age’ (1999, 4). Myth was an important historical
construction of early societies, practiced by pagan and non-pagan nations that were
founded ‘through their myths of the gods’ (1999, 5). Myths served to ground human
relationships via a world of gods that could form unassailable belief systems as a
base for societies and settlements.
Such conjuring of mythical spaces and ﬁgures, and their association with the
production of binding laws shaping terms and behaviour, can be traced into the
modern age. The amateur Austrian scientist Hanns Hörbiger (1860–1931) developed
a theory of the formation of the universe governed by an oscillating crystallised
cosmos. Hörbiger’s Glacial Cosmogony, also known as World Ice Theory (Glazial-
Kosmogonie or Welteislehre), was a highly obscure pseudo-science of the structure
and materiality of the cosmos. Its supporters included SS leader Heinrich Himmler,
who championed Hörbiger’s concept of a crystal universe in the myths he was
building around ancestral veneration of the dead (Ahnenkult) for his SS regiments.
Hörbiger believed that ice was the basic matter for all cosmic processes of the moon
and planets in the solar system. This fanciful cosmological theory of ice was based
not on scientiﬁc research, however, but on a dream. Rejecting Newton’s scientiﬁc
discoveries of 200 years earlier, Hörbiger’s crystal cosmological dream continues the
mythic space of the universe. A follower of Hörbiger, the German biologist and writer
Hans Wolfgang Behm (1890–unknown) came to prominence in the 1920s and 1930s
with his theory of the cosmos through a series of publications. With striking titles
like ‘Colloid Chemistry’ (Kolloidchemie, 1925), ‘Death of the Planet and Turning
Point of Life: Prehistoric Thoughts On the Coming Scientiﬁc Worldview’ (Planetentod
und Lebenswende. Urgeschichtliche Betrachtung zum kommenden naturforschlich
deutbaren Weltbild, 1926) and ‘The Cosmic Powers and Us: The Destiny of Our
Earth’ (Die kosmischen Mächte und wir – Der Schicksalsweg unserer Erde, 1936),
his books also reinvented myth as the centre of the universe’s evolution. Behm’s forays
into the occultation of natural science, which also attracted the attention of Himmler
and his occultation of the SS, would radically shift after the war to focus on the
German forest, and especially the Grunewald adjacent to Teufelsberg.35 Hörbiger and
Behm’s mythical pseudo-science of a glacial universe carried the mythopoeic logic of
the world coming into being into the contemporary age. Their mythical formation
of the universe connects, and to a degree reiterates, the stories spun by the mythic
romancers of gods and demons of the skies and earth.
Behm’s ‘10,000 Years of Grunewald’ (Zehntausend Jahre Grunewald) has a chapter
on ‘Wartime and Post-War Inﬂuences as Forest Devastators’ (Kriegs und
Nachkriegseinﬂüsse als Waldverwüster). Waldverwüster (forest devastator), the term
Behm uses to describe the desecration of the forest, is not made up but borrowed
from the German Forestry Act (Forstgesetz, §16) authored by Carl Ludwig Theodor
Brater. Published in 1852, Brater’s law of the forest is an extension of the German
law of the city. By 1875 the Forestry Act had become the Prussian Forest Protection
128
Disappearance: natural forgetting

## S. 144

Act (Preußisches Waldschutzgesetz), a series of laws and regulations governing the
public’s use of the forest. The Prussian Forest Protection Act not only covers juridical
governance of the forest by humans but also incorporates animals, weather, natural
disasters and war. Behm’s devastators of the forest can be also connected to another
site, the devastated wasteland of post-war Berlin in ruins. As we have learnt, it was
Lingner and his associates who would transform Berlin by gathering the rubble and
piling it onto wasteland that would be turned back into forest. Teufelsberg’s
transformation from the visible site of destruction to the placidity of the park connects
both forest and burial. From the wild boar that roam its forest, to those who walk
the paths that cut across its terrain, to the children who ﬂy their kites, Teufelsberg
remains only a surface experience of the buried city.
Shifted from the exploded boundaries of destroyed Berlin and bundled into
geography, the buried city remains unexplored for its potential to produce new forms
of descriptive mythology. Myth is both locational and universal, it is site-speciﬁc and
also site-less, it is connected to terrain, to the geography of the land, to the vastness
of the seas and the limitless dimensions of the cosmos. Understanding what might
constitute Teufelsberg’s myth would involve combining the forest and the buried city
as a part in the progression of the human endeavour. In Mythologies, Roland Barthes
states that ‘the myth of the human “condition” rests on a very old mystiﬁcation,
which always consists in placing Nature at the bottom of History’36 (1972, 101).
Constructed for the disappearance of the destroyed city, the ‘mystiﬁcation’ of
Teufelsberg would come through its emergence from its foundations, piercing the
surface of the forest ﬂoor. The reporting in German current affairs magazine Der
Spiegel in 2005 concerning the ‘discovery’ of Speer’s Wehrmacht Academy formulates,
only 60 years later, the mystiﬁcation of the buried city and its contents. ‘Myth’, Barthes
asserts, ‘is not deﬁned by the object of its message, but by the way in which it utters
this message: there are formal limits to myth, there are no “substantial” ones’ (1972,
107).
Everything, then, can be a myth? Yes, I believe this, for the universe is inﬁnitely
fertile in suggestions. Every object in the world can pass from a closed, silent
existence to an oral state, open to appropriation by society, for there is no law,
whether natural or not, which forbids talking about things.
Barthes 1972, 107
Given the recentness of Teufelsberg’s construction, the apparent journalistic and
public ignorance of its underground contents can be credited to the success with which
the buried city is camouﬂaged. It is hard to see Teufelsberg as anything other than
a forest. Returning the city back to the forest, as Teufelsberg does, raises the question
as to what would emerge if the buried city were to be revealed? As Teufelsberg offers
no actual clues to those wishing to know what lies under its forest, the physical story
of the buried city must be mythopoetically conjured or ‘calling up’ to establish where
it came from and what its story means now. The idea of an archaeological dig on
Teufelsberg (raised in the introduction) would not, I am sure, unearth a cache of
historically valuable items of the kind we normally associate with buried artefacts.
But suggesting such an excavation would highlight Teufelsberg’s historical place and
reconnect it to present day Berlin.
Disappearance: natural forgetting
129

## S. 145

What is the value of undertaking such a project? Granted that Teufelsberg is of
no archaeological value in material terms, the value of the dig would be historical.
One aspect would concern understanding the human capacity for mass destruction
and the human ability to cover over war and violence. In Auschwitz or Treblinka,
the physical presence of buildings and the outline of former buildings where millions
were killed evoke unfathomable horror and prompt visitors to grapple with the human
capacity for evil. While an archaeological exploration of Teufelsberg cannot be placed
in the same category as Auschwitz or Treblinka, it evokes what happens to a people,
city and nation in the era of unlimited destruction. Its distinctive archaeology reveals
the human ability to design and replace catastrophe with the placidity of nature.
Containing approximately 16,000 buildings, a dig at Teufelsberg would reveal a
compressed urbanity of the former homes of Berlin’s inhabitants. The buried city can
also be conceived of as the brutal repression of the history of Berlin, as a constructed
city-anaesthesia and as a landscape for forgetting. Returning to Landscape and
Memory, Simon Schama remarks that landscapes have long been imbued with
mythologies. Through nature, the disappearance of the buried city becomes
mythologised; a speculative site as much as a landscape:
. . . it is clear that inherited landscape myths and memories share two common
characteristics: their surprising endurance through the centuries and their power
to shape institutions that we still live with. National identity, to take just the
Figure 35 Teufelsberg Wintersport, 7 Dec 1980 F Rep.290–229873, Landesarchiv Berlin
Photo: Wolfgang Albrecht
130
Disappearance: natural forgetting

## S. 146

most obvious example, would lose much of its ferocious enchantment without
the mystique of a particular landscape tradition: its topography mapped,
elaborated, and enriched as a homeland.
Schama 1995, 15
The purpose of proposing an archaeological dig is to allow the ancestral ‘homeland’
of present day Berlin to be ‘mapped, elaborated, and enriched’. Connections could
be drawn to the histories of razed cities such as Babylon, Mycenae and Carthage and
humanity’s archaic propensity for destruction. In the short history of its existence,
Teufelsberg is an example of how constructed disappearance can absorb the propensity
for destruction, saving humanity its embarrassment. Every year, Hiroshima and
Nagasaki formulate the shame for allowing the killing of tens of thousands by the
use of the atomic bomb. Today, the destruction of towns and cities such as Aleppo
or Gaza City continue humankind’s relationship with physically destroying the homes
and livelihoods of civilian populations.
In Forests: The Shadow of Civilization, Harrison informs us that Vico viewed the
destruction of cities as an act of treachery and as substantially more inhuman than
the human propensity for savagery, from which one could ﬂee. ‘For Vico such
barbarism signalled the beginning of the end of the human age and the imminent
metamorphosis of cities into forests – a return, as it were, to the lawless state of
nature’ (1993, 101). If Teufelsberg were to be viewed as part of Vico’s ‘lawless state
of nature’, then its unearthing would take us back to the barbarity of humanity. In
Augmented Landscapes, architects Mark Smout and Laura Allen argue that the
human-controlled landscape takes on ‘an artiﬁcial patination. Alien materials interrupt
the processes of growth and decay. New and evolving features created by man are,
to an extent, absorbed by the ﬂuid and yielding nature of our surroundings’ (Smout
and Allen 2007, 6). Returning the city to the forest from which it emerged means
reasserting the forest as the maker of laws. Generating new laws for society by
unearthing the geography of the buried city may also point to a new philosophy of
the history of destruction, proposing ways to deal with the destruction humanity
makes other than covering it over.
In their ﬁnal joint work, What is Philosophy?, Deleuze and Guattari contemplate
a geophilosophy; a geo-earth-philosophy of contrasts via varying occupations of the
ground. The tendency in French philosophy, they contend, is to build structures, while
the Germans tend to lay foundations and the English tend to ‘camp’. Applying
Deleuze and Guattari’s geophilosophy to the law can reveal that, unlike English
common law with its uncertain origins, German law has more certain foundations,
with deﬁnite beginnings pre-Roman law along with other forms of legal administra -
tion post-Roman in the laws of German settlement and, later, its adoption of the
Napoleonic code.37
After the end of WWII and the defeat of the Nazi regime, amid the realities of
Europe-wide devastation, the nation began the search for a new start. Known as
Stunde Null (Zero Hour), new legal frameworks were drawn on both sides of the
ideological divide separating East and West Germany that was acutely realised in
Berlin’s fragmented identity. Time beginning anew was to replace destruction, with
the psychological beneﬁts of reconstruction that would realise Sebald’s self-anaesthesia
concept. The suggestion to unearth Teufelsberg now, 70 years after its construction,
presents new legal opportunities in how the buried city can be reconﬁgured to be
Disappearance: natural forgetting
131

## S. 147

representative of present-day Berlin. In the depths of Teufelsberg rest not only the
historical ruination of Berlin but also the foundations of a new legal order emerging
from its forest; an amalgamation of the laws of nature and the destructive laws of
man. Unearthing Teufelsberg rewrites the laws of the forest and, upon its emergence
into the open, underwrites new forms of co-existence between history, nature and
humans.
Conclusion
François-René de Chateaubriand (1768–1848) is buried on the small tidal island of
Grand Bé near the mouth of the River Rance in Saint-Malo, France. Defender of
Christianity, amateur archaeologist and romanticist writer, Chateaubriand’s body lies
in a stone grave on the edge of a rugged bluff facing the sea. Born into aristocratic
privilege, his life was lived in turmoil and circumstances ranging from political life
and soldering to destitution and poverty. Fleeing the French Revolution of 1789,
Chateaubriand crossed the Atlantic to North America, visiting Philadelphia, New York
and Boston before heading to the southern states along the Mississippi River and then
Florida. On his return to France, he joined the royalist army and, badly wounded at
the battle of Thionville (1792), was smuggled across the English Channel to London
where he recovered. Without the ﬁnancial support of his family, Chateaubriand’s life
in exile was mostly lived in abject poverty, surviving on a meagre income from teaching
French and translating from English to French, most notably John Milton’s 1667 epic
poem Paradise Lost. Chateaubriand’s autobiography ‘Memoirs from Beyond the
Tomb’ (Mémoires d’outre-tombe), published in 1850, retells his life and travels over
four decades, from the time of Louis XVI, the French Revolution and the reign of
Napoleon through to the Restoration period. ‘I was pressed to present parts of these
memoires during my lifetime; I prefer to speak from the bottom of my cofﬁn. My
narrative will thus be accompanied by these voices which have something sacred about
them, because they come from the sepulchre’38 (Chateaubriand 1961, xxii). French
writer and philosopher Maurice Blanchot (1907–2003), in his book Glances From
Beyond The Grave, refers to Chateaubriand’s Memoirs from the point of view of a
life metaphorically lived with one foot in the grave:
But, if the truth be told, it is not death which speaks in these Memoires, but
rather existence as dead, as always having been, always also a thing of the past
immobilized in a life which remains in its grandiose manner, alien to any future
and even to the future of death.39
Blanchot 1992, 152
While not sharing Chateaubriand’s conservative values, Blanchot does give him
some credit for an adventurous life lived. It was Chateaubriand who, on visiting
Pompeii, proposed that this unearthed city be turned into a living historical stagemuseum that would speak from the grave. Thinking about Chateaubriand’s voice
from the sepulchre of his cofﬁn resonates with how the voice of the buried city speaks
from the grave of Berlin’s destruction. To dig into the grave of the buried city would
reveal a new set of geological materials, different from any other earth strata. In
Architecture in the Anthropocene, Seth Denizen draws on associations between
geology and the city that point to a continuum of the history of humanity.40
132
Disappearance: natural forgetting

## S. 148

The practical necessity of mapping the surface geology of cities has encouraged
a deeper understanding of the stratigraphic relations between human beings and
the geological present. Understanding the extent to which patterns of urbaniza -
tion, domesticity, burial, recreation, architecture, horticulture, and warfare are
factors of soil formation has produced a new set of possibilities for the production
of the geological present.
Denizen 2013, 42
Among the layers of concrete, brick and dust, the story of the buried city will not
reveal the urban structures unearthed in Pompeii or Herculaneum. A mythopoetic
antithesis to these archaeological jewels of antiquity, Teufelsberg’s archaeology will
no doubt reveal a formless city where tree roots have burrowed through the cracks of
concrete and brick to draw the ether of the buried city to the surface through the
branches and leaves of its forest. Drawing on the early works of Nicolas Steno
(1638–1686) and studies of the earth’s strata by his contemporary Horace de Saussure
(1740–1799), the British geologist James Hutton (1726–1797) developed his theory
of the geological history of the earth. Hutton developed his theory of the earth
proclaiming its formation as vast and limitless in time. Contrary to the accepted
theological and biblical based reckoning that claimed the earth’s age at 6000 years in
line with God's seven-day creation of the earth, Hutton proved to be the truer of the
two. Today the accepted theory of the earth’s age is approximately 4.55 billion years.
In 1785 he published a Theory of the Earth that was to position his concept of earth
time at the forefront of geological science. Hutton referred to his geological history of
the earth as ‘deep time’, the time that resides in the layering of the earth’s strata. Reading
the strata of the earth would reveal earth time as distinct from the time of human
existence. A century earlier, Sir Isaac Newton had experimented with the transmission
of light and developed his laws of motion and gravity, and a century before that, men
of medicine had carried out dissections of the body to learn from the internal workings
of its anatomy and physiology. In the same way, ‘deep time’ helps us understand the
buried city. Although radically different to the infant age of Teufelsberg, Hutton’s
earth–time strata measure ment takes us from the disappearance of the city, from the
surface of the forest, to delve into the ground to formulate the geological history of
the buried city.

1 In many districts of former East Berlin (less so in former West Berlin), such as Prenzlauer
Berg, Lichtenberg and Mitte, grey concrete stucco was applied to most reconstructed
buildings. It was quick and cheap and, as a result, very little of the building’s former façade
ornamentations were restored. Many buildings became a grey wash of clawed lines of
concrete trailed over the complete façade covering whole streets.
2 The casting of shadows (by bodies, architecture and objects) brought to life the animate
and inanimate, an effect fully exploited in the German expressionist ﬁlms of the 1920s
such as Robert Wiene’s The Cabinet of Dr. Caligary (1921), Friedrich W. Murnau’s
Nosferatu (1922), Fritz Lang’s Die Nibelungen (1924) and W.G. Pabst’s Pandora’s Box
(1929). Shadows deﬁne the visual appearance of watching these ﬁlms, creating a visceral
and scenographic interplay between light and form with each ﬂickering image oscillating
between the real and hyper-real expressionism. Although ﬁlm technology was still in its
infancy and prone to failings (uneven ﬁlm speeds in cameras, unstable ﬁlm stock,
incandescent lighting), ﬁlm experimentation nevertheless thrived within these limitations.
