## S. 152

5
New ground
Unearthing Teufelsberg, against
forgetting
Everything that is real has a ground for its reality. 
Everything that is possible has a ground for its possibility. 
Everything that is necessary has a ground for its necessity. 
Nothing is without ground.
Martin Heidegger, The Principle of Ground
Extracting – scraping back
In Richard Long – A Line Made by Walking, Dieter Roelstraete explores the various
connections between walking and landscape. His account centres on Long’s ﬁrst
landscape artwork made in 1967 in a ﬁeld outside London, for which he walked
back and forth for 20 minutes forging a path of trampled grass ending on the verge
of a forest. Roelstraete writes that ‘Long’s A Line Made by Walking may be a straight
one, but the telling of its story is anything but a straightforward affair’1 (2010, 1).
At ﬁrst glance, Long’s straight path does not appear startling, yet the line he made
disrupts the random lay of the surrounding natural terrain. On the surface, Long’s
imprint illustrates the human impact on nature; the forging of a path and the
trampling of grass by the human body’s exertion on the landscape.
Two years after Long’s walk, Welsh artist Keith Arnatt was photographed vertically
sinking into the ground in a nondescript clearing. The ﬁrst image shows Arnatt
standing on the ground and the following images show his body incrementally
submerging and ﬁnally disappearing in the last image. Titled Self-Burial (In Nine
Stages), the work is as humorous (‘digging one’s own grave’) as it is poignant (the
interred connections between body and ground). Quoting Arnatt, Roelstraete writes
‘it was originally made as a comment upon the notion of the “disappearance of the
art object”. It seems a logical corollary that the artist should also disappear’
(2010, 54). Long and Arnatt differ in how they conceive the body’s impact on and
relationship and intersection with the ground. Long’s walk, made in a clearing but
terminating at the threshold of a forest, also draws attention to the unknown 
beyond. In One Place after Another, Miwon Kwon characterises site-speciﬁc art as:
‘Site-determined, site-orientated, site-referenced, site-conscious, site-related’2 (2002, 1).
The works by Long and Arnatt, it can be assumed, are both site-speciﬁc, yet it would
appear that their respective locations do not necessarily guide their making. Each
artist’s intersection with ground is a negotiation between body and site; 
each a disruption of ground. Long’s walk over the surface of ground illustrates the
human evasion and disruption of the natural environment. Arnatt’s artwork, on the

## S. 153

other hand, performs a disruption in ground for the disappearance of the body 
through burial.
The territory of ground is constant negotiation of the terrain; a movement between
location and landscape, surface and subsurface. Ground denotes location and surface/
subsurface materiality (soil, sand, gravel, stone) and the experiencing of ground
(slippery, dry, cracked, rough, etc.). Landscape implies the broad expanse of ground
as the terrain, geography and topography that characterise distinctive geological
formations. In ‘The Principle of Ground’, Martin Heidegger asserts,
The principle of ground reads: Nihil est sine ratione. It is translated: Nothing is
without ground. What the principle asserts can be rewritten in the following
forms: Everything has a ground, that is, every thing that in some way or other
is. Omne ens habet rationem. Everything that is real has a ground for its reality.
Everything that is possible has a ground for its possibility. Everything that is
necessary has a ground for its necessity. Nothing is without ground.3
Heidigger 1974, 207
Heidegger’s evaluation of ground is not focused on ground per se but on grounded
reality and the experience of things, thought and interactions that construct our
knowledge of the world. From a rationalist perspective, these interactions and
philosophical understandings between human existence and ground are demonstrated
through the material constructions on ground of towns and cities and the in-ground
cultivation of land. ‘What the principle of ground asserts is therefore familiar to us,
and because familiar, immediately obvious’ (1974, 208).
On the surface, Teufelsberg behaves like any other ground. It has the materiality
and texture of ground; undulating terrain, wet, muddy, smooth, soft, rough, hard
and dry. And yet Teufelsberg’s ground contains the materiality of former buildings
reformed as landscape. Heidegger asks: ‘Why must the ground be rendered, that is,
brought forward? To where is ground rendered?’ (1974, 209). The construction of
Teufelsberg rendered the destroyed city as ground and geography 17 kilometres from
its original location, Berlin. The making of Teufelsberg is a relocation of the city of
Berlin. As ‘nothing is without ground’ and Teufelsberg’s ground is the city, this
conﬁrms that ‘everything that is real has a ground for its reality’ (1974, 208). As
new ground, landscape and nature, Teufelsberg appears as real as any other ground
and hill, landscape and nature.
Heidegger’s ground theory and the ground depressions of Long and Arnatt serve
to connect the ‘obvious’ yet often dismissed and misunderstood relationships between
human and ground. As an extension of human and ground, Teufelsberg transforms
city to ground to geography. In Dark Writing, Paul Carter argues that ground and
the larger scale of ground, i.e. geography, are inseparable, requiring the ability to
‘think ﬁguratively’ and ‘inhabit a different country of thought’:
To ﬁgure something out means to think ﬁguratively. It is to associate formerly
distant things on the basis of some imagined likeness. It is to draw together things
formerly remote from one another. The line of such thought represents a
movement, a dynamic contraction that cannot be adequately represented by the
dimensionless line of cartography. To think ﬁguratively is to inhabit a different
country of thought. In this, the ground cannot be taken for granted as a uniform
138
New ground: against forgetting

## S. 154

New ground: against forgetting
139
and ﬂat plane in which the ideal ﬁgures of thought are incised. The environment
of ﬁgurative thinking possesses topological properties: it has points that lie far
apart but belong together; it also has surfaces that look close together but in fact
never meet. It is a world where the laws governing relationships count, and where
the value of passages is recognized.4
Carter 2009, 6
Carter informs us, ‘one step toward a reinvigorated geography is to materialize
its process’ (2009, 21). Materialising Teufelsberg as a ‘reinvigorated geography’ offers
an opportunity to understand the buried city’s depressions on ground. In their own
ways, Long, Arnatt, Heidegger and Carter perform and ponder a rewriting of ground;
theory of humankind, knowledge, experience, materiality, location and body. Carter
claims that ground is composed of rhythmic variations that inform our movements:
‘Rhythm is the contraction of movement into physical forms; it makes sense of both
movement and stasis’ (2009, 15). Long’s rhythmic repetitive walk on ground and
Arnatt’s disappearance of the body in ground are part of the history of marking
ground as previously explored in the references to Vico, Harrison and Lingner’s
concepts of ground, landscape, space and human burial. In Spatial Formations, Nigel
Figure 36 Künstlische Aufschüttung aus Trümmerschutt Teufelsberg Wildersdorf
Grunewald, 20.9.1966, F Rep.290–115663, Landesarchiv Berlin
Photo: Hans Seiler

## S. 155

Thrift argues that human interaction to the spatial formation of location is a product
of the body’s engagement with movement as much as with things and objects.
The body is in constant motion. Even at rest, the body is never still. As bodies
move they trace out a path from one location to another. These paths constantly
intersect with those of others in a complex web of biographies. These others are not
just human bodies but also all other objects that can be described as trajectories in
time-space: animals, machines, trees, dwellings, and so on5 (Thrift 1996, 8).
Thrift’s concept is located at the intersection between body, place and space that
need no central point of reference for their connection. This oscillation between body,
place, space and ground informs our spatial knowledge: the geographical experiencing of terrain written onto the body. ‘Movement was a prerequisite of geographical
know ledge. But it formed no part of geography’s representation of the world. Or, if
it did, then it was a kind of movement that ultimately brought about its own
extinction’ (Carter 2009, 16). As human-made geography and imported ground,
Teufelsberg marks the successful disappearance and location of the destroyed city of
Berlin. ‘It is something to reﬂect that these environs, in which we understand who
we are and where we come from, are cultural analgesics dulling history and geog -
raphy’s pain’ (2009, 19). Thrift and Carter propose a spatial rethinking of ground
by relocating the movements within human relationships of space, culture and
geography; in this view, the location of Berlin is both the reconstructed contemporary
city and also the constructed human geography where it is buried. Berlin’s history
of destruction resulted in the construction of ground; Teufelsberg’s ground is the
geography of Berlin.
In Postmodern Geographies, Edward Soja begins by declaring his aim ‘is to
spatialize the historical narrative, to attach to duree an enduring critical human
geography’6
Today, however, it may be space more than time that hides consequences from
us, the ‘making of geography’ more than the ‘making of history’ that provides
the most revealing tactical and theoretical world. This is the insistent premise
and promise of postmodern geographies.
Soja 1989, 1
Soja’s focus on the postmodern duree is ‘to create more critically revealing ways
of looking at the combination of time and space, history and geography, period and
region, sequence and simultaneity’ (1989, 2). Soja’s ‘time and space, history and
geography’ trace the lines of ‘a shaping force (or medium) in social life: the crucial
insight for both the socio-spatial dialectic and an historico-geographical materialism’
(1989, 7). Teufelsberg’s ‘time and space, history and geography’ is a reshaping of
the destruction of the city into the inﬂated ground of the buried city
In The Ground of the Image, Jean-Luc Nancy refers to the picture plane as the
emergence of depth from ground to surface. Nancy’s focus is on ‘the ground of the
image’ through the separation of layers ‘cut out within a ground’ that formulates 
the depth of the image. ‘In this double operation, the ground disappears. It disappears
in its essence as ground, which consists in its not appearing. One can thus say that
it appears as what it is by disappearing. Disappearing as ground, it passes entirely
into the image’7 (2005, 7). This disappearance of ground in the viewing of the image
creates an awareness of depth at the surface.
140
New ground: against forgetting

## S. 156

The image does not stand before the ground like a net or a screen. We do not
sink; rather, the ground rises to us in the image. The double separation of the
image, its pulling away and its cutting out, form both a protection against the
ground and an opening onto it. In reality, the ground is not distinct as ground
except in the image: without the image, there would only be indistinct adherence.
More precisely: in the image, the ground is distinguished by being doubled.
Nancy 2005, 13
Nancy’s doubling of the image as a movement between surface and depth pierces
the picture plane like an X-ray, rendering it three-dimensional. Visually locating the
surface and depth of Teufelsberg resonates with the reading of the ground and
geography of the buried city. Surface interactions with the geography of the buried
city are a result of the hidden layers of rubble piled over the 25 years of its
construction. Designed for in-ground invisibility, the buried city’s transformation
comes through the appearance of nature.
The practical solution of removing the rubble from destroyed Berlin separated
destruction from reconstruction, forming a new geography. Post -1945, Berlin’s rubblechoked streetscapes turned many districts into unliveable and dangerous wastelands,
leading to an exodus of its inhabitants. This was not the ﬁrst time in history that war
and destruction had made a city dangerous to live in, but in terms of scale it greatly
Figure 37 Teufelsberg Wildersdorf Grunewald, 1966 Landesarchiv Berlin F
Rep.290–115662
Photo: Hans Seiler
New ground: against forgetting
141

## S. 157

surpasses the destruction of Carthage and Babylon. Cities have also come and gone,
moving from great metropolis to abandoned ruins such as Petra and Mycenae or by
nature as was the case of Rome. In his chapter Crime and Punishment on the Capitoline
Hill, Mark Bradley writes that the capital ‘was fraught with topographical hazards in
the heart of Rome; the landscape itself was designed to exterminate and expel those
who did not “ﬁt in”‘8 (2012, 116). On the surface, Teufelsberg appears not to be
‘fraught with topographical hazards’. Yet if its surface was pierced and the depth of
its geography revealed, the topographical hazard would reveal a history of violence
and destruction. Scraping the surface of Teufelsberg would allow for ‘a viewing
through layers’ of its ground, ‘formulating the depth’ of the buried city’s ‘fraught’
geography. To make visible the buried city is to reveal its ground, viewing Teufelsberg
as an as-yet-undiscovered archaeology.
Conclusion
Sixty years before Hanns Hörbiger’s 1913 pseudo-scientiﬁc World Ice Theory of an
oscillating crystal cosmos, the designers of New York’s Central Park, Frederick Law
Olmsted and Calvert Vaux, were thinking about New York’s ‘pleistocene ice sheets’.
In Frederick Law Olmsted and the Dialectical Landscape, from The Collected
Writings edited by Jack Flam, Robert Smithson imagines the site of Central Park in
the Palaeolithic Age.9
Imagine yourself in Central Park one million years ago. You would be standing
on a vast ice sheet, a 4,000-mile glacial wall, as much as 2,000 feet thick. Alone
on the vast glacier, you would not sense its slow crushing, scraping, ripping
movement as it advanced south, leaving great masses of rock debris in its wake.
Under the frozen depths, where the carousel now stands, you would notice the
effect on the bedrock as the glacier dragged itself along.
Smithson 1996, 117
Known as Greensward at the time, the site of Central Park had not only shifted
over the million years of the earth’s geological evolution, it was now shifting above
ground thanks to soil imported from neighbouring New Jersey (approx. 10 million
cartloads, totalling 14,100 m3) to construct the park’s landscape topography. Smithson
mentions that Olmsted and Vaux were inﬂuenced in their design for Central Park
by notions of the sublime and the picturesque in William Gilpin’s Observations
Relative to Picturesque Beauty (1789), Edmund Burke’s Inquiry into the Origin of
our Ideas of the Sublime and the Beautiful (1757) and Uvedale Price’s An Essay on
the Picturesque (1794). Smithson describes Olmsted as one of the ‘forerunners of a
dialectical materialism applied to the physical landscape’10 (1996, 119). Olmsted and
Vaux conceived Central Park not only as a picturesque idyllic landscape but also as
a dialectic between garden and wilderness. According to Smithson, ‘the original
deﬁnition of wilderness’ is ‘a place that exists without human involvement’ (1996,
124). Central Park’s construction is totally made from human involvement and in
this it resembles Teufelsberg. Each landscape and geography comes via a shifting of
ground; both share a formal plan that also allows for designed wilderness, for nature
to take over.
142
New ground: against forgetting

## S. 158

The importation of ground to Central Park and Teufelsberg not only constructed
their terrains but also created the foundation for their plantations; Central Park’s
soil was hauled from New Jersey; the topsoil that covered Teufelsberg’s rubble was
hauled by train and truck from the surrounding countryside of the northern city of
Bremen. The importation of ground that covered the bedrock remnants of New York’s
Paleolithic Age gave rise to its park. Teufelsberg’s importation of ground gave rise
to its forest, making the buried city invisible. Both sites are a construction from
imported ground to realise a designed idealism of nature. Teufelsberg is the result of
removing rubble from the city of Berlin to create a walk-up public park; Central Park
creates a landscape in the middle of the city. The 10 million cartloads of soil for
Central Park and the millions of tons of rubble that ‘shifted’ the city of Berlin to
Teufelsberg account for each park’s construction of the picturesque. Noticing the
different ways in which Teufelsberg rewrites the history of Berlin’s destruction gives
rise to the opportunity to reveal the true materiality of its geography. To reveal
Teufelsberg’s underground layers would be to excavate its ground of rubble until the
original ground of the site was reached. To excavate the ground of Central Park
would terminate on reaching the compacted glacial debris. In the case of Teufelsberg,
an excavation of the site would entail extracting the remnants of the buried city,
involving the practice of archaeology. Teufelsberg’s buried city and the present city
of Berlin, I would argue, lay on two grounds bound together by archaeology; the
unearthing of one leads to the unearthing of the other.
Future archaeology – unearthing
‘the archaeological consciousness is born more
of confrontation with the future than with the past.’
Alain Schnapp, Discovery of the Past, The Origins of Archaeology
Spatially thinking through Teufelsberg serves two purposes. First, it allows for a
constant change in reading of its terrain; city as grave, grave as geography, geography
as designed landscape. Second, it allows the opportunity for Teufelsberg to be
connected with Berlin’s destruction through archaeology. Excavating Teufelsberg’s
ground uses the practice of archaeology to understand the human geography that
reshaped Berlin’s destroyed buildings into an architectural landscape. Deploying
archaeology to excavate the buried city will most certainly not lead to the recovery
of artefacts of value, but ‘confrontation with the future than with the past’ as
expressed by Schnapp. The fragments of concrete, brick and dust will more likely
lead Teufelsberg to be regarded as an important human-made geography of the 20th
century. As James Hutton and his contemporaries in the 18th and 19th centuries
analysed the earth’s layers of stratiﬁcation to calculate its origins over time,
Teufelsberg’s archaeological value proposes a similar analysis of its stratiﬁcation to
calculate its origins over time.
In Theory and Practice in Archaeology, Ian Hodder points out that ‘archaeologists
tell stories about the past, and so about the present and future, but their stories differ
from ﬁction by their basis in the material experience of “other” worlds’11 (1992,
155). Hodder’s analysis of archaeology’s role of revealing the past in the present is
part of how to ‘include experiences of the distant past’ into our present lives. ‘We
tell stories about ourselves by meditating on the archaeological record, but those
New ground: against forgetting
143

## S. 159

stories are inﬂuenced by the experience of that record. Archaeological record and
contemporary society constitute each other’ (1992, 155). In his book on Athenian
archaeology, The Nation and its Ruins, Yannis Hamilakis asserts that ‘. . . archaeology
as a discipline, as a set of principles, devices, methods, and practices, creates its object
of study, out of existing and real, past material traces’12 (2007, 14). Hodder’s
archaeology ‘involves the archaeologist acting as interpreter between past and present,
between different perspectives on the past, and between the speciﬁc and the general’
(1992, 170). Hamilakis’s archaeology is the relationship of history and nationhood;
formulating the ‘link between archaeology, antiquity, and national imagination’
(2007, 25). What both tell is that the archaeology of revealing objects of human
existence from the past is only a part of formulating and situating unearthed histories
in the present. Paul Virilio declared that his interest in the bunkers of the Atlantic
Wall was ‘solely archaeological’.
I would hunt these gray forms until they would transmit to me a part of their
mystery, a part of the secret a few phrases could sum up: why would these
extraordinary constructions, compared to the seaside villas, not be perceived or
even recognized? Why this analogy between the funeral archetype and military
architecture?
Virilio 1994, 11
Virilio’s excursions to the French coastline during the 1960s to photograph
Germany’s WWII war defences for his photographic essay Bunker Archaeology
(previously cited in Chapeter 4) presents a contemporary archaeological undertaking
of the recent past. Virilio is not solely concerned with establishing historical
archaeological data but with recording his personalised attraction to architectures of
war. The Atlantic Wall, a system of concrete bunkers constructed between 1942 and
1944, was designed to repel an impending military landing of Allied forces along the
French coast. Designed to appear as natural sand dunes and thus disappear within the
natural lie of the coastal topography, the bunkers had emerged, tilted and rolled from
their original sites of camouﬂage by the time Virilio began to photographically document
them. Virilio’s interest in analysing the form and strategic design of these architectures
for killing an invading army is to some degree blunted by the bunkers’ transformation
and spatial reorganisation, creating a landscape of sublime funerary objects.
For me the organization of space would now go hand in hand with the mani -
festations of time. . . . This actual archaeological break led me to a reconsideration
of the problem of architectural archetypes: the crypt, the ark, the nave. . . . The
problems of structural economy had become secondary, and now I would
investigate the Fortress Europe, which was vacant from now on, with an eye to
the essence of architectural reality.
Virilio 1994, 14
The bunkers of the Atlantic Wall and the buried city at Teufelsberg are designed
to disappear. Both blend with their surrounding terrain so as to appear to be
something else; bunkers as sand dunes; buried city as natural geography. My
excursions to Teufelsberg sought to ﬁnd existent signs or material evidence of the
buried city protruding the surface. Each walk over Teufelsberg brought me closer,
144
New ground: against forgetting

## S. 160

as Virilio had found in his multiple excursions to the French coast, to the buried city.
Yet, the absence of ﬁnding identiﬁable material form allowed for a better
understanding of what I was looking for, namely ‘a reconsideration of the problem
of architectural archetypes: the crypt, the ark, the nave’ of the buried city. The way
I began to understand the ‘architectural archetype’ Teufelsberg presented was to see
beyond its surface to confront its ‘archaeology of the brutal encounter’13.
Alain Schnapp considers archaeological practices where ‘for the archaeologist it
is not enough that the objects make sense, they must be linked to a place, to an area,
to practices which allow them to be viewed as assignable, interpretable entities’14
(1996, 24). In Discovery of the Past, The Origins of Archaeology, Schnapp names
the origins of archaeology as a discernible discipline: ‘[I]n the ﬁrst half of the
nineteenth century a new term – archaeology – was increasingly used, and this shift
in vocabulary corresponded to a modiﬁcation of the role and purpose of knowledge
of the past’ (1996, 275). Previous terms for archaeological practice, he writes, were
‘archaiologia, antiquitates, antiquities: for over two thousand years these were the
terms used to describe the study of the material past in the West, and the men who
devoted themselves to this study were called “antiquaries”’ (1996, 275).
The scholars who explicitly asserted their archaeological credentials aimed to
create a new branch of knowledge which was not subservient to philology but
embraced the entire material part of human history. In order to achieve this they
undertook the construction of a speciﬁc tool for the classiﬁcation of objects:
typology. But typology alone could not provide a full framework for the
reconstruction of the past. It was necessary to assign groups of objects and
monuments to speciﬁc periods, and then to observe the soil, distinguish the layers
and recognise the human activities of ancient times. To this end archaeologists
salvaged the idea of stratigraphy, the foundations for which had been laid by
geologists.
Schnapp 1996, 275–6
The shift from ‘archaiologia’ to the professional science of archaeology (dating,
collection and preservation) was developed by the eighteenth-century French
antiquarian Legrand d’Aussy. ‘Legrand d’Aussy opened the way for a modern practice
of the antiquarian profession. For the ﬁrst time the fragility of archaeological
monuments became the focus of attention, and for the ﬁrst time means of protection
and investigation’ (1996, 279). The unearthing of Teufelsberg does not presuppose
an archaeology of discovery or an unearthing of fragile objects associated with
archaeology and antiquity, such as those unearthed at Thebes, Athens, Rome and
thousands of other sites in Europe and throughout the world. Instead, the aim of
unearthing Teufelsberg is to establish the unlikeliness of discovering archaeological
objects. Within this loss of the archaeological object, the unearthing of the buried
city moves the practice of archaeology to the ‘grand narratives’ of the postmodern
condition of twentieth-century destruction and war that created the materiality for
the funerary architecture of the buried city; concrete, brick and dust.
Unearthing Teufelsberg raises a question: how did the people of Berlin take the
step of burying their city? The answers are multiple and complicated. As we have
discussed, Teufelsberg’s construction was a quick way of disposing of the rubble of
destroyed buildings choking Berlin’s streets, obstructing reconstruction. Certain
New ground: against forgetting
145

## S. 161

materials were salvaged; bricks, steel and other metals and stones.15 Yet much of the
rubble from Berlin’s bombed-out buildings was gathered, carted and dumped, creating
a series of geographical features within the city in the seven Trümmerberge and (using
a far greater amount) to construct Teufelsberg. On account of its scale and distance
from the centre of Berlin, Teufelsberg played a far more important role than the
Trümmerberge not only in burying and forgetting destroyed Berlin but also in burying
the recent Nazi past in order to begin anew. By burying their city, the people of
Berlin were able to psychologically look the other way by turning their back on the
past and looking to the future, as discussed in Chapter 2 with reference to Walter
Benjamin’s interpretation of Paul Klee’s painting, Angelus Novus. Teufelsberg’s
construction enabled a turning away from the past and, from the top of hill, the view
of a reconstructed Berlin in the distance. As well as achieving this turning away from
the past, the physical burying of the city is also related to the cultural self-suppression
in German literary circles, the failure to record, from a German perspective, the effects
on the civilian population as a result of the devastation of German towns and cities.
Sebald is highly critical of this failure that informed the concept of self-anaesthesia,
Figure 38 Teufelsberg – Modell für die Aufschüttung, 24.06.1959, F Rep.290–65117,
Landesarchiv Berlin
Photo: Christian Zocher
146
New ground: against forgetting

## S. 162

in his attack on the silence of German writers unwilling to engage the subject. Given
the above summary of explanations, it is nevertheless understandable how a people
came to bury their city. The post-war German silence and readiness to forget the
Nazi regime that spellbound a nation for 12 years (1933–1945) is somehow buried
at Teufelsberg. Archaeology is one way, 70 years later, of unearthing the construction
of silence and forgetting. The above explanations both challenge and make a case
for my reason to carry out an unearthing of Teufelsberg. They also prompt the
question: What can archaeology do to somehow explain the buried city?
The practices of unearthing the past in present time have led to an understanding
of the various types of human settlement. The unearthing of remnant objects from the
past has fed theories of human occupation of ground, building and cultivation of land,
cultural and material evolution and, more broadly, animals and nature. As ground
provides the elements for human survival, archaeology provides the physical collection
of human existence over time. Archaeology accumulates data by analysing ground to
create predominant geo-philosophies regarding the human endeavour. ‘Ever since
philosophy assigned itself the role of ground it has been giving the established powers
its blessing, and tracing its doctrine of faculties onto the organs of State power’16
(Deleuze and Guattari 1987, 376). Deleuze and Guattari’s mission for a spatial geophilosophical formation proposed more vexing questions than answers:
. . . philosophy follows the same movement as the other activities; whereas
romantic philosophy still appealed to a formal synthetic identity ensuring a
continuous intelligibility of matter (a priori synthesis), modern philosophy tends
to elaborate a material of thought in order to capture forces that are not thinkable
in themselves.
Deleuze and Guattari 1987, 376
Given that the philosophy of ground and human interaction is constantly forming
and reshaping our connections with the world, it might be worth asking how ground
might be experienced and remembered other than as terrain, strata and earth?
Addressing this question to Teufelsberg, the answer is an archaeological one; by
unearthing the memory of the buried city.
In The Destruction of Memory, Robert Bevan suggests that remembering forms
‘a continuity of successive experiences, setting down layers of meaning’17 (2006, 16).
Bevan provides a connection between geography and memory where such layers exist,
resulting
. . . in an especially strong power of place – a psycho-geography, an awareness
of the past (rather than an architectural avatar of a petriﬁed spirit) that is
dynamic, handed down by people rather than recorded on the very stones, and
is speciﬁc to a particular historic political context. The worth of such places
increases where efforts to destroy them remind communities of this value. If the
touchstones of identity are no longer there to be touched, memories fragment
and dislocate – their hostile destruction is an amnesia forced upon the group as
a group and in its individual constituent members. Out of sight can become,
literally, out of mind both for those whose patrimony has been destroyed and
for the destroyers.
Bevan 2006, 16
New ground: against forgetting
147

## S. 163

Connecting Bevan’s psycho-geography to Deleuze and Guattari’s geo-philosophy
suggests that ground contains the datum for the retaining of memory and history
(capital, spatial, cultural), which also reafﬁrms Heidegger’s aphorism that ‘nothing
is without ground’. Working through the practices of archaeology, Laurent Olivier
in The Archaeology of the Contemporary Past asks: ‘If archaeology is concerned with
fossilized remains from the past, they are nonetheless preserved in the present, and
it is effectively in our present that they are manifest to us’18 (2001, 180). Olivier’s
cause for contemporary archaeological practice is to ensure its connectedness to the
‘environment of the present time; the present, made up as it is of a diversity of past
temporalities’ (2001, 187). If archaeology is piecing together the fragmentations of
human history widely connected to human occupation of ground, Olivier’s
archaeological concept does not rest on the unearthing of objects per se but on the
intersections between ground and human:
. . . all manifestations that bear witness, physically, to human activity are, by
their nature, concerned with archaeology. The archaeology of the contemporary
period has a legitimate existence, then, but a frame in which it can ﬁnd its proper
place remains to be discovered.
Oliver 2001, 188
Olivier’s suggestion that ‘archaeology of the contemporary period has a legitimate
existence’ proposes an approach to an archaeology of the buried city. By combining
Figure 39 Trummer-Abladestatte, 01.09.1956, F Rep.290–49279
Photo: Landesarchiv Berlin
148
New ground: against forgetting

## S. 164

Teufelsberg with archaeology, what becomes ‘manifest to us’ is how the buried 
city creates a ‘legitimate existence’ of how and why the people of Berlin buried their
city.
Contemporary geologists have declared a new age of human geography, moving
from previous acknowledgments of how humans have shaped the earth to cultivate,
mine and build their cities, to an earth now under the control of humans with
devastating consequences for nature. This new ‘Human Age’ formulates the human
impact on the earth, measured by global emissions from fossil fuels and their
catastrophic effects of animal and plant life. ‘The Human Age’ marks the tipping
point in the relationship between human and nature where humans, for the ﬁrst time,
have overtaken nature as the superior entity on the planet. This shift in the balance
between human and earth marks the beginning of a post-nature human existence,
which geologists have termed the Anthropocene. No longer isolated to the geological
formation of the present earth’s condition, the concept of the Anthropocene is also
used by environmentalists, theorists, philosophers and cultural and economic
commentators. In Anthropogenesis: Origins and Endings in the Anthropocene,
Kathryn Yusoff writes:
The Anthropocene installs an origin and end – anthropogenesis – into the history
of rocks (geology), which is characterized by continuous temporal conservation
and propagation of life forms, with no beginning, nor end. This anthropogenesis
erases the unceasing stratiﬁcation of human life by geology, the geomorphicity
of geologic forces.19
Yusoff 2015, 22
The forces of silence and forgetting that helped to create an environment that could
allow a people to bury their city is in essence part of the Anthropocenic nature of
Teufelsberg’s post-city ground. Scraping back this Anthropocenic ground reveals the
strata of the post-city that incrementally rose over the 25 years of its construction.
As human history gives way to geologic horizons, the matter of human subjectivity
must change and reach beyond life and the organism to think its way through
the stratiﬁed layers of the earth’s formation. It must abandon its anthropogenesis
– in order, ironically, that it can say something about how to live beyond the
material erasure of the Holocene.
Yusoff 2015, 20
Covering the last 11,700 years to the present, the Holocene provides a quantiﬁable
period in evaluating the relationships and evolution between humans and earth. The
Holocene examines the geological shifts of the earth’s strata that trace the effects
over time of human occupation of ground. As a recent part of the Holocene relation -
ship between human and ground, the buried city has to date drawn little interest for
understanding the Anthropocenic forces that created its existence. The buried city’s
successful camouﬂage, allowing it to appear to be no more than a hill covered by a
forest, denies its part in the continuity of human geography in relationship to the
Holocene and Anthropocene. To associate Teufelsberg with both is to recognise its
artiﬁcial nature as part of the new strata of the earth; the buried city as the result of
a human denial of its existence.
New ground: against forgetting
149

## S. 165

The archaeological value in unearthing the buried city lies in its disguise as real
nature, a realness that has been so successful as to create its own disinterest. This
indifference comes not only from the public and their authorities, but also from the
disciplines of geology, archaeology, human geography, landscape, architecture and,
more broadly, history. As archaeology yet to be extracted, history yet to be written
and geography yet to be revealed, the buried city is sited at the intersection between
the Anthropocene and nature. This as-yet unrecorded intersection, relating the buried
city to past histories of destruction and the human capacity to cover and forget, makes
a compelling argument for the archaeology of Teufelsberg. To determine an archae -
ology of the Reveal, its presence must be marked; digging into the Anthropocene of
the buried city as a way of understanding its connections to the human endeavour
of destruction and forgetting.
Teufelsberg was born out of war and destruction. It was constructed as the burial
tomb for the destroyed city with a forest planted above, carved with pathways for
public access. According to Harrison: ‘Nature knows how to die, but human beings
know mostly how to kill as a way of failing to become their ecology’ (1992, 249). As
the grave of the buried city, Teufelsberg has yet to concede to its ecology of destruction
and death. The buried city is a continuance of the ancient and modern evolution of
the technology of war and destruction. As such it joins the ancient cities of Sodom and
Gomorrah, Carthage, Babylon, Troy and Mycenae, the twenty-ﬁrst-century
obliterations of Hiroshima and Nagasaki, and, in the twenty-ﬁrst-century, Aleppo,
Homs and Gaza City. Teufelsberg, it must be said, does not directly connect with death
as these cities do; it is the results of death and destruction; it inters people’s homes.
To claim Teufelsberg as archaeology is to concede its current disconnectedness
from present-day Berlin. Once reconnected, claims placed upon its Anthropocene can
then be explored; human, material and natural. ‘A grave marks the mortality of its
creators even more distinctly than it marks the resting place of the dead’ (Harrison
2003, 20). Teufelsberg’s human-made geography marks the connection between
human burial and city burial. If the prime motive of archaeology is to claim tangible
connections with the past through material objects, Teufelsberg is a tangible marking
of human existence. As the site of the buried city, Teufelsberg is an example of ‘The
Human Age’; geology to geography from mass destruction. Returning to the lack of
interest in Teufelsberg, it may be because it lacks the visual romantic landscape
associated with ruins or perhaps it needs to live longer in history, more than its present
70 years, before the Anthropocenic geological value rises to be claimed.
Focusing on the relationship between landscape and romantic poetry, Noah
Heringman’s Romantic Rocks, Aesthetic Geology asks: ‘Why are rocks and landforms
so prominent in the literature of this period?’ Heringman delves into the various ways
that eighteenth-century romantics incorporated the geology of nature to enrapture
human desire, longing and despair (in the landscapes and seascapes of Caspar David
Friedrich, Karl Friedrich Schinkel, William Turner, Thomas Gainsborough and Joseph
Wright of Derby, to name just a few). According to Heringman, ‘the term “romantic
rocks” designates a predisciplinary construct, a widely understood representation of
rocks as the raw material of nature that helped to generate the notion of materiality
itself’20 (2004, 21). Heringman’s assessment of the picturesque geological presence
(the ‘raw material of nature’ in landscape painting) is distinctly different to
Teufelsberg’s hidden geology; its raw material is the buried city, but connected by
landscape and geography.
150
New ground: against forgetting

## S. 166

The study of the earth’s strata (as previously cited) by the eighteenth-century
Scottish geologist James Hutton developed a geological concept of the earth known
as ‘deep time’. Hutton formulated his scientiﬁc principles regarding rock formations
to calculate the earth’s age in his book Theory of the Earth21. Hutton’s model of
‘deep’ geology can be transposed to the nascent geological time of the buried city.
Composed of concrete, brick and dust, a geological reading of the buried city’s strata
connects it to the disjunctions and gaps in Berlin’s present-day built environment.22
Walking Berlin’s streets, one experiences the movements in periods and stylistic leaps
between buildings sitting side by side. Metaphorically applying Heringman’s ‘romantic
rocks and aesthetic geology’ to the ‘rocks’ of Berlin’s reconstructed and contemporary
architectures exposes more than it hides in the urban disturbances resulting from the
city’s destruction and post-war reconstruction.
Experiencing Teufelsberg, the depository of destroyed buildings and, appropriately,
the key to Berlin’s planning and architectural disruptions, is the opposite of experi -
encing Berlin. Adept at hiding disruption, Teufelsberg offers little sign of or contact
with its geological material. ‘Archaeology deals in the material remains of society, in
things, their associations, assemblages, environments and contexts’23 (Olsen 2012,
vii). As there is no disruption to suspect Teufelsberg of being anything other than a
park, its origins lie unchallenged. Yet, as the geology of the rubble hill shifts and
moves, bits and pieces of stone, brick and concrete pierce the surface, breaking the
ground that disguises its true foundations. Yet, on the whole the experience of
Figure 40 Teufelsberg-Kunstliche Aufschuttung aus Trummerschutt Trümmerschutt F Rep.
290–264853, 1952, Landesarchiv Berlin
Photo: E. Schwab
New ground: against forgetting
151

## S. 167

walking Teufelsberg is a steady interaction between the body and the incline of the
terrain, much like any other hill. ‘An already-made geography sets the stage, while
the wilful making of history dictates the action and deﬁnes the story line’ (Soja 1989,
14). The buried city was conceived to disappear, to hide the history of destruction
and create forgetting. Teufelsberg was designed to appear as a park, a place that
could offer the public a natural experience of well-being with nature. The buried city
is not yet imbued with an archaeology of desire. But as a perfect example of funerary
architecture, the desire to ‘glance from beyond the grave’ is to change and give identity
to a city that was buried and remains ‘with no gaze, faceless, and nameless’ (Blanchot
1992, 161).
At this point in the discussion, it might be worth inquiring into the merits of
exhuming the buried city’s material geology for the future archaeologist discussed
above. Chapter 3, Building Out – Lingner’s Biomorphic Vision, sought to give an
appraisal of Reinhold Lingner’s landscape concept in turning Berlin’s mountains of
rubble into spaces for therapeutic well-being. Embedded within the Urstromtal,
combining rubble sites with natural terrain, Lingner reconﬁgured Berlin’s destruction
into aesthetically pleasing landscapes. A supreme example, Teufelsberg’s park
successfully blankets any visual presence of Berlin’s WWII destruction. Aided by
modern instruments of technology, the future archaeologist may be able to seismically
scan the hill to deﬁne areas for excavation to unearth the strata of the buried city.
What the future archaeologist ﬁnds may tell of a period in human history that many
tried to forget but that remains a signiﬁcant site illustrating the human capacity for
mass destruction.
The aim of Unearthing Teufelsberg is to discover how a people came to bury their
city. It is to cut through the history of the buried city to create encounters with what
occurred not as a ‘sense of rootedness but a sense of profound difference’24 (Gross
1992, 84). This difference may return us to Freud’s use of the German term unheimlich
and concept of the uncanny later taken up by Anthony Vidler in The Architectural
Uncanny. ‘The word unheimich literally means that which is “non-home” (un-heim),
i.e., alien and foreign’ (1992, 86). The slice would entail a type of ‘homecoming’ not
of people to the city but the buried city to the people.
To undertake an excavation of Teufelsberg asks for an encounter with the buried
city – a ‘glance from beyond the grave’ through the practice of archaeology. Knowingly
creating a disruption and interference with its geography and forgetting in hiding the
buried city, archaeology connects its origins of destruction to present-day Berlin under
the public’s gaze. In cultural terms, unearthing Teufelsberg is a reaction to the literary
silence from a German perspective regarding the suffering of civilians from the aerial
bombardment of German cities during WWII, as criticised by Sebald. Unearthing
Teufelsberg does not intend to be a memorial to the former inhabitants of the buried
city or to victims of death and destruction from its bombing. Unearthing takes a
literary concept by deploying the practice of archaeology to write by unearthing the
buried city. The literary writing would take on the materiality exhumed, exposing
the porosity of the buried city’s materials; layer upon layer of unrecognisable
fragments that make up its strata; decorative plasterwork, carved stonework, bricks
and concrete. With these fragments the future archaeologist might discern them to
be remnants from churches, town halls, shops, factories, apartment buildings and
more. Unearthing the archaeology of Teufelsberg allows for the return of bodies to
the buried city. Each archaeological action breaks the ground of Teufelsberg and
152
New ground: against forgetting

## S. 168

exerts a change to experiencing this ‘end of architecture’ and the city. The archaeology
of the Reveal enables an above-ground and in-ground dialectic of experiencing and
viewing the strata of the buried city. As a geography of human-made destruction,
unearthing Teufelsberg challenges the dark reality of its history, staging a collision
between the fragility of the body and destruction. The seismic readings of the buried
city’s geology, the layers of strata denoting the time of its construction and micro
movements of its fault lines, formulate the archaeological experience of the Reveal;
human burial and city as crypt combined.
Conclusion
Nearly 50 years before Chateaubriand wrote ‘Memoirs from beyond the Tomb’
(Mémoires d’outre-tombe) he visited Pompeii in 1802, declaring ‘Pompeii is living
antiquity’. Enthralled by what he was seeing, Chateaubriand saw the opportunity to
go further than the archaeology of the city, conceiving the idea to turn Pompeii’s
physical history into a scenographic experience of Pompeian life. Chateaubriand
thought that Pompeii could perform its history rather than be the history it is. In
The Architectural Uncanny, Anthony Vidler tells that Chateaubriand’s historicising
project would not only focus on Pompeii’s archaeological ruins but would also deploy
props as instruments to illustrate his ‘living antiquity’ concept. ‘He dreamed of a new
form of nonmonumental museum, which would leave in place the tools, furniture,
statues, and manuscripts found among the ruins . . . with the roofs and walls of the
houses rebuilt as a mise-en-scène of everyday life in ancient Rome’ (1992, 46).
Chateaubriand’s desire to ‘stage’ Pompeii would inevitably replace the presence of
archaeology with a history that performs. Luckily, for both archaeology and visitor,
Chateaubriand’s history-theatre did not materialise; if it had, it would have removed
the very history of its time.
The town was evidently no common archaeological site, its ruins bleached by
the sun and exorcised of social memories: history here seems to be suspended in
the gruesome juxtaposition of these grisly remains and their apparently homely
surroundings. Chateaubriand’s folksy museum was, in fact, still inhabited.
Vidler 1992, 47
Chateaubriand’s desire for composed archaeology was in principle futuristic.
Today, there are countless 3D animation and gaming programmes involving historical sites, offering either historicism or combat.25 Chateaubriand’s scenographic
history for Pompeii that invokes a staged remembrance of the city is a very different
notion to the problematic of confronting remembrance of a catastrophic past. Ester
and Jochen Gerz’s ‘Monument against Fascism’ (Mahnmal gegen Faschismus) in the
munici pality of Hamburg-Harburg, Germany, is a memorial designed as a simul -
taneous action between invisibility and remembrance.26 Consisting of a 12-metre
standing square column of steel and lead, the memorial is designed to disappear over
eight years (1986–1993) into an awaiting underneath cavity. The monument allows
the public to sign their name with metal pens on the column’s lead cladding – each
a recorded engraving of protest and vigilance against fascism. As each year passes
the column drops incrementally into the cavity, allowing for a new surface to come
into reach. Finally, the memorial disappears, ﬁlling the cavity along with the
New ground: against forgetting
153

## S. 169

inscriptions of approximately 70,000 names and messages. Gerz’s ‘Monument against
Fascism’ is in fact an archaeological excavation; an extruded column that has emerged
from a constructed ground and returns to its origins through burial. An artefact for
remembrance, yet based on the disappearance of the material object, it is the kind
of contemporary archaeology Laurent Olivier argues for. Its designed disappearance
of the physical monument is transferred to an oral engagement from those who
inscribed their name and messages to others, keeping alive their own and the
monument’s presence.
Another constructed form designed from the point of view of its disappearance,
physically and politically, can be cited in Maya Lin’s Vietnam War Memorial. Sited
on Washington’s National Mall, a carefully curated green space ﬁlled with grand
historical monuments, Lin’s memorial distinguishes itself from the other shrines, tombs
and obelisks that dominate the mall: dissecting the ground through an in-ground
scarring of site, Lin’s memorial takes the visitor into the earth to a reﬂective mirroring
with the names of the dead and missing soldiers inscribed in mirror-ﬁnish black granite
walls27. Citing the entry criteria for the competition, Adrian Parr in Deleuze and
Memorial, Culture, Desire, Singular Memory and the Politics of Trauma discusses
the politics of representation of a socially and politically repressed war: ‘A national
design competition was announced with four criteria: to include the names of the
dead and missing, to be reﬂective and contemplative, not to be a political statement
Figure 41 Pompeii, February 2014
Photo: Author
154
New ground: against forgetting

## S. 170

about the war, and to blend with the site’ (2008, 56). Clearly requiring the memorial
‘to blend with the site’, the criteria for the Vietnam Memorial immediately placed it
at odds to the Mall’s existing monuments. In Boundaries, Maya Lin explains that
the ‘memorial is meant not as a monument to the individual, but rather as a memorial
to the men and women who died during this war, as a whole’28 (2000, 45). Lin’s
design could be interpreted is a rejection of the criteria by opposing successive
governments’ refusal to ‘render the distressed Vietnam War subject’ (Parr 2008, 59).
Lin’s design narrative for the Vietnam War Memorial is to engage the public
through a collective unearthing in opposition to a government that actively encouraged
its forgetting. Her memorial is intended to activate remembrance by a public and
nation that had turned its back on the war and its veterans. Unearthing Teufelsberg
has similar intentions to both the Gerz’s Hamburg-Harburg memorial and Lin’s
Vietnam War Memorial. Where the words of protest inscribed onto the steel column
are equally inscribed into the memory of the people who signed, and the names of
the dead and missing soldiers inscribed into the black granite are reached by
descending into ground, the excavation of Teufelsberg seeks a connection to the buried
city’s design for forgetting to the ‘distressed subject’ of Berlin’s destruction.
Unearthing Teufelsberg works against burial and forgetting through a reawakening
of its contents as much as how it came into being. Present-day Berlin exists without
the full history that formulated its destruction, division and reuniﬁcation that lies
buried at Teufelsberg. Unearthing Teufelsberg is concerned not with the value of the
archaeological object exhumed, but with the continuity of Berlin’s history. As burial
inters the dead into the humus of the earth, the buried city relocates the history of
destruction as geography, landscape and nature. Undertaking an archaeological dig
on Teufelsberg formulates a material interaction between the body and the buried
city. It is archaeology of engagement that places the body and the public back into
the buried city. Walking Teufelsberg performs a contemporary archaeology of the
surface void of acknowledging what lies underground. Wrapped in geography and
covered by a forest, an unearthed Teufelsberg would effectively connect the buried
city to Berlin. It is by way of archaeology this rubble hill comes into being; archaeology
and people, city and geography; to replace disappearance with appearance, forgetting
with remembering.

The ﬂy-through animated sequences and plasticity of the rendered spaces and surfaces differ
greatly (as one would expect) from the physical experience the partially excavated palace
provides. The 3D-generated animation deﬁes archaeology; history and time, expressing
instead an artiﬁcial object performing archaeology.
26 The ideas expressed for remembrance in this memorial, ultimately dependent on its physical
disappearance, have remained a strong inﬂuence on my project towards destruction/
reconstruction dialectic. A number of publications of the artist’s work detail this memorial,
and it is also included in a number of books on remembrance and memorials including
James E. Young’s, The Texture of Memory – Holocaust Memorials and Meaning (New
Haven: Yale University Press 1993).
27 Maya Lin was 20 and a student of architecture at Yale University at the time. She was
also of Asian ethnicity and bore a great deal of racist and sexual discrimination from many
Vietnam Veterans; ﬁrst she was Asian and second the memorial was not a monument like
all the other monuments along Washington Mall, but a buried cut into the ground. See
Adrian Parr, Deleuze and Memorial, Culture, Desire, Singular Memory and the Politics
of Trauma (Edinburgh: Edinburgh University Press 2008).
28 See Maya Lin Boundaries (New York: Simon and Schuster 2000).
New ground: against forgetting
157

## S. 174

Conclusion
Remembering: undoing forgetting,
the reveal
This book begins with me walking Teufelsberg. Carrying my 16 mm camera, my aim
was to ﬁlm my walk (the uphill climb, my steps, the ground), to capture remnants
of the buried city – protruding stones, bricks and pieces of concrete. Looking through
the camera while walking up Drachenberg, I tripped and fell. Lying on the ground
I could still hear the sound of the ﬁlm speeding through the camera. I had captured,
I guess, my fall and a close-up side-angle of dirt.
Picking myself and my camera up, I reached the top of Drachenburg. What I saw
was something quite moving – something I wasn’t prepared for. I saw a man, arms
raised skywards, turning in a continuous circle. At the same time and on the same
hill, a hang-glider, held aloft by his yellow parachute, was launching himself into the
air. The man and the hang glider connected with the air they both embraced, the
man in a devotional movement to his God, the hang-glider temporarily taking ﬂight.
Different, yet linked through air, each also beneﬁtted from the inﬂated ground the
buried city provides.
From the beginning, my interest in Teufelsberg was to unearth the buried city, to
generate debate on how a people came to bury their city. My interest in developing
an answer to this question has considerably widened, embracing various repre -
sentational theories, histories, philosophies and disciplines: urbanism, architecture,
landscape, human geography and archaeology. Symbolically, I feel my fall was a
physical interaction with all of this. What I had tripped over, pieces of rubble, didn’t
amount to much (the photograph in the preface is evidence of the banality of the
image and fall), but it was what I came to Teufelsberg to ﬁnd.
The chapters in this book establish relationships between human and city burial,
as well as linking Teufelsberg and present-day Berlin. I sought to develop my thinking
about the buried city via a number of concepts: ruins, forgetting, remembering,
ground, disappearance. Literary works by W.G. Sebald, Hans Eric Nossack, Heinrich
Böll and Alexander Kluge, among others, helped to frame the buried city within the
cultural amnesia concerning the recent past that permeates post-war German society.
The pre-war architectural projects of Albert Speer and the post-war urban and
landscape planning projects of Hans Scharoun and, in particular, Reinhold Lingner,
with his Biomorphic Vision and Urstromtal theories, played an important part in
developing my ideas on the buried city.
Archive photographs allowed me to look at a people reeling from the horrors 
and atrocities of war, engaged in the task of reconstructing destroyed towns and
cities. While writing, however, I have hardly glanced at them, as every image is
indelibly etched into my memory. Works on the reading of images by writers including

## S. 175

Susan Sontag, Roland Barthes, John Berger and Jacques Derrida helped to deepen
my approach to viewing and analysing the photographs. The pictures showing women
and men planting scions in holes dug into Teufelsberg’s rubble ground and the
accompanying image showing women and men with buckets of water, watering these
cuttings that would later grow into a forest covering the buried city, helped to
develop my understanding from a human geography point of view. Norbert Jung’s
Psychotope and Biotype, and Alexander and Margarete Mitscherlich and Dominick
LaCapra’s works on mourning, trauma and absence, helped to develop the human
geography for an understanding of how a people came to bury their city.
Later, this summary of the various connections between people and the buried
city gave rise to a proposal to unearth Teufelsberg. This archaeological concept
consisting of two projects: The ﬁrst, Graphiens, a series of extracted core samples as
material portraits of the Trümmerfrauen and Trümmermänner, are intended not as
a set of memorials for their remembrance but to illustrate the links between human
and city burial. The second project, an incision into the buried city, Dissection, is
aimed at creating an embodied experience for the public within the buried city’s
geological strata. Conceptually, Dissection constructs an architectural foyer to the
buried city by proposing a forum space and human interface with destruction and
materiality of the former city and former people’s homes. Monumental in scale,
Dissection is likewise not intended to create a monument out of the buried city, but
instead to reﬂect the human capacity for destruction and forgetting.
The intension of the Graphiens and Dissection projects, collectively called the
Reveal, is to portray two distinctive experiences of the buried city. The standing core
samples of extruded strata, the Graphiens, are the exteriorised extrusions of the buried
city conceived at a human scale for their viewing. Dissection is aimed at creating an
open-cut exteriority to intersect the public within the buried city’s strata. The visual
aesthetic guiding each project’s rendered imagery is partly inﬂuenced by the engineers
and surveyors etchings documenting the Monuments of Egypt during Napoleon’s
1798–1801 Egyptian campaign, as well as the sectional drawings of strata by the
English geologist James Hutton. Furthermore, Chateaubriand’s scenographic staging
of Pompeii’s archaeology as ‘living antiquity’ raised questions of how to characterise
the material contents of Teufelsberg, not as a theme park but in terms of the realities
of destruction wrought on the city. The Reveal unearths Teufelsberg to uncover its
origins to present-day Berlin. One can imagine the buried city lying embalmed and
preserved for an awaiting afterlife. The Reveal is one way of illustrating the futility
of destruction so that the buried city can re-enter the world with a message.
Graphiens – towards the new visible
The imagery that ﬁrst came to mind for the Graphiens was inﬂuenced by geological
surveys of extracted sentiments of earth, ore and rock and the Antarctic ice sheet
surveys that date the evolution of the earth’s temperatures over millennia. The strata
core samples extracted from Teufelsberg are conceived as material portraits of the
buried city, and of the Trümmerfrauen and Trümmermänner who constructed the
rubble hill. Numbering twenty-ﬁve, one for each year of Teufelsberg’s construction,
the Graphiens will be excavated from various locations on Drachenberg (Kite Hill).
Extracted at varying depths ranging from 2.5 to 5.5 metres, the samples are sited
close to the cavities from which they were drawn. Each core sample is inserted into
160
Remembering: undoing forgetting

## S. 176

clear Perspex tubes, allowing the material in the layers unearthed to be viewed. Each
extraction cavity will be plugged by a concrete disc with the depth of the drill hole
inscribed onto its surface. By bringing to the surface and exposing the buried city’s
strata, the Graphiens create an inner–outer dialectic for a ‘physical co-presence’
between the body, burial and archaeology.1
. . . the ‘dirt’ of the ‘dirt archaeologist’ has changed qualitatively and quantita -
tively. Thus archaeologists working on the contemporary past tend to direct
themselves to that which is forgotten, to attempt literally and metaphorically to
ﬁnd what has been ‘buried’ and obscured, sorting through the hyperactive creation
and dissipation of resources, information and material goods.2
Buchli and Lucas 2016, 79
Victor Buchli and Gavin Lucas’s An Archaeology of the Immaterial, offers an
explanation of how the material of the buried city has for so long been immaterial
and misshapen geography. The extraction of free-standing extruded rubble cores
(Graphiens) as portraits to the labour of the Trümmerfrauen and Trümmermänner
who, by burying their city, also constructed its forgetting. As part of remembering
the immaterial buried city, extracting the core samples also poses a legal dimension
of the material, concerning ownership of the buried city. According to Vico, the
placement of buried funerary urns demarcated the ground of the ancestors, allowing
their descendants to claim that same ground. Considered in the same manner, the
Graphiens replicate the funerary urn as an above-ground demarcation and claim.
Acknowledging the buried city as the ancestral site of present-day Berlin poses the
question of who to apply to for permission to extract core samples? Another thought
worth exploring concerns the type of memory evoked by the Graphiens in remembering
the destruction of Berlin. In Remembering, A Phenomenological Study, Edward Casey
asks how memory and place, inherently connected, have suffered from being separated.
If place is indeed so important for memory, why has it been so pervasively
overlooked? One answer has already been suggested: the primacy accorded to
time and to temporal phenomena generally. But there is a second reason as well.
This is that the signiﬁcance of place, formerly unquestioned, has been forcibly
undercut-by a ﬁxation on what I shall call ‘site’, that is, place as leveled down
to metrically determinate dimensions.3
Casey 1987, 184
Both place and memory, it can assumed, are equally repressed in the buried city.
As the buried city was designed to be forgotten, the standing Graphiens are conceived
to enact the material and memory of the buried city.
The machinery required to carry out the extraction of core samples would be the
same as that used by geologists who undertake soil and rock sample testing. Due to
Teufelsberg’s material composition of concrete, brick, stone, dust and its relatively
young age in geological terms (70 years), the materials may not have yet compacted
into a solid composite material, so that extracting the core samples from the core
drill would require careful attention. The placement of the core samples into the
Perspex tubes, fabricating their base and installation on the hill, expresses an
archaeology of the unidentiﬁable city and the identiﬁable trauma, tragedy and labour
of a people who buried their city.
Remembering: undoing forgetting 161

## S. 177

Figure 42 Teufelsberg, longitudinal section from Drachenberg to Buried Speer Building
Rendered image: Author and Tran Dang

## S. 179

164
Remembering: undoing forgetting
Dissection – inside the buried city
The imagery that inﬂuenced Dissection came from viewing various indentations in
earth undertaken by humankind; opencast mines that spiral downward for the
extraction of ore, the smoothing of topographies, tunnelling under mountains, through
to the subterranean spaces of the catacombs of Naples and Rome and the 5000-yearold underground city of Cappadocia in Turkey, to name a few. It was also inﬂu -
enced from experiences of memorial including Berlin’s Holocaust Memorial and
Washington’s Vietnam War Memorial. Most appropriately, Dissection is based on
an interpretation of Reinhold Lingner’s landscape architecture theories to facilitate
landscape as an experience of human and city burial. Finally, Dissection seeks a
depiction of the body and the buried city as much as Rembrandt’s painting The
Anatomy Lesson of Doctor Nicolaas Tulp (1632) depicts the dissection of the body
and knowledge. A pictorial reenactment of an event, a ‘staged’ anatomical lesson,
Rembrandt’s painting shows a partial dissection of a dead man’s arm.4 A key
document of the Enlightenment period, the painting is a record of the advances in
medicine through the exploration of the inner workings of the body. Likewise,
Dissection opens the geography of Teufelsberg, creating an incision into the rubble
hill to reveal its geological make-up, where the public dissect the interior of the buried
city and the capacity of human destruction.
Constructing Dissection poses difﬁcult yet solvable problems in terms of scale.
The operation would employ construction techniques similar to those used in the
Figure 43 Graphiens, extruded core samples, Drachenberg
Rendered image: Author and Tran Dang

## S. 180

Figure 44 Dissection, section showing public passageway for viewing Buried City
Rendered image: Author and Tran Dang
Figure 45 Dissection, cut-through detail of Buried City
Rendered image: Author and Tran Dang

## S. 181

building of underground car parking for commercial buildings. The insertion of steel
sheet piling, demarcating the width of the incision, would allow the rubble between
these walls to be excavated. Steel I-beams positioned at 3-metre intervals with bolted
cross-sections would then be constructed in front of steel sheet retaining walls. The
steel grid would then be faced with 50 mm Perspex sheets, as well as COR-TEN steel
sheets, since the pressure exerted on the steel grid structure by the outside mass of
rubble would prohibit a full cladding with Perspex. The Perspex sheeting would allow
visitors to view the exposed rubble at various intervals along the incision. The effect
of the Perspex and COR-TEN steel sheeting side walls lends itself to both the exposure
and the concealment of the buried city; an inner façade with apertures for looking
in rather than out. In The Architectural Uncanny, Anthony Vidler talks of ‘buried
transparency’, an idea that comes closer to the experience of Dissection.
Perhaps the correct description of this mapped form would be a ‘buried
transparency,’ a term that would not so much refer to a preconceived architecture
ensconced underground but to a kind of architecture embedded in ground zero,
operating according to all the counter rules of an underground existence.5
Vidler 1992, 131
Given that Drachenberg rises approximately 55 metres above ground level at 
its steepest side, it is accessed from the main hill of Teufelsberg from which the
entrance into Dissection would be positioned. The angle of incision aims to intersect
with Speer’s Faculty of Military Science (Wehrtechnische Fakultät) buried under
Teufelsberg. Standing at approximately 18 metres high (ﬁve ﬂoors plus attic), the
Faculty was built on already elevated ground. The walk into Dissection would
terminate at the uppermost part of this remnant of National Socialism. The exit from
this conﬁned interior of the buried city would be via steps to the top of Drachenberg,
from which point the Grapheins would come into view. Graphiens and Dissection
respond to the buried city, by offering different experiences to the mass and materiality
of destruction. Dissection could be associated with the undulating in-ground
experience of Berlin’s Holocaust Memorial inasmuch as Graphiens could be associated
(albeit in reverse) with Hamburg-Harburg’s Memorial Against Fascism, both
previously cited. Associating Graphiens and Dissection with these existing memorials
allows for a continuity with collective memory of Berlin’s destruction, reconstruction
and forgetting. Displaying the material life of Teufelsberg, Graphiens and Dissection
projects that make up the Reveal seeks to position the monumental undertaking, scale
and human labour involved in burying Berlin’s destruction. As an antithesis of the
city, yet building with the material remnants, the Reveal situates the buried city as
a supreme example of combining landscape and remnants of architecture.
The destruction of cities begins anew with their reconstruction, creating the sublime
cycle between humans and conﬂict. It would appear that 20th-century destruction
of cities is no different to the destruction, razing and erasing of ancient cities like
Carthage, Troy, Babylon, Mycenae, Sodom and Gomorrah. The aim of Buried City
has been to explore how a people decided to bury their city in the 20th century, a
century unsurpassed in the history of destruction and killing. With no end in sight,
the question remains as to what the people of similarly destroyed cities in the present
day such as Homs, Aleppo and Gaza City – along with inevitable future examples –
will decide to do with the ruins of their cities and the histories of their destruction?
166
Remembering: undoing forgetting
